A collection of the artist’s most notable drawings for
The Bulletin , including some two hundred
cartoons, portraits, and humourous pictures.
Demy folio (about 18 in. x 12 im),printed on superfine
paper, and handsomely bound in decorated cloth, with
special end-paper. With a note on the life and work of
May, and a full iconography.
Edition limited to 975 copies at 108. 6d. ; and 25 copies
in superior binding, with extra plate, at £1 1-18. 6d.
Note.—This volume includes a full series of reproduc-
tions of the best of Phil May’s Australian work. For the
personal and historical interest of the book, no less than
for the artistic value, it must remain a unique Australian
publication. The greatest care has been taken in the
facture to produce a worthy memorial of May’s talent.
Subscribers’ names, with remittance, may be sent to
The Bulletin offices. Sample copies may be seen at The
Bulletin offices, 214 George-street, Sydney, and 369
Collins-street, Melbourne.

after publication, if found desirable.

When the leaves fall, and to the burial-ground
You come to seek the grave where I lie lowly,
In some dim comer you shall find a mound
With many a gentle flower to make it holy.
Then, for that fair gold head, dear, pluck betimes
Blossoms my heart has nourished to enfold you:
These are my dreams of sweet unwritten rhymes,

Some Others of that Cult .
A DECLINE and fall of even the hope of
literature in Melbourne may be dated
from about the time of the death of
Marcus Clarke. It seemed as if the
death-frost which chilled him to day nipped
many a bud of promise into sterility. A good
deal was expected from Garnet Walch in those
days. More still from one who is now utterly
forgotten, Walter W. Swan. And Walstab, too,
was in his prime, with the smell of Asia still
strong on him. J. J. Shillinglaw was to give us
something new about Captain Cook,, and all the
facts of Flinders's life and Bass’s death. G.
Gordon McCrae had copyrighted a few pages of
his Comic History of Australia. Old Bob
Whitehead and even “ Jack ” Bunster were sus-
pected of lurking genius of a coarse-grained sort,
and The Argus and Australasian were still open
to anybody who could say anything a little better
than it had been said before.
Walter Swan, from his newspaper office in Sta-
well, wrote the second-best novel so far published
in Australia. Robbery Under Arms comes first
easily. Hake Miner's Harvest is an unmistakeable
second. It won in the competition for the 100/.
pri/.e offered by The Sydney Mail proprietary in or
about 1878. (It will be noted that this proprietary
encourages literature to the extent of 100/.
once in each quarter of a century.) Luke
Miver’s Harvest is really a novel with Australia
in all its breadth and bareness, its sordidness and
its wealth ; its crudity and unrealised possibility
in every line. A bit crude itself maybe, but
genuine and strong. And the man who wrote it
did nothing else worth recording, and writes no
more now for ever. Still, let us keep hold of that
one little bit and set a light on it that may make
a glowworm glimmer along all the future.
Boor Garnet Walch. His name was big in
those days, and earlier he had written “The
Little Tin Plate,” and many other clever things.
He had done pantomimes which did not lack
sparkle or humour. He had always a play on the
stocks, and held a nice little-billet, with plea-
sant literary environment, as secretary of the
Athenaeum. Last time I saw Garnet he was like
an end of thread which a tailor has plucked out
of his needle—(one Chamisso used that expression
first)—-and .. . there was nothing done. Even as
the once robust body, the germinal and seemingly
fertile mind had withered. No song, no book, no
play; “no nothing hardly;” for the dramatisa-
tion of Robbery Under Arms is with things which
do not count unless on the wrong side. Walstab
sterilised in similar fashion, working industriously
enough in evening journalism, which also is
Marah and ashes.
*
“Satan’s Ganymede” makes a tragic note in
the mean and sordid time. He went over to the
flesh pots, took a wife with money and a billet at
the Exhibition, with L. L. Smith for taskmaster.
Thence one day he was a-igissing. Another day
and another passed ; books and cash correct,
notwithstanding. Then on a certain morning a
man, more wan than he who took into Macbeth
the tidings of his last hour, rushed headlong down
from the tower of the Exhibition building. He
had found something unked there. It was “ ”
hanging by his neck from the topmost beam of
the cupola.
Gordon McCrae wrote a book of many verses,
not all bad ; but why did he not finish the
History ? He had several good sketches pre-
pared, the notions of which were worthy of Phil
May. One represented a bit of old Sydney
Government House life in Macquarie’s time. A
group of ladies in the foreground debated the
question of precedence with some vehemence;
and a flunkey, with a Bow-street runner’s nose
(finger alongside it) looked on. Another of that
sort speaks deferentially : “ Ladies! may I
suggest that you enter in the order of your convic-
tions.’- The other is, “A new variety of fowl.”
Poor qld Bligh, of course, coming out from under
the bed. A foul lie is this, but... devilishly well
told. Maybe it was the excellent aptness of these
and other sketches which barred the publication
of that book, for I cannot think that McCrae
failed to bring it to completeness.
j*
One who did keep up his head well in that time
was “The Vag.”—“ Julian the Apostate,” as he
lived to be called. His coming was as the coming
of Arthur in its ambiguity, but not otherwise. All
the world, maybe, has not heard the story. Hugh
George {Argus) had come to his office’late one
night and was disturbed by a row outside. He
found an angry man who wanted the editor, and
the editor at that hour was not visible to strangers.
“ Could I serve your turn ?” “ That might be if
I knew your rating.” “Manager of this paper.”
“ I’m a journalist, and it seems to me journalism’s
wanted, and darned bad, on this paper.” “I
looked him up and down,” said George; “he
was not elegantly attired, but he forestalled any
oomment by the observation —“ Never mind the
bags, boss ! —he’ll shine when he strips.” End of
it was a guinea and instructions for an article;
and that end was the beginning of much fun.
The old Yag. in later years made a lot of fun by
his truly splendid audacity. Who can forget him
entering the big room of the Yorick, the crimson-
lined daps of his martial cloak thrown back, the
right eye blazing, and the left —a bit swivel —•
rolling passing strange. He had just returned
from St. Hubert’s, and—“ Well, boys,” said he,
“old St. Hubert’s damned. Went up there to-
day as usual, expecting to be met by a Frenoh
gentleman, and was turned back from the
gate by a dirty Irish...what not!” The fact
was that Castella had sold out the week
before to an eminent Melbourne merchant, of
Irish extraction, with whom the Vag. had not
played chanies. “What did you do, Vag. ?”
‘\l cursed him by all my gods, and went across
to the pub.” The Vag. in his earlier city sketches
did the best descriptive work we have seen in
Australia. Hugh George admitted openly his
engagement was the most profitable he ever made
for the paper. But he made a fool-finish. Two
sets of initial letters got into his head, and they
ruined him. “There are the F.F.V.’s, he
said, “and by God, sir, they do not consort with
the F.C.F.’s.” The First Families of Virginia
and the First Convict Families of New South
Wales, of course. They were blared and bruited
about at the “Colinderies”in Glasgow. The old.
reps, of the old, old families of the old State
beoame wroth, and cables went over fast.
The end was another cable, all plain talk, from
Collins-street to the Vag. In wrath he
sought advice and got it, to the effect that the
matter was libellous and the despatoh by cable
publication. A writ for ten thousand pounds,
then, of which nothing oame; and a long and
happy connection closed with the ourt acknow-
ledgment that Mr. Julian Thomas’s services had
been appreciated by the paper and that he vacated
his position of his own free will. The old chap
did not strike many good strokes after that, but
went about head up, and chest expanded, till he
died—pen in hand, copy-slip half-covered, head
for the first time down (for He Who Is to be
Obeyed had passed). So they found him, stiff and
cold. He left one good book, Convicts and Canni-
bals, which is anathema to French officials and
English missionaries, and therefore doubtless
tolerably accurate and certainly well worth
reading.
Very early in the seventies (which is harking
back a bit) I met a man in Melbourne who pro-
posed a visit to the Yorick Club. I asked should
we see the essayist of The Australasian there ?
and that man laughed. “ There is a story about
that,” said he ; “the question was once mooted
of his admission, and the president settled it by
saying, “Why, I understood the Yorick Club
was formed to keep Birnie out.” But why ? It
had seemed to me always that the man who wrote
those essays must be fit company for men or
angels. (Those were salad days.) Poor old
Birnie !—-a little experience answered the question.
In the eighties I met him. Garrulous in speech
as terse with his pen, sordid in life as elegant in
his discussing of all life’s concerns. And still I
hold that those essays, or the best of them at
least, are gold from the refiner’s furnace. He
died on top first, poor Birnie, and they took him
out to the Yarra Bend to get ready for the under-
takers.
J*
That was a cruel and sordid time in Melbourne
for literature unendowed and unwilling, or
unable, to take to the mill-horse work of keep-
ing the records. There was another side. The
schoolmasters in affluent circumstances were
posing as litterateurs, and with abundant success.
Alexander Sutherland was accepted as a critic, a
poet, a historian (God help Melbourne of the
eighties!), and W. H. Fitchett was running a
school, a church, and a newspaper, and preparing
for that blood and blazes crusade with which he
stormed first The Argus and next the Tomfool-
dom of the world a decade later. There was also
Professor Hearne, with 'The Aryan Household,-
which is not an Australian book, but a world’s
classic. And there was Kreutzer Maecenas
Turner, looked up to as a kindly patron and
trustworthy guide. His guidance would, in a
literary way, have been fatal as that of
the strange - woman spoken of in the Pro-
verbs, in another sort; while as to patronage,
he could not even birl his Kreutzer till he
had reported the case to that abomination of deso-
lation in the holy place, the C.O.S. The professor
is the only man on that side who has our esteem.
As to those others, it is not to be said that any
one of them was of the heaven-born, or to the
altar consecrated. But this much may be said,
and with absolute assurance, each one had a pellet
of the pith of the true matter in him, and might
under happier circumstances have helped the
world in his time and cast a few germinal grains
on the lap of the future. Therefore, it is not to
be permitted to Philistinism to snuff them wholly
out.
F.M.
A book of Satires and Verses, by Davison
Symmons, found limited publication at Melbourne in
October. Symmons was a journalist who gained a reputa-
tion on The Argus staff, and died last July in his forty-
first year. D. Macdonald, who writes a prefatory sketch,
says that it was as one of the three original contributors
to “ The Passing Show ” in The Argus on Saturdays that
Symmons first made his name, and he it was who sug-
gested the signature “ Oriel ” still attached to this
column. The book contains some seventy 4to. pages of
verse, or prose and verse intermingled—light, bright
rhymes on subjects of the hour, not perfect in form, but
composed with evident fluency, and often handled
cleverly. The mode is often that of parody or
humourous exaggeration ; and, as the preface suggests,
it is a pity that the points arp usually of momentary
interest and the permanent value of the work is slighter
than the author’s merit. But the satire on local rhymers
in “Poetry as a Fine Art” is iikely to continue readable :
Oh, our gallant G. Cross, yes in spite of her shoulder
And her big bumble foot, was the pride of the stud ;
Chuck your leg o'er her back, ’t was a terror to hold tier —
She ‘d windgalls and spavins, but yet she had blood.
For her sire was a hoss—good old Rafferty’s Willy,
And her dam teas a pedigreed Tanglefoot mare ;
So the Poison blood ran in the veins of that filly,
The pride of Coobilly— the old G. Cross mare.
“ The backblooks ” are quite capable of reading that seri-
ously. “ The Poetry of the People,” a set of satirical
imitations of the Purtell manner, is no less taking. The
rare serious pieces are not so effective —this, for example,
of a death in shipwreck, is on the verge of anti-climax :
He scanned the waves with a seaman's eye,
But never a rescuing sail in view —
Death in the billow, death in the sky,
Death in the hovering sea-bird’s cry —
Knew !—he knew
What was the duty he had to do,
With a sinking ship and a dying crew.
But " Patriotic Poetaster ” and “ The British Sunday ”
are gaily inspired—
Oh, give us our English Sunday, with its reverent air of
gloom,
And the smell of the Sunday dinner suffusing ths stuffy
room ;
And the children, peevish and fretful, in dread of the
threatened slap
That chastens the first disturber of father's postprandial
nap.
In through the open windows the breath of the Sunday
titrims,
Tinkling of parlour organs—Moody and Sankey’s hymns,
Subtle whiffs of the gutter, oaths, and curses, and cries —
This is the British Sunday -we all of t is love and prize.
There are several similar good things, displayed often in
dexterous metre. Perhaps “ The Socialistic Quarrel,” in
which one Labour orator accuses another from The Argus
point of view, is happiest of all. Symmons will be fairly
represented by full quotation.
THE SOCIALISTIC QUARREL.
Oh, ’Ancock,'aughty ‘Ancock—to turn again and rend
A pal as alius stuck to you—yer old familiar friend.
But with the serpent’s poisonous tooth of base ingrati-
tude,
Yer stabbed me through my vittles—which I never thought
you would.
Once we was ’appy, ‘Aneock ; them timet is gone, alas !
When oft-times in some ’andy pub. we shared a social
glass,
And likewise in my ’umble ‘ome you’ve peeked a bit with
me —
Which you alius liked an onion as a relish with yer tea.
And there was onions, ‘Aneock ; but there was more
behind —
A friend that ‘ung upon yer words— a welcome warm and
kind ;
Yes, one who thought that ’Ancock’ s words must always
be sincere 1
So vanish friendships only made in onions and in beer.
Once you was ‘umble, ‘Ancock, yer style was not so loud ;
But now that you’re a M.L.A. success has made you
proud.
Still I ’ll not cuss thee, ‘Ancock, ’owever false you be,
Let ‘Eaven and the ballot-box be judge 'twixt you and me.
Let ‘Eaven’s be the ‘and, ‘Ancock, to strike the final
blow
That on the dawn of freedom’s morn shall lay the traitor
low.
When with ‘er sickle in 'er hand she garners in 'er crops,
And me and Flynn is occupied in lootin’ of the shops.
An 'eated fancy goads me on to prophesy the scene b
When tyrant Trenwith quails and shakes before the
guillotine,
And through the streets of Collingwood the ‘ungry wolf
shall prowl,
A-bitin 'yer constituents—'owl! ‘aughty ‘Ancock, ‘owl l
MEMORANDA
The precious. Whistler’s The Gentle Art of
Making Enemies is being reprinted by Heinemann at 10s.
6 d. net. Oscar Wilde’s Poems, long out of print, were
recently reprinted by a Paris pirate in 500 copies at 215,.
net. The new Amer. Mosher edition of these poems, at
$3 net, includes “ Ravenna,” “ The Sphinx,” and “ Read-
ing Gaol,” besides the 1881 book. John Lane announces
at 7s. 6<f. net Under the Hill, a book Of Aubrey Beards-
ley’s work.
Of absent authors. A. H. Adams recently had
an article in 27te Nineteenth Century (Bond.) and a tale in
The Cosmopolitan (N. Y.). His completed serial story for
boys is to be republished in a book. On the whole, his
fluency and persistency seem to be confirming an English
footing. Louise Mack’s stories of school-days, Teens and
Girls Together, after running through a girls’ magazine,
have been republished in book form in London. Louis
Becke has recently published Helen Adair'-, but the life
has- left his stories—-and the sale. His work no lppger
sustains his reputation, and his reputation scarcely sus-
tains his work. f
L. Hopkins, traveller, was interested in New
York by the magazine illustrators’ trade union. Some
ten years ago there was a reflux of smart American
designers from Paris ; and careful publishers lowered
prices. The elder illustrators found that they Were in
danger of being left high and dry by a falling market;
called the younger men to aid ; and established a club
which practically fixes a scale of payments for illustra-
tions. Harper’s Weekly and the rest now pay up to old.
a page instead of the 10 1. that “ Hop.” remembers ; and
advertised work like Christy’s and Gibson’s is rated even
more highly. So the best men live in clover, and “my
country house ” and “my yacht’’ are common expres-
sions in the studios.
Three brothers of the remarkable Lindsay
family are professional illustrators—Norman and Lionel
in Sydney, and Percy in Melbourne. A sister, Ruby,
published a drawing in Xmas Critic. The no less remark-
able Dyson family provides one author— Edward, and
two illustrators—Ambrose and William; while a sister,
Jessie, published a drawing in Xmas Punch. And the
two families have been recently united by marriage.
The first edition of Darwin’s The Origin of
Species (price 6 d. ; posted B d.) is latest of the Ration-
alist Press Association’s cheap reprints. This series now
includes eleven volumes by Hukle.v, Tyndall, M. Arnold,
H. Spencer, J. C. Morrison, G. Allen, Haeckel, E. Clodd,
S. Laing, and Darwin. No worthier or essentially
cheaper books are printed in English. Everyone should
own them and read them. The George Robertson Pro-
prietary supplies the set for ss. 6 d. ; posted anywhere for
7s.
The Manchester Guardian, from mttny points of
view the best of Eng. newspapers, finds the illuminating
word for Furphy’s Such is Life. “Itis a book for light-
houses and long voyages ’’—and large, calm Bush spaces,
one may add.
Gabriele d’Annunzio was one of the bride-
groom’s witnesses at the Rudini-Labouchere marriage at
Florence in November. The marriage was signalised by
Labouchere’g careful withdrawal from his villa while the
religious ceremony was taking place inside. This is
surely an irreclaimable infidel! p
A complete file of The Bulletin, from the first
number, is worth about 401., and is rarely offered at that
price. W. Dymock, the well-known Sydney bookseller,
has an incomplete file priced at 251., with some months
missing in 1880, 1881, and 1882, but with the rare
early issues from January to May, 1880, intact. Other
rarities in the same bookseller’s very large second-hand
stock, which should be known to all book-buyers, are
sixteen volumes of The Empire, commencing in 1850 ; an
illustrated reprint of Burton’s Arabian Nights ; a collec-
tion of “State Trials" (10 vols., 1750); and a large,
collection of first editions of Dickens, Lever, Thackeray
and others. Catalogues may be had by country customers.
Henri de Eegnier, replying to a querist, finds
only one living French novelist worth mention—Anatole
France. “He, he is of Ferney—an admirable writer, to
whom we owe much. He is classic: there are others
whom I admire sincerely. As for foreign novels, I hare
always had an unaffected liking for those of d’Annunzio ;
and everything that I have read by George Meredith has
enchanted me.”
H.: In the course of following Art (!) I’ve been
drawing illustrations for a tailor’s advt. The tailor
complains bitterly' of the glaring faults of Bulletin draw*
ings from his point of view. Vincent and A. Dyson put
their buttons on the wrong side of the coat, he says. (So
they do ; I looked ’em up.) And as for cut, fit, and
style ! Why don’t artists subscribe to The Tailor and
Cutter ? As for me, by following instructions I have
raised myself in that tailor’s mind to the position of
“ the best artist in Australia !"
RECEIVED
The Beryl Stones, a story by Mrs. Alfred 1 Sidg-
wlck (Lond., Arnold ; 2s. 6 d.). Unremarkable.
About Poets and Poetry, by John Christie (Well.,
N.Z. Times) is a 36pp. reprint of an article dealing chiefly
with J. L. Kelly’s verses. “Have you not observed,”
says the writer, “ that genius is not a thing of localities,
but is as likely to be found in a village of huts as in the
metropolis of an Empire? Wherever it is found, it is the
rarest manifestation of the divine of which human nature
is capable, and should ever be welcomed with gladness
and reverence,” etc.
The Machinations of Janet, by Sarah Tytler
(Lond., Long; 2s. 6<L) Janet was a young woman so
fond of cooking and scouring, sweeping and dusting,
that, though the possessor of 30,000?., she put her wealth
behind her and continued ardently cooking and scouring,
sweeping and dusting. Some husbands might like to buy
the book for a gift with a moral: there is no other reason
for buying.
Anglo-Americans, by Lucas Cleeve (Lond.,
Bell; 2s. 6d.) A story of women in their relation to
American and English males. The point that among
U.S.A. well-to-do classes the man is devoted to the
woman, while in England the woman is devoted to the
man, is emphasised in a.tolerable story. “The English-
man marries as he buys a horse, a picture ; the woman is
part of his domesticity, but the note of domesticity in
himself is wanting. His wife must always be there to
wait on his moods, not he on hers.”,
The Children Who Pan Away, by Evelyn
Sharp (bond., Macmillan; 2s. 6 d.) Pictures of childish
character, with an elder love-story in the background.
The detail is conscientious and depressing.
Barbara Winslow : Rebel, by Betlv Ellis (Edin.,
Blackwood; 2s. 6d.). An Eng. romance of love and
adventure in the day of the Monmouth rising. Mechani-
cally efficient ; uninspired and null.
An Irish Cousin, by E. Somerville and M. Ross-
(Lond., Longmans; 2s. 6ri.) A gloomy story, with gleams
of hilarity due to Irish scenes and characters. “ Admirable
novel,” “bright vivacity,” “delightful picture,” “Charles
Lever at his best,” are Eng. critical phrases—influenced,
one imagines, by the authors’ better work./.
The Boy, Some Horses, and a Girl, jy Dorothea
Conyers (Lond., Arnold ; 2s, 6 A.), is a jolly Irish story,
and a hook to exorcise blue devils. It is a slight thing,
to be sure, making no pretensions to superiority ; but it
holds andi gives real gaiety. “Oh, surely, Norah,” says
a married Irish peasant—“ well, the match was almost
med betune us ;. but Mary Moylan here, she was always
great wid Miss Maguire above, an’ the lady guv her a line
cow to have as a fortin whin she married, an’she had wan
before. So Mary havin’ two cows, an’ Korah Magee but /
wan, I changed me mind, for sure yer honour knows that
there is n’t the differ of a cow betune any two women in
the world.”
The Heart of Rome, by F. M. Crawford (Lond.
Macmillan ; 2s. 6tf.) Crawford’s yearly novel with the
sentimental foreground, the- adventurous middle, and
the dubious Italian background, is to be avoided by-
most people—seeing that all authors in grooves are to be
avoided. Crawford is one of many who, years ago, set
down an integer of more or less temporary value
9ay 3; and who- now, past the point of crisis, recur
yearly with a moulmful 222222 till the grave swallows
author and series. The Heart of Rome is fiction agree-
able and workmanly enough ; but in it arid the rest there
is no spur, no nourishment. It does not pay ,to waste
life in dige-iting literature’s husks : even the kernels are
often bought too dearly.
The Key of Paradise, by Sidney Pickering
(Lond., Arnold ; 2s. 0(t.), is a novel of love and adventure
in Italy, well! written. The modern adventurous romance
proceeds according to formula : The Prisoner of
Zenda may be taken as a type. The modern adventurous
romance is hardly worth reading, but Pickering manages
it jusft as well as Anthony Hope, at all events.
The Sketcher, No. 18, illustrated by F. Raynor
(Dunedin ; Wilkie and Co. ; 6 d.) Some of the drawings
ate tolerable imitations ; some are intolerable.
The Struggle for Sea Power, by M. B. Synge
(Edin., Blackwood; Is. Sld.) is the fourth volume of a
, “ Story of the World ” series for schools. The plan is to
narrate cardinal episodes dramatically ; and this volume
is perhaps the best of the series, though in touching Aus-
tralia Phillip is four times called Governor “ Phillips.’
The illustrations are not good, and far too scanty .
children’s books should be half pictures.
The Inter-Marriage of Disease, by F. A.
Nyulasy ( Maryborough Standard, Vic.)—a pamphlet of
44 pp., is disgracefully printed. Possibly it was worth
printing, to enforce a popular moral; but it contains
little that is original. .

BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC 71GID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how.he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £\ for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen—ln answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, t thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£\. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself m your
hands. I knew my case was very dilh-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Felt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and stronglv recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Woman’s Song.
A poet sang one song,
And with its pulsing fire
Awoke my woman’s passions strong,
And quickened my desire,
Till, like a moaning wire
That echoes music from a gong,
My soul replied to his, aflame
With love that will not die.
He kissed me—ah !—and spoke my name,
And then—he passed me by
(His eyes were on the sky).
A horseman rode out West
And left me all alone ;
He sang the song my heart loved best,
But oh! my heart was cold as stone,
Fearing the dangers guessed and known,
So that I could not rest.
I dreamed, I dreamed he would return,
And, at the eventide,
When glowing embers smoke and burn,
Would tell me of his ride
(I listening close beside).
A sailor sailed away
Into the rising sun
To fight through storm and starry spray
A battle never lost nor won.
I prayed to God for one life— one ;
I thought God’s angels heard me pray,
That he would come to me again
And lift his eyes a-shine.
The ships are full of gallant men,
Yet there were none like mine
(Ah, me ! His eyes a-shine !)
My husband counts his gold
Amid the noise of streets.
His back is bent, his face is old ;
Forgetting Life holds many sweets
He counts in cash Life’s passion beats,
His hand in mine lies cold.
He speaks no soft love-words to me —
Words my heart pines for, whispered low;
And yet it matters not, you see,
Because my heart broke long ago—
(The song the poet sang, you know).
Why did he ever sing that song ?
Why was my love so hot and strong ?
Ah! The jingling spurs as my horseman rode
Into the West with his sad heart’’s load.
And my sailor’s eyes as they stooped, a-shine,
To mirror the light o’ love in mine-.
Why did they come ? And, God, tell me why
I still live on as a living lie ?
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
The Rata.
O Rata, proudly borne on high
Thy purple treasure swings ;
Thou hast usurped the purple dye
Of emperors and kings;
They thro’ the years have borne the brunt
Of tempest and of flood ;
The purple on thy vaunting front
Is drawn from royal blood.
O Rata, not thy might alone
Hath raised thee up from earth ;
The king thou mad’st a stepping-stone
Lifts thee above thy worth ;
Thro’ others’ greatness thou art great,
With others’ fairness fair;
Had kings laid by their royal state,
Thou, Rata, wert not there !
What centuries of growth have built
Thou seizest as thine own ;
It is the blood that thou has spilt
That drapes thine airy throne ;
Death from thine arms’ embracing springs,
The monarchs are no more ;
True type of tyranny of kings,
No heart is at thy core !
M.L.
AHAU.
This Night.
Gas lamps, like stained stars fallen,
And hard, clear arc lamps shine ;
The half-moon fronts cloud squadrons
That flee in broken line
From this keen wind that wraps us,
As in a gabardine.
Awash on yellow margin
The river whispers low,
And silent, splendid pine trees
Guard us in changeless row.
We feel Night’s cool, soft fingers
Round us her peace-robe throw.
Yon city near us sleeping,
How far from us it lies !
Forgotten is Day’s bondage,
No more we’re worldly-wise.
We’re infidels to Mammon
Who sit ’neath Night’s pure skies.
We speak not, but dream only
(0 Dreams, so vain, so fair !).
Proud boats of hope we sail ia
Through azure seas of air,
With winds of joy to waft us
Afar from life and care.
O Time, whose march incessant
Turns sunbright locks to rime ;
Who dims so soon the glamor
That makes Youth’s joy sublime 5
Who drowns our faiths and follies—
Spare this night, O Time !
VIC.
LEONTINE SCOTT.

Mention Tub Bulletin.


PAINTERS’ COLIC
EVIDEINTCE
VALUABLE TO THE TRADE.
_ Th© Case of
Mr. F. DAVISON.
(by our reporter.)
The annual exhibitions of pictures painted
by local artists receive more appreciation
from the public every year. The gene-
rous support accorded to such ex*
hibits serves as a graceful acknow-
ledgement of the advancement made by
students. The persistent and painstak-
ing efforts of our painters have an educa-
tional effect upon their numerous admirers,
who are agreeably made familiar with de-
pictions of typical Australian life and
scenery. Another division of painters to
whom appreciative recognition Is due are
those who embellish and decorate our
private residences and business establish-
ments. Mr. Francis Davison, of No. 27
Wyndham-street, Alexandria, belongs to
this class, in connection with which he had
something to say of an interesting charac-
ter :
“I think,” said Mr. Davison, “ that the
unhealthy materials used in my trade had
something to do with giving me years of
suffering. Painters are especially liable to
colic, w hich inflicts the most indescribable
pains in the bowels. My cause of trouble
was principally in the side ; at least, it was
there I was attacked first. The agony was
so great that 1 used to break out in a state
of perspiration through sheer excess of pain.
Often when walking down the street I was
suddenly seized with such dreadful parox-
ysms, which completely doubled me up,
making it impossible for me to walk for
se\ eral minutes.”
“ How long wer£ you thus afflicted ? ”
“ For fully five years I did not know
what it was to be free from pain, which
afterwards appeared in the stomach with
increased violence. Indeed, the piercing
shafts of torture which assailed that region
made me moan with pain, from which I
hardly ever experienced a respite. I
rapidly became so exhausted and worn out
that my friends were anxious about my
condition, and my family were in great dis-
tress whenever they heard me groaning. A
little relief was obtained through the ap-
plication . f hot fomentations and various
kinds of poultices ; but the ease only lasted
about a minute, and then I could feel the
pains gradual'y coming back, until its full
force burst upon me again with increased
severity, and prostrated me with its
violence. I was often incapable of going
to work, and even when I did go I was
afraid to undertake the perilous positions
w hich painters have sometimes to occupy.
I avoided as much as possible ascending
ladders, in case of a sudden seizure of my
trouble rendering me powerless- to hang
on.”
“ You appear to have had a bad time?”
“ Yes, I had. There was a feeling of un-
certainty and anxiety in my mind every
morning as to what the day would bring
forth. Quite unaccountably, I could not
discard from my thoughts the notion that a
dreadful accident, or some impending evil,
was in store for me.”
“ Perhaps you are naturally nervous?”
“No ; quite the reverse. Before my
sickness came on I did not know whet fear
or nervousness were. I could go out in the
dead of night and confront any danger with-
out the least hesitation. But now my
nerves were completely shattered. I was
afraid of my own shadow. Naturally
enough I sought medical advice, a doctor
visiting and prescribing for me regularly.
This occurred when I was living in Queens-
land, and I don’t mind telling you that by
this time I was reduced to a skeleton
through my inability to eat, which I very
seldom did. I had no appetite whatever ;
but, knowing that if I did not eat a little
to keep body and soul together I would
soon die of starvation, I used to try and
brace myself up when meal times came
round, and take my seat at the table.
Directly the food was placed thereon I was
overcome with a tsrribi® sickness at tho
stomach. Hurriedly vacating my chair, I
used to go outside, where continual retch-
ing brought on insufferable headaches and
giddiness. By way of variety my stomach
occasionally wanted food. I felt as though
I could really enjoy a little, but a few
minutes after swallowing anything I had
severe pains in the chest, and swelling, be-
sides acute sensations under my shoulders.
It was a very rare thing to have a nights
sleep, although I went to bed every evening
feeling quite exhausted and done up. I
was accustomed to rise in the morning with
a nasty taste in the mouth, after turning
restlessly about nearly all night, with my
limbs aching with intense weariness, and
the pains in my side still gnawing away,
As though to add their quota to the evils I
have already mentioned, my throat was
parched and dry, and constipation was
another trouble. A feeling of drowsiness
never left me, and, combined with my
general weakness, I appeared like a man
half dead. The prospect of getting through
a day’s work in this condition was nob a
very bright one, and many a time I had to
come home unable to continue my work. I
really thought I was done for.”
“ What gave you that impression ? ”
“ Because my doctor could not cure me.
I then came to New South Wales, and en-
gaged another doctor, under whose care I
remained with the same unsatisfactory re-
sults till my hopes were exhausted. The
medicines prescribed by both doctors were
utterly useless in every respect.”
“ Did you consult a third doctor ? ”
“No ; I managed without. One of my
mates, who had suffered as I was suffering,
told me he was cured by Clements Tonic,
even when his medical adviser had failed.
That hint was good enough for me. I
bought some Clements Tonic, and when I
had taken three bottles I felt quite a
different man. My pains in the side were
much easier, and all the other ailments had
been quite cured. Appetite, sleep, and
strength returned in a short time, and de-
spondency gave way to cheerfulness. Three
more bottles of Clements Tonic cured me in
every detail, and I have not been troubled
with my old sickness since, and I give you
my authority to publish these remarks
simply out of gratitude, for I trust a relation
of my sufferings and cure may help other
poor sufferers on the way to recovery/’
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I,.Francis Davison, of 27 Wyndham-street, Alex-
andria, in the State of New South'Vales, Common-
wealth of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely de-
clare that I have carefully read the annexed
document, consisting of three folios, and con-
secutively numbered from one to three, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any way my state-
ments—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
Ing any payment; and I make this solemn declara-
tion conscientiously believing the same to be true,
and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act,
1900.
Subscribed and declared at Alexandria, this fourth
day of April, one thousand nine hundred and two,
before me,
W. B. CARTER, J.P.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 7, 1904.
A Whole Hog Party.
Labor dictation means very little if theie is no loan
money available, and apart from small local contributions,
that is the position to-day.— Sydney Telegraph (28/12/’O3).
In the case of the advent to power in the Common-
wealth or States of the Labor party, the consequences to
the communities would not be entirely without advan-
tage. This phase of the question is being discussed by
Labor leaders, as an article elsewhere in this issue
demonstrates. It would be the signal for the closing of
the London money markets and of the local markets also ;
for capitalists in Australia as well as in London would
refuse to lend money to those they distrust. That would
certainly give some compensations, for extravagance
would have to be confined to the expenditure of the con-
solidated revenue. Necessary public works that would
have to he constructed would remain in abeyance in the
unlikely event of a Labor party in office.— S.M. Herald
(28112/' 03).
The Labor party appears to think that the time may
come when they will succeed to office—a perfectly legiti-
mate aspiration. If they do, they can only govern with
the consent of the majority of the electors, and my
deliberate conviction is that the electors of New South
Wales will never commit their destinies to a so-called
Labor party, which I designate the Socialistic party.
They talk of putting an end to future borrowing. If, by
some stroke of good or ill fortune, the Labor party came
into power with such a policy of putting an end to public
borrowing, they would speedily put an end to them-
selves. At the same time, I want to guard myself by
saying that I am not at all in favor of unduly borrowing.
—Opposition member and ex-Minister Hogue, in Sydney
Telegraph (29/12/’O3).
If the Labor party choose to drop the solidarity vote,
and come down with a simple democratic programme
embracing the stoppage of loans and living within an
income, they would gain an accession of strength, which
in the Parliament after the next, if not in that, would
probably enable them to take office.— Ferguson, ALL.A.
of N.S. IF., in Sydney Telegraph (29/12/’O3).
Then came Mr. Dacey. He told them something
interesting. The Labor party was against reduction of
members, but, as it was the will of the country, they
would bow to it. That will be worth remembering.
The member for Botany said he would preach a crusade
against borrowing, and, if Joseph got into Egypt, there
should be no more borrowing.— Sydney Telegraph
(29/12/'O3).
“ Well,” answered the same spokesman, “ we will stop
all borrowing of money unless for works which will be
absolutely reproductive.”
“That is what Mr. Carruthers says he will do,” was
the answer of the journalist.
“No; Mr. Carruthers has stated that he is in
favor of continuing to borrow money, and that the
c uintry cannot get along without borrowed capital; hut
our party say no. We assert that the time has come
when the State ought to pay for the maintenance of all
public works out of ordinary revenue, and only spend
borrowed money for the paying back of loans which may
have matured and for the carrying out of reproductive
works .’’—Sydney Telegraph’s interview with a Labor
member, name not given (29/12/’O3).
The above are a few straws (two days’ supply of
straws) which sjiow how the wind is blowing.
* •, . * * *
Australia has never been great on party names.
Most of its parties have called themselves
“Liberal” or “National ” or “Reform,” or some-
thing of the sort; one of them called itself the
“Great Liberal” party. The Labor party has
about the brightest and most original name
in Australian history, but even that isn’t
wildly unusual. This country never did any-
thing in party nomenclature to compare with the
Hooks and the Cods of Holland, or the Hats and
Caps of Sweden ; and when the Boers subdivided
into the Hands-uppers (who surrendered early) and
the Bitter-enders (who saw the war out to the last),
they showed a capacity for sizing up the situation
in two words, which is rare in Australia. There
isn’t necessarily much in a name, hut sometimes it
goes far, and becomes a national power and a
valuable battle-cry. Just now, any political leader
of the requisite size who founded a Whole Hog
Party would have a future before him in N.S.W.
—provided he lived up to the name; and a red
banner, with a complete white hog on it, hung out
wherever the party candidate addressed the .
public, would he a power in the land—provided
the party lived up to its hog and its doctrines,
and was worthy of its banner. For N.S.W. is
weary—very, very weary—of living in a muddy
backwater of politics. It is tired of being a
lagoon. It is sick of Reformers who don’t reform
and economists who don’t economise; it is even
growing weary of Freetraders who never practise
'reetrade. Nothing big enough to rouse a thrill
has happened in N.S.W, State politics since the
foundation of the Commonwealth ; everything is
the faded hue of an old maid’s back hair. Plat-
forms nowadays are mostly platitudes, and old
ones at that —the “restoration of Responsible
Government,” “judicious economy combined with
a due regard to the necessity for developing the
great natural resources of the country,” “ a well-
considered policy of progress based on a full
appreciation of the importance of preserving the
fundamental principles of the constitution ” and
maunderings of the sort. There never was a
time in N.S.W. when a Whole Hog Party had
better prospects of success.
• • • •
Such a party needs to get the biggest idea avail-
able and seize it with both hands. It wants its
central principle to be one that can’t be ignored—
one that will make everybody either wildly enthusi-
astic or wildly angry—and must pledge itself to
that principle so completely that there is no going
back. A party which pledged itself to abolish
all borrowing, except for the renewal of exist-
ing loans, and to find, say, £1,750,000 or £2,000,000
a year out of revenue for public works, and
£250,000 a year out of revenue for the repayment
of old loans, would take possession of the
biggest idea that is available now in Australia—
and if it put its whole soul into that idea it would
win almost to a certainty. If it fully believed in
its own creed it could make a vastly greater sen-
sation in N.S.W. than Joseph Chamberlain did
in Britain when he preached Protection to a com-
munity which had been brought up to regard the
importing disease as a sort of god. Britain was
half-weary of Freetrade, but it didn’t know it till
the right man seized its ear with both hands and
told it of the fact. N.S.W. is a great deal more
tired of borrowing, but it may not realise the fact
for many years yet unless the right party takes it
by the ear and explains things in a voice too loud
to be mistaken. The people want a change from
the grey monotony of things. The great vote
which they gave the other day for a
reduction of the Assembly showed their
weariness of the old purposeless drift, and
the great vote they probably would have given, if
they had been allowed, for the complete oblitera-
tion of the Upper House, would have further
revealed the same state of mind. A Whole
Hog Party, which invited them to join in the
deadly earnest pursuit of some principle big
enough to attract everybody’s attention, would
call up the corpse of cornstalk politics from its
grave.
* • * *
Nobody expects much in this matter from John
See, whose incapacity for coming in out of the
wet amounts to a whole umbrella factory. Nor
does anybody expect much from the Carruthers
Sarty, which is to the See party what the moral
TIGGINS, with a red nose on his countenance and
a bottle in his tail-pocket, is to the cheerful rake.
The Carruthers party has all the platitudinous
respectability of a Baptist family struggling with
a cold dinner on Sunday. Both these parties
talk vaguely about “economy” and the
“development of the great natural resources
of the country ” and the “maintenance of sound
constitutional principles,” and all the other
bangs and noises that have resembled iron-
mongery falling eloquently down stairs any
time these last 20 years. But all these things
mean nothing in particular, and unless the
Labor party is prepared to take a new depar-
ture, to cut loose from the old, dull, mean-
ingless buzzings of the other two factions, and
to dare to have a big new idea, the next election
will be a mere drab pottering of political ghosts
without any striking principle or purpose among
them. Some of the more advanced of the Labor
members already incline to the idea of being
the Whole Hog Party at next election. And
already Joey Carruthers’ lieutenant, the dull
Jimmy Hogue, of the whole Hogue party, is
alarmed at the new development, and warns the
Labor members that they will commit suicide if
they leave off borrowing. This anxiety on the
part of Jimmy Hogue, of the whole Hogue party,
to prevent his opponents committing suicide is
pathetic in its generosity. Also, the pitiable
S.M. Herald lays it down that Australians are
such poor, wretched, cowardly creatures that they
can never pay for their own public works, and
can never have any public works if the supply of
loans comes to an end. It is worth while being
a Whole Hog Party in order to demolish that
doctrine of cowardice and drivel.
* * * *
The time is in sight when something must be
done —when it is necessary to choose between
being the strong man who seizes the opportunity
and gets there with dignity, or the weak man who
is dragged along in the dust by the force of cir-
cumstances and gets there without any dignity.
Soon the supply of loans threatens to stop; on
present appearances there is a choice between being
the masterful individual who swears off the drink,
or the feeble-minded man who has his supply of
liquor stopped and is taken away by two policemen
to an inebriate asylum. When the supply of loans
stops, then the Government of the day must
choose between abandoning all public works and
improvements and settling down into utter stagna-
tion, or handing all new works over to the cormo-
rant of Private Enterprise, or else finding the
money out of revenue. If some party doesn’t
seize the opportunity and the credit, a time will
come when it will have to tackle the hard work
and the responsibility without the credit. The
Democratic and Progressive party is more deeply
interested in this matter than any other. It is
more deeply pledged to State Enterprise than any
other party. And it seems to be certain that a
feeling has sprung up in England against granting
any further loans while the State Enterprise party
is on deck, therefore it, above all others, must learn
to do without Joans. It begins to dawn on Cohen
that while he wants to own our railways on
lavish land-grant principles, and to possess our
tramways and telegraphs and docks, it is sheer
foolishness to provide money so that the State
may own them—even nominally own them.
Tiie great extension of municipal enterprise
in England seems to be the chief cause
of this. During the last few years the English
municipalities have been borrowing large sums at
3 or 3J per cent, with which to construct or
acquire tramways, water and gas works, and
many other undertakings out of which the private
Cohen used to make 7 per cent. There is a dis-
inclination now on Cohen’s part to find the money
for any more Public Enterprise that involves him
in a loss of 3J per cent., so Public Enterprise
must either learn to do without Cohen— which is
quite possible; or acknowledge itself a failure—
which would be an unspeakable disgrace and
disaster.
• • * *
There is nothing to he gained at such a time as
this by vague promises of “economy,” or by
undertaking to borrow only for reproductive
works. The country is sick of the ill-used word
“economy,” and the other promise means next to
nothing—it only signifies, in many cases, having
£35,000 a year interest to pay on a reproductive
work which only produces £lOOO a year. Even
the Northern Territory railway is a reproductive
work—it reproduces enough to pay about half
the working expenses and none of the interest.
If an enterprise pays only 1 per cent, it isn’t re-
productive enough to justify borrowing ; if it pays
6 per cent., it is so reproductive that a country
which doesn’t put its own money into it and
secure the whole 6 per cent, as an aid to the
revenue is a fool. Moreover, half-measures are
bad policy as well as bad principle. In a time
of utter political stagnation, the Whole Hog
Party is the one that dazzles the public eye It
represents strength and courage, and the public
loves both. If the Labor party carries out
its half-formed idea, and puts this particular
Whole Hog on its banner, and does it in a
way that leaves no room for doubt, it will be the
best-advertised party in Australia. It will be
cursed or blessed at the fullest length in every
paper in Australia, and almost every paper in
England. It will get acres of free advertising.
It will get the loudest applause and the largest
dead cat that the State can produce. But to earn
that applause, advertisement, and cat it will have
to declare emphatically that it will either occupy
the Treasury benches or the Opposition benches,
and not be a half-way institution any longer—that
it is resolved for the future to be the head and
not the tail of any alliance, and that it will
be against every borrowing party all the time.
Then N.S.W. politics will no longer be of the
indefinite color of the old maid’s back hair, and if
the party fails it will, at least, not fail, as it
threatens to do now, by a colorless process of
fading out.
* • • •
There are other things that a Whole Hog Party
can promise with advantage. It should pledge
itself that, if returned to power with a working
majority, it will, in its very first session, intro-
duce a heavy absentee tax, a graduated land-tax,
a Bill establishing the Referendum, and a measure
for the abolition of the Upper House, and that it
will either carry these measures or lose its billet
in the struggle. And it should promise that, if it
doesn’t get into power, it will raise such Cain
as cannot readily be ignored, on behalf
of this programme, in Opposition. As re-
gards all these matters it will, of course,
be taunted with its past neglect and its
past misdeeds, but to that the simple answer is
always ready: “There are three parties in the
House. They all sinned equally. We have re-
pented, and the other two haven’t.” There will
he gibes, no doubt, about the returned prodigal
and the fatted calf, but, after all, the prodigal
who returned got the calf, and the one who stayed
away didn’t. The calf, as an asset, is not to be
despised, but it is an asset that needs to he pur-
sued earnestly. If the N.S.W. Labor men decide
to he the Whole Hog Party they should not only
send out their own speakers throughout the length
and breadth of the land, but, while the Australian
Parliament is in recess, they should borrow from
the Federal Labor party every man with any
.oratorical gifts, no matter what State he
represents, who can be pressed into the
service, and thus carry the Whole Hog
gospel into every corner of the State. They
must contest every electorate where there
is not already a strong candidate who is
willing to accept the Whole Hog idea. With a
fighting platform embodying the immediate and
total abolition of borrowing except for renewal
purposes, establishment of a public works fund
and a sinking fund out of re,venue, graduated
land-tax, absentee-tax, the Referendum and extinc-
tion of the Upper House, the party would almost
certainly win, or if it dia not win it would come
hack so much stronger that it would have every
prospect of winning at an early date. And either
way it would so completely shake up the mouldy
stagnation of N.S.W. politics that they wouldn’t
fully grope their way back to their old tomb for a
quarter of a century.
• • • •
If the N.S.W. Labor party doesn’t adopt this
idea it has always to consider the possibility that
a leader may arise (improbable as it seems) who
will seize the opportunity on behalf of some other
party. That will be bad for Labor. And if no
party moves in the matter the approaching
election promises to be a petty little scuffle of
persons of no importance, in which, from sheer
lack of any big principle, the most violent and un-
reliable daily paper will carry the day.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Defunct Fiscal Issue.
Sydney Worker lately published an interesting
yarn on the fiscal question, from which the fol-
lowing is an extract:
We do not pretend to sink the fiscal issue. We say
that it is not alive, and that it cannot be sunk because it
is dead and buried, and that when The Bulletin and the
Daily Telegraph attempt to galvanise Protection and
Freetrade into life they are political body-snatchers who
disturb the peace of the dead and mouldering.
The Bulletin doesn’t want to quarrel with any-
body, so let it be assumed that the fiscal issue is
dead, as the Worker says. And then—to get
back to business—which side of the corpse is the
Federal Labor party going to be on when it takes
office, as it must do some day, and probably on an
early day ? Is it going (with perfect indifference)
to raise revenue by duties that, incidentally, assist
local industries at the same time—which is Pro-
tection, even if it is very moderate Protection ?
Or is it going (without taking any interest
in the matter) to raise revenue principally,
or partly, by duties that won’t assist any
local industry, which is revenue-tariffism wku the
essential feature of Freetrade that it doesn’t give
any preference to the Australian worker ? Or
does it purpose (without regarding the matter as
of the least importance) to depend on direct taxa-
tion, and have no customs revenue, which is
absolute Freetrade ? And, if it doesn’t know,
when is it likely to know ? If there is any way
by which the Federal Labor party can go into
office, and not take the alleged dead body of the
fiscal issue with it, that way has not yet appeared.
The party may not care a straw about any fiscal
device, but which of the fiscal devices that it
doesn’t care a straw about is it going to adopt
when the time comes ? It must adopt some fiscal
policy, however dead the Worker may hold
them all to be ; one corpse or another is needed for
revenue-producing purposes. The party may
be equally ready to take any one of
the three, but that doesn’t in the least
alter the fact that it must take one,
and people have a right to ask which one. If it
could hold office without having a fiscal policy,
then it might reasonably say that the question
was dead ; as it can’t do that, it should be as easy
for it to state its fiscal policy before getting into
power as after. Does the party’s alleged com-
plete disregard of the allegedly dead fiscal issue
mean that it will leave undisturbed whatever
tariff it finds in operation when it takes office ?
Or will it go in for Freetrade while remarking
that it would just as soon have Protection ? Or
will it adopt high or medium or low Protection
when it doesn’t care a straw if it has Freetrade?
Or will it try Revenue Tariffism, while not caring
2d. whether it has that orsomething else? Whether
the country gets Protection or Revenue Tariffism
or Freetrade from a Government which believes
the thing is alive, or from one which believes it is
dead, makes little difference to the country—it
still naturally wants to know which it will get. The
country would address just the same kind of ques-
tion to a party which said the White Australia
question was dead. That party might be comp’etely
indifferent—it might shut out niggers while not
caring if they came in, or let in niggers while not
caring if they were shut out —but it must do one
thing or the other, and people who have definite
views about niggers would want to know which
thing it would do. If it was so indifferent that it
didn’t know, there would be a tendency on the part
of all the people who had definite opinions to keep
it out of office till it did know.
The Carious Situation of the Northern
Territory.
It is a common saying that Queensland has, in
proportion to population, the Biggest Debt on
E irth, and while S. Australia and the Northern
Territory are regarded as one State, as they
usually are, the saying is quite correct. But
though the Territory is governed from Adelaide,
and represented in the S.A. Parliament, and is
lumped in with S.A. as a State of the Common-
wealth, it is really a separate institution. It was
once a bit of British soil administered by S.A. ;
now, until the Australian Government takes it
over as a Federal property, it is a bit of soil
belonging to nobody in particular, administered
by S.A. This produces curious results. The debt
of the Territory is now something like £750 per
inhabitant (white or Chinese—the aboriginal
isn’t worth counting in this connection). The
revenue is about £50,000 a year and the
expenditure about £170,000. The difference is
added each year to the public debt. The Terri-
tory, being frankly bankrupt, can’t borrow itself,
so S.A. borrows for it. S.A. holds that it doesn’t
matter if it borrows £120,000 or so annually under
this heading to pay interest on old debts (the
whole of the interest on the Territory’s debt is
paid out of borrowed money) because, as it says,
the Territory is responsible to it for the money.
It doesn’t reckon this sort of thing as borrowing
for purely revenue purposes, because it isn’t S.A.’s
own borrowing, but the Territory’s borrowing.
Yet as the Territory, on present appearances,
can’t possibly pay up, S.A. might as well debit
the amount to its dog, and put the dog’s I O U
into the Treasury as security. Taking S.A.
and the Territory together, there is a huge
deficit every year, but no attempt, is ever
made now to remedy a large part of that deficit—
it is supposed to be fully covered when S.A., as
guardian of the Territory, writes out the Terri-
tory’s hopeless lOU for the amount, and puts it
in the S.A. Treasury. Then S.A. gives its own
lOU to Bull (who wouldn’t take the Territory’s
lOU as a pipe-light) and the thing is done. Of
course, if S.A. paid the Territory's deficit out of
its own revenue it would be quite right in taking
the Territory’s lOU for the money; it might
The Blind, Obedient Bead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A curious memorial of the Boer war is recorded from
Surrey At Burstaw, in that county, a drinking trough
and fountain have been erected by a local personage in
memory of four hundred thous ind horses killed and
wounded during the South African war, from 1899 to
1902, “in t cause of which they knew nothing.”— Daily
paper
Their bones lie glistening on the veldt, their
shoes are rusted red,
They are gone where spur and rifle are at rest.
Good dreams to all that legion of the blind,
obedient dead !
Good pasture in their islands of the blest 1
Knowing nothing of the combat, recking nothing
if they won
When the echoes of the last shot died away ;
They are dreaming of the far-off bush and creeks
and shade and sun,
And the gallops at the breaking of the day.
Did they wonder at the trumpet call that urged
them to the onset,
And the harder, tenser hand upon the rein,
Than the hand that held them steady for the
station roofs at sunset,
Or the girl across a dozen miles of plain ?
When the purple dusk grows deeper, and the
Four White Stars look down,
And an eastern wind blows oversea from home;
To their white bones, shining silver, from the
bush and from the town,
Does a sigh of dear remembrance never come ?
When the mob breaks through the timber, do the
stockmen never sigh ?
Do their hearts in idle pipe-dreams never yearn
For our horses in their long sleep where we sent
them out to die,
To an exile past retrieval and return
The girls who tingled, waiting at the sliprails,
quick to hear
The ring of hoofs at moonrise through the trees—
Will they waken for a moment from their love-
sleep, with a tear
For the silent hoofs at rest across the seas?
Their bones are glistening on the veldt, their shoes
, are rusted red,
They are gone where spur and rifle are at rest.
Good dreams to all that legion of the blind
obedient dead !
Good pasture in their islands of the blest!
M.L.

The Cornstalk Insurrection.
Last week John Haynes, M.L,A., held an
obscure little meeting in a murky little Sydney
hall, to promote the secession of N.S.W. from the
Australian Commonwealth and rend the Constitu-
tion into pipe-lights. Nobody of importance was
there and hardly anybody of no importance. One
or two very much forgotten back-numbers smote
the table considerably, and said that N.S.W. was
a blighted spot, and wasn’t properly treated, and
proposed to tear up the Commonwealth and hang
the flag of revolt out to dry. It was a mildly
bathetic proceeding all through. The alleged
grievances, when they could be seen through a
driving rain of adjectives and a flying herd of
verbs, appeared to be that the landing of Six
Hatters had been delayed a little owing to
the operation of a ’law which the bulk of
the N.S.W. representatives had cordially
approved when it was passed. Also that
an Education test was applied to immigrants
under a system which existed in N.S.W. before
the Commonwealth adopted it. Likewise that
the Federal capital wasn’t fixed by the very first
Australian Parliament —principally because some
of the N S.W. members differed violently from
the rest of Australia ; and that a N.S.W. minority
couldn’t over-rule an Australian majority in the
matter of the tariff. In addition to these things
it seems that the Commonwealth supplies N.S.W-
with too much revenue, and N.S.W. spends it
badly. Also the N.S.W. State Government
received from the Commonwealth the money
collected as fodder duties during the drought
season, and refused to refund it to the “ perishing
farmers,” which is apparently held to be a grievance
against the Commonwealth Government. About
these things the obscure meeting was violently
apostrophised in its murky little hall, and John
Haynes plugged the furniture with great violence,
and called on those present to throw off the yoke and
sell it to a marine store. It was a painful example
of wasted perspiration on a hot night. The stout
little orator threw himself wildly up into the
atmosphere that once was free but is now enslaved
by A. Deakin, and spoke wild, indefinite language
about “degradation and insult,” and said vaguely
that N.S.W. had had a brick thrown at its
tenderest feelings, and had been subjected to the
egg of scorn arid the vegetable of contumely. He
also staked his life on the success of the movement
for breaking up the union, so his tombstone may
be considered as good as ordered, and the clods
can already be heard falling on his coffin. The
show had less vitality in it than a gathering of
stamp collectors or a meeting to discuss an obscure
comma in Shakspeare. But it appointed an
excited treasurer to take care of the tuppence, and
a violent secretary to address the postcard, and
about half the audience refused to vote, and some-
one told the boss of the movement not to be
ridiculous, and then the insurrection went away
to have beer.

The Bills for Resumed Properties.
The Queensland Government, it was mentioned
last week, had sent in its claim for properties
taken over by the Australian Government, the
amount demanded being something near
£2,400,000. The N.S.W. claim for £4,000,000 or
so is also said to be ready, and any claims that are
still outstanding will probably arrive very soon.
Treasurer Turner, as an economical man, will
probably feel an inclination to argue about these
claims, which are said to be rather lavish, but it
is to be hoped that, for once, he will suppress his
economical instincts and just pay up. Payment
is to be made in every case by taking over State
debt to the amount of the claims. It is essential
that the State debts should be taken over
by the Commonwealth—as much of them
as possible, and as soon as possible. If
£20,000,000 of State debt is assumed by
the Commonwealth in payment for properties
which are really only worth £10,000,000
or £5,000,000, it is just the same to Australia as if
£20,000,000 of State debt was taken oyer by the
Commonwealth in return for no properties at all,
but merely as part of a general scheme of debt
consolidation. Whether the debt is called a State
debt or Federal debt, the same Australian people
owe the money, and have to pay the interest.
Probably all the States will seek to shamefully
overcharge the Commonwealth for the transferred
properties, but that only means that the Aus-
tralian people, in their State capacity, will seek to
overcharge themselves in their Federal capacity.
There will be the same old debt, only more of
it will be Federal debt and less will be
State debt, instead of things being the other way
about. But the advantage of liberality on the
part of the Australian Government lies in the
fact that it will probably make an effort to pay
off the debt, while the States almost certainly
won’t; therefore the more debt that is transferred,
whether in payment for transferred properties or
on general principles, the better. Moreover, the
occasion is one whereby the Australian Govern-
ment can earn a good deal of cheap popularity.
In making up their bills for transferred properties
the States seem as anxious to overcharge the
Commonwealth as if they were overcharging, not
themselves, but a complete stranger. If they
can extract any satisfaction out of overcharging
themselves it would be well to let them have the
satisfaction. To create friction by cutting down
the bills, when that process would be merely the
Australian people in their Federal capacity
refusing to be overcharged by themselves in their
State capacity, would be a most superfluous
proceeding. The people would lose nothing by
having their bills for resumed properties cut
down, but probably they would fail to recognise
the fact. When there is an opportunity to be
frankly generous to the people at their own
expense, and without in any way increasing the
country’s debt or its interest charge, it is good
policy to be frankly generous.
A Mild Little Sinking Fund.
Mr. Holman, the N.S.W. Labor member with
the great wash of eloquence, was interviewed last
week by Sydney Telegraph, and poured himself
forth at some length. He probably was less dis-
astrous than he might have been, but he certainly
was disastrous enough. For instance, this was
Holman on the largest and most urgent question
of the day:—
As to the policy of no borrowing, that is pleasant to
talk about, but at present hardly to be considered. Our
revenue does not meet our present large expenditure
without burdening it with public works. The points
upon which we shall insist will be to establish a large
sinking fund, and to go in for no borrowing without an
adequate sinking fund. The policy of the Labor party
will be, I think, to borrow for works so reproductive that
they will pay interest, and provide for a sinking fund, or
to insist, if they cUn’t do so, that extra revenue shall be
levied by means or in additional tax to provide a sinking
fund. This suggestion has not been before the party yet,
but I believe it will be adopted when the sub-committee’s
proposals come up for discussion.
Holman, plainly, isn’t the man N.S.W. is looking
for. It doesn’t want any man who sits down and
moans that, because the State, despite its recent
enormous increase of revenue, fails to meet the
ordinary expenses of Government, therefore the
idea of making the revenue cover public works
expenditure also is only a dream. Anybody can
moan. What is required is the man who will
make the revenue cover the ordinary expenditure
and the public works expenditure also, and pro-
vide a sinking fund into the bargain. Reckoning
the State’s extra revenue and the expenditure of
which the Australian Government has relieved it,
N.S.W. has, even after paying old age pensions,
£1,700,000 a year more cash to spend than it had
two or three years ago. The fact that all
that money is spent, with nothing special
to show for it, isn’t a sufficient explana-
tion, though Mr. Holman seems to think it
is. The people didn’t elect legislators merely
to remark helplessly that the blessed money
has disappeared. A further sample of helpless-
ness is the suggestion that the State should just
go on borrowing, and be content to establish a
sinking fund on future loans —where the works
are unproductive or only slightly productive
the sinking fund to be provided out of
taxation. If a State went on borrowing
£2,000,000 a year, and paid £500,000 a year
into a sinking fund (to accumulate at 3J per
cent.), the result, in another 50 years, would be
that the N.S.W. public debt would be about
£35,000,000 more than it is now. It would be 38
years before the debt began to diminish. That is
saying nothing of the fact that the payment to the
sinking fund would probably be a great deal less
than £500,000 a year, and that while borrowing
continues, and the interest bill grows larger and
more distressing, there is always the danger—
almost the certainty—of the sinking fund being
seized and used as revenue. While the borrowing
habit lasts the sinking fund itself is always
borrowed sooner or later. Just now N.S.W.
borrows, in an ordinary year like 1901, about
£1,300,000 for reproductive works—works which
pay at least part of the interest bill attached to
them, however small that part may be —and about
£BOO,OOO for wholly unproductive works ; i.e.,
for purely revenue purposes. If it continues
this process but pays £500,000 a year to a sinking
fund, it will still be borrowing all the money for
its reproductive works—no matter how little
reproductive they may be, about £300,000 a year
for revenue purposes, and also borrowing all
the money that is put into the sinking fund.
At that rate it would be nearly 16 years before
the annual payment into the sinking fund
and the interest derived (at 3J per cent.) from, the
accumulated fund (if it hadn’t been stolen) would
equal the amount dishonestly borrowed each year
for revenue purposes alone. The business would
hardly amount to more than a resolve to
begin reforming in a small way 16 years
hence. If Mr. Holman really speaks for
the Labor party in this matter, then the
circumstance is to be regretted. No small,
pottering, half-hearted reform like that will meet
the case. Of course, neither the See party nor
the Carruthers party has suggested even this
much. But then the See party is a corpse, and
the Carruthers party is a cadaver. The Bulle-
tin doesn’t address them, for it is waste of time
to preach to the dead.
Braddon, Pro-Trader and Freetectionist.
The new Australian Parliament will enjoy one
joke which also brightened the path of its pre-
decessor. It will be gladdened by the presence
of Edward Braddon. Braddon is a violent
Freetrader, and his language about people who
practise Protection is pointed and emphatic. He
is sorry for them —very sorry. He looks down on
them as they struggle round on their low intellec-
tual level, as if he were a metal rooster on a
church steeple. Braddon was for 5J years a
violently Freetrade Premier in Tasmania; and
before him Henry Dobson was a vigorous Free-
trade Premier for nearly two years; and before
Dobson, Fysh was an energetic Freetrade Pre-
mier for oier five years. These three strong
Freetraders maintained a Protective tariff of over
1000 items, and spoke severely about Protection,
Braddon being especially loud on the sub-
ject. Tasmania only required to raise about
£3 10s. per inhabitant annually in State and local
taxes, and Britain, which Braddon says is the
model to be followed and the living proof of the
prosperity which arises from Freetrade, raises
£4 ss. per head without damaging the Freetrade
principle. But Braddon apparently either never
had any faith in his own alleged faith, or else
some foreign enemy hired him to damage,
Tasmania by imposing on it the curse of
Protection—in other words, according to all
the visible evidence, he was either a slimy
humbug, or a traitor, or a person in the last
stage of mental confusion. Braddon still
denounces everybody who doesn’t believe in
Freetrade, but he says that “ as a matter of High
Policy ” the agricultural produce of his own State
must be protected against that of Maoriland.
Therefore it would seem that his own Freetrade
doctrine is Low Policy, or Muddy Policy, or
something like that. Protection, according to
Braddon, is a false doctrine and leads to poverty,
misery and woe; and he demands this misery for
his own constituents with great violence. He is
quite sure that Freetrade is a blessing of the
grandest description, and anybody who tries to
bless Brad don’s agricultural constituents with it
willfind Braddon standingin the road with an axe.
Braddon will die rather than see his own con-
stituents blessed. Braddon is against bonuses as
a nefarious and insidious form of Protection, but
the vigor of his demand for a bonus for the iron
industry in his own electorate is a striking instance
of vitality in so old a man. All these wild, weird
jokes don’t in the least abate Braddon’s scorn for
the errors of Protection, and the old gentleman
nearly bursts with energy as he denounces the
evils, of the thing that he demands for his own
constituents. Braddon is a bright spot in the
Legislature, and his intentions seem to be quite
honorable apparently he doesn't know any
better.
Dill Macky as a Supporter of Rome.
Two recent election incidents seem to suggest that
the Dill Macky crusade is, after all, more fiscal
than anti-Popery, more of a ’cute dodge on the
part of George Reid and Joseph Carruthers to
prop up a falling cause than the folly of fanatics
honestly believing that the great duty of electors
is to beware of Rome. Against Spence, Labor
and Protectionist candidate for the Darling
at the Federal elections, the Dill-Macky
organisation supported Acton, who happens
to be a Roman Catholic; and for the Armidale
by-election (State Parliament) the organisation
supported Kearney, the Carruthers candidate,
who is also a Roman Catholic. Of course, the
question of a man’s religion has ordinarily nothing
to do with politics—though when The Bulletin
goes into politics it intends to be the Selected
Eire-worshipper and the hope and trust of the
Amalgamated Buddhists’ Defence Association.
But since the Dill Macky crowd says that
there should be no issue in politics but that
of Protestantism, and that the pressing pro-
blem of the hour is to confound the knavish
tricks and horrid plots of the Papists, it is
mightily curious that it should run under its
banner almost any Roman Catholic who is in
favor of suppressing Australian industries for the
benefit of the importer. The fact makes it more
plain than ever that .the P.D.A. is not really
built on the beautiful calves of Dill Macky and
on a sincere and genuine hatred of the Pope, but
that it is a low-down trick of the calico crowd to
stampede the rabbit-brained fanatics of the com-
munity to vote the Foreign-trade ticket, under the
delusion that in doing so they are trampling on
the Scarlet Woman. Dill Macky’s calves are
exhibited in the front window of the P.D.A.
shop; in the back office are George Reid and
William M’Millan.
