More Six Hatterism.
One “ Alpha ” recently sent the usual incredible
Tory yarn to Brisbane Courier :
I would like, through your columns, to call attention to
one of the results of the class legislation of laborites. By
last home mail a letter from Scotland tells of four
brothers and two other relatives, with capital amounting
to nearly £30,000, who made up their minds to come to
Queensland, bringing with them their wives and families.
They intended to buy land and engage in mixed farming
and dairying. They were to bring with them three female
servants, all of whom had been for many yeais in their
service, and in whom they could place perfect confidence.
However, on going to the local emigration office to arrange
for their passages to Brisbane, they for the first time
heard of the restrictions on engaged servants, and, on
making further inquiry at the London office, learned of
the six hatters and other equally insane acts, and they
then made up their minds that Queensland was no place
for them. They are now considering Canada or Argentina
as fßeir future homes, and this may be only one of many
similar cases.
Tbe Tory party, when it has nothing better to
do, finds a lot of recreation in inventing somebody
who knows of an alleged letter from somewhere,
stating something to the effect that a family of
162 brothers, with their old and attached retinue
and suite, intended coming to Australia with 6d. or
£275,000 or something, but heard of the admission
of the six hatters, and at once swore off. But this
time the lie looks unusually improbable The six
married relatives with their families who decide
toemigrate in a drove are unusual. Thesix farming
relatives with £5OOO apiece look very unusual —
about as unusual as six affluent cookies back of the
Darling River. The old and attached servants,
to whom they are so much attached that they
won’t part with them on any account, are also
unusual; the average British female servant now-
adays is about as much addicted to staying “ many
years ” in one place as the Australian one. The
fact that these people, who won’t part with the
old and attached female servant, are always
coming to Queensland, and never to any other
State, is another curious circumstance ; this is the
third time Queensland has been blighted in this
way. Perhaps Queensland has a more imaginative
Agent-General than the other States. Yet Agent-
General Tozer doesn’t seem to want popula-
tion ; when an elderly and infirm Queenslander,
stranded in London under very sensational cir-
cumstances, applied to him for a return passage he
was only answered with a brutal jibe that he had
better shipas astoker. And the wildest hmi or of
the whole thing is that, unless these ol I and
attached servants have so little faith in the
old and attached master that they won’t trust
him (or he won’t trust them) without a formal
agreement, there is nothing to prevent the whole
drove of them coming to Australia. Further, the
name and address of the intending settler who
has thrown up Australia in disgust are never
given, and as a rule the name of the person who
knows of him isn’t given either, and the third
party who knows of the anonymous person who
knows of the other anonymous person is generally
as anxious to keep his name dark as the rest. All
these things make the whole business look like an
impudent and blastiferous lie.

That the picturesque yarns of Age and Argus about
the contentment of S.W. Victorian tenant farmers
are merely lies to check land legislation is proved
by recent developments. At Wickliffe Road, in
the heart of tbe rented properties, the tenants
have formed an association for the express purpose
of drawing attention to the unsatisfactory condi-
tions under which they live—or exist, as the
speakers termed it—and to arouse public interest
in tbe matter of compulsory land resumption.
These farmers showed that they were nothing but
the slaves of the land-owning squatters. To live
decent lives, to build comfortable homes, to rear
families was simply impossible under the present
system. There is little hope of redress from the
present Government—the Government that made
no attempt to buy the cheaply-sold Moffat
estates —but when that political wreck and ruin
is blotted out, there may be a chance of getting
land on something like fair terms.

“Monowai”: The beauty of your really honest
Freetrader is that he may always be trusted to
give away his own case. Thus, e.g., Gorst in
North American Review :
The condition of disease, debility, and defective sight
and hearing in the public elementary schools in poorer
districts (i.e., of Britain) is appalling. The research of a
recent Royal Commission has disclosed that, of the
children in the public schools of Edinburgh, 70 per cent,
are suffering from disease of some kind, more than half
from defective vision, nearly half from defective hearing,
and SO per cent, from starvation . . . There are grounds
for at least suspecting a growing degeneracy of the
population of the United Kingdom, particularly in the
great towns.
Exactly. This is how things are alter 50 years of
Freetrade.
+
In 1901 the N.S.W. Parliament passed a law
authorising the State to buy up large properties
for closer settlement. Under that law, according
to a recent Parliamentary paper, 27 estates have
been offered. Of these, also according to the
paper, 12 were declined, two offers were with-
drawn ; one property was sold privately before
the Government nad made up its mind ; five offers
are described as “informal apparently they
were ruled out on that ground; one is described
as “ not dealt with finallythree are “awaiting
deposit towards expenses of investigation ; ” ana
as to the fate of the other three the Parliamentary
paper supplies no information whatever. There
is absolutely no evidence that a single property
was bought, or that the process of closer settle-
ment was really commenced. One of the proper-
ties that were declined was the A. A. Co.’s 420,000
acres at Port Stephens—price 10s. an acre—an
estate which it seems almost certain would
have been acquired it business was really
intended. On the evidence, there is nothing to
show that any serious intention of resuming pro-
perty or promoting closer settlement ever existed.
The law was passed as a concession to the Pro-
gressive party in Parliament. It was something
to whoop about. It enabled Ministers, when
taunted with having done nothing, to say, in
deep, impressive tones, “This is a base slander ;
didn’t we pass the Closer Settlement Act?” But,
to all appearance, N.S.W. would be almost, if not
quite, as well without the Act. Acts aren’t every-
thing ; lazy and incapable administration can
nullify any number of Acts.
-o
A case lately brought under the notice of the
Victorian Board of Health shows to what extent
private enterprise can and does adulterate food
and drink without fear of punishment. An officer
of the Board had obtained from a “ highly respect-
able ” firm of tea and coffee merchants a 21b. tin
of coffee for 2s. 6d. The label stated that the
contents of the tin were freshly blended and
roasted, coffee with a proportion of chicory to
make it palatable. Analysis disclosed the fact
that the mixture was 50 per cent, chicory. The
matter was submitted to the Board’s solicitors,
who advised that it would be hopeless to prose-
cute, inasmuch as the price paid for the mixture
was not that which would have to be paid for
pure, coffee. Like so many of our silly laws, this
one is founded on a clause in the British Health
Act. It is a very silly law, and knocks all the
precautions against adulteration into a cocked hat.
Under it, if you don’t know that the value of a
certain quantity of pure coffee is 2s. 6d., and a
highly respectable man sells you what he swears
is pure coffee for 2s. 5d., and it is really a mixture
of coffee, chicory, brickdust, and the devil, and
is only worth 6d., still you have no redress. And
how many people can carry in their heads all the
time an exact recollection of the lowest price at
which everything can be sold in a pure and un-
adulterated state ?

“ T.H.” : Anglican Bishop Langley, of Bendigo,
seems to be troubled with a very bad attack of
specks before the eyes. During the last month
or so he has got off on his congregation several
remarkable utterances. One had reference to the
second advent of Christ, and set all the rich men
of his congregation wondering whether they had
not better look around for some means of reducing
themselves, so that they might be able to scramble
through the eye of that needle. Since then
Langley got a terrible move on in regard to
Socialism, which he says is
a method that was being adopted by many men, by
which they desired to cause a social revolution through-
out the world, in order that some of the anomalies of life
that God had allowed to exist could be remedied.
Langley’s theory is plainly that the “anomalies
which God has allowed to exist ” should not be
lightly interfered with. Yet his church sends
missionaries to the “heathen” to remove the
religious anomalies which God has allowed, to
exist. And his believers maintain large police-
men to combat the moral anomalies, and make
laws to remedy the criminal anomalies, which
God has allowed to exist; and they make rail-
ways, and dig away God’s lumpy anomalies with
a shovel, and bridge over His hollow anomalies
with a viaduct; and they bring water in pipes
to interfere with His dry anomalies, and make
drains to upset His boggy anomalies. And when
He makes a child with one short leg and a long
one they employ a surgeon to undo His anomaly.
All civilisation consists in remedying theanomalies
which God allows to exist. Langley’s doctrine
of non-interference with anomalies would reduce
the race far below the level of the blaokfellow.
The same story from England— Henry W. Lucy
tells it in his London letter: —
While no effort was spared by employers of labor to
reinstate former employees coming back from the war in
South Africa, considerable difficulty is found by reservists
to obtain situations. The reason given is significant and
regrettable. Employers find that owing to the system
under which he has lived for some years, a discharged
soldier is of but circumscribed value when he returns to
peace avocations. Trained as a machine, without oppcr-
tunity for forming and following judgments of his own,
he sh'ufHes through his day’s work with the main incen-
tive of trying to get someone else to take as large a share
of it as can be shifted from his own shoulders.
On Gov.-Gen. Tennyson’s suggestion, Premier
Deakin has promised to consider the question of
havinganofficialaccountwritten of Australia’s part
in the war. If the Federal Government agrees to
the silly proposal, it is to be hoped that, anyway, it
will include two schedules, one a list of all ex-
cohentingenters who have got into gaol since they
came back; the other a collection of Judges’ re-
marks, so far as they can be got, when these
prisoners were in front of them. While they’re
showing what Australia did for the war, let them
thus also show what the war has done for Aus-
tralia.

“ Alba” on the Asiatic situation: —
Just now Japan is the pet fighting pupil of European
drill and gunnery instructors. They saw with pride how
the Jap chased the Ohow vegetable gardeners from their
mud fortifications a few years ago—they called it the
China war. Later they beheld him under the walls at Tien-
Tsin, where his flanks were buttressed by picked Euro-
pean troops, his sea-way assured by the allied fleet.
There is nothing hysterical or effeminate about the
Russian ; his army is the one in Europe and Asia that
never gets excited. In the days of the Shogun war,
when the Jap was wearing preserved meat tins on his
armor, Russia was in a condition to put a Gehenna
of siege-guns around Plevna and Kars, where she
annihilated regiments of the best and quickest
fighters in the world—the armies commanded by Osman
Pasha and Mukhtar of that ilk. When the Jap meets the
cold, scornful Cossack, who will ride round him all night,
and shoot at him when he is sleepy, and damage his
praying machine and his colored gamp, the London
magazines will arise and apologise for the little brown
man and his simple ways. They will call the Russian
a big, hulking fellow, who feeds on tinned blubber and
axle-grease. They were equally abusive when the white
Cossack walloped the Turk in’77—the brown friend and
ally of Britain. When the Russian people have dealt
with their autocrats—the ermined wolves at St. Peters-
burg—they will stand forth as a kindly and simple nation
full of humanity and love of their kind, for they are the
blood relations of Leo Tolstoi and Ivan Tourganev.
Just now a number of respectable Christian papers are
preparing to stand by the little brown pimp and his gawd
Buddh. But the bear is never affected by newspapers
when he is taking an active interest in chrysanthemums.

RABBITS!
ARE THEY TO BE
A PROFIT OR PEST?
It rests entirely with our settlers whether it shall cost them and the State a large amount
annually to continue the war which has been waged against “ bunny ” for years past,
whether they will take the trouble to turn him into an asset which will bring a steady
revenue to the country and provide employment for THOUSANDS of its people. The
Rosewarne Packing Company, Ltd., is now receiving rabbits at its works in Young,
Narandera and Sydney. The highest market price is given in return for freshly-killed
rabbits free of any commission or charges. For fuHher particulars> address-
THE ROSEWARNE PACKING COMPANY, Limited,

or YOUNG.
Wanted those who neglected to vote to be
merely disfranchised for a space, but Levi was
more drastic, and wanted a £25 penalty imposed
for such neglect of duty. Of course both amend-
ments were rejected, yet M'Cutcheon’s had some
logic behind it. Every voter who can’t show that
he is absolutely unable to vote should be com-
pelled to vote, under penalty of being struck off
the roll. And, in addition, if he disapproves of
all the candidates, he should be allowed to give
a negative vote —to vote against them all, and if
the negative vote is in the majority, then a new
election should be held, at which the old candi-
dates—the ones just ruled out—should not be
allowed to compete. Without the negative vote
it would be possible for a violently Orange
candidate to find himself driven to vote for one of
two Roman Catholics, and it is impossible to say
what effect that might have on his constitution.

“Monsieur Beaucaire” goes pleasantly
on his way at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, wiping the
floor with the British peerage and exposing the
baseness of the gentlemen of England. And just
at this moment England is in alliance with Japan,
and Russia is thinking of throwing the Chrysan-
themum into the middle of the Pacific, and Mon-
sieur Beaucaire’s countrymen are more or less in
alliance with Russia, and some of them have been
dining with a Russian admiral, and waving
the vodka and the bear’s grease round their heads
to celebrate the prospective licking of Britain’s
brown acquaintance. Still, “ Monsieur Beaucaire”
can't be taken seriously enough to amount to an
international complication. International com-
plications are mostly ironclads nowadays, and
cost ±soo,ooo each, and weigh Providence only
knows how many tons, and the gentleman in
beautiful clothes, who dances round with a 2oz.
sword and prods little holes in the British peerage,
is so unlike a modern complication that even if
Britain and France went to war it needn’t neces-
sarily interrupt the run of the Williamson drama.
** * *
The most striking feature of the new
Sydney Tivoli bill is a melodrama in three acts,
or, rather, a condensation in three acts of the
plot of every melodrama on record. Act I. : Prof.
Dewar, the cat-and-dog man, rings for refresh-
ments, and a dog jumps on the table. He chains
the dog up. Virtue, in the shape of a black cat,
lies, unsuspecting the dark future, on a chair.
Act 11. : The prof, is called away for a moment.
Vice wriggles out of its collar, cleans the prof.’s
plate, lifts Virtue on to the table, and wriggles
back into its collar. Innocent Virtue, of
course, gets bumped on the head. Act 111. : The
plate is refilled ; and again the prof, is called
away. The villainy is repeated. But this time
the villain is slower in getting back into his
collar. So Vice is vanquished, and Virtue,
triumphant, gets an extra saucer- of milk. Most
trained-dog performances consist of two or three
animals walking first on one pair of legs and then
on the other pair ; and though Dewar’s dogs do
that, they also do a number of more unusual
things, of which the before-mentioned drama is a
fair sample. Mrs. Sims Reeves, widow of the
English tenor, transforms herself into a human
gramophone, and reproduces as nearly as she can
her late husband’s singing of “My Jane” and
other old favorites, and does it with considerable
success. Probably in a great measure for the
original s, sake, she received a very hearty welcome
on Saturday. Othersinthe bill include the Cirnocs,
Whitburn, James Harvey, and a number of song-
and-dance ladies.
** * *
“Mama’s New Husband'’ continues his
career of frivolity at Sydney Palace, and gives
full houses as good a time as he has himself. The
Bulletin recommended this scrap of American
music-id comedy a week ago, and nothing has since
happened to lead it to withdraw its testimonial.
* *
At Sydney Criterion Charles Holloway
is still turning the Garden of Eden upside down,
and explaining, per Miss Gresham, that Woman
sins because Man tempts her; and the new view
of the situation appeals to almost as large houses
as it did before all Sydney made its new resolu-
tions and decided henceforth to know sin only as
a memory of the past. Last week Hesford, being
a small man. wasn’t seen over the heads of the
crowd that packed the theatre ; this week, his
work as the rich father who has to be killed in the
first act in order to create the essentia] rich
orphan is seen to be well worth mentioning.
Charles Holloway himself, Beatrice Holloway,
Stanford, Miss Gresham, and some others were
mentioned before. Generally, “ Why Woman
Sins ” can be spoken of as a superior melodrama,
and the mounting well worthy of the traditions of
the house of Holloway.
** * *
The average monologue entertainer is a
weary individual who occupies a rather dreary-
looking empty stage and becomes hopelessly hoarse
in one hour’s time. A few minutes later his smile
gives out and becomes merely a wrinkling of the
eyes and a stretching of the mouth, or a ghastly
artificial grin. Mel. B. Spurr, at Sydney Queen’s
Hall, does not belong to this category. He is a
small young man with an unusually intellectual
face, and his stage is got up like a modern
drawing-room in a sub-tropical climate, with
plenty of waving palms. The glow from the red-
shaded lights is inspiriting, and the young man
with the rather clever face moves about as quietly
and easily as though he were in his own den. He
talks in a natural way that impresses each item of
the audience with the idea that the words are
spoken to him personally. He glides easily from
Btory to song, from song to music, and it is all
done with an ease and grace that is refreshing.
In fact, his show is not a show at all, it’s a case of
calling round to spend a delightful evening with a
clever, pleasant fellow who does all the talking
and provides the music and the amusement, while
the guest supplies merely the laughter.
4|&
The pantomime at Sydney Royal has been
trimmed and polished and generally improved, so
that it now runs its merry course within three
hours. There are many remarkably clever little
girls in this panto.—so many as to give pause to
the man who goes to court a girl of 18 or 20 under
the impression that she is a shy and unknowing
little thing whose timidity must not be rudely
shocked. At the Royal the girls seem to have all
the self-possession and artfulness possible at the
age of six or seven.
* * * *
“The Great World of London” still
prospers at Sydney Lyceum. It has the advan-
tage over most melodramas of a strengthening of
the interest towards the end. The final act, in a
Salv’army doss-house where Englishmen who will
never be slaves enjoy the blessings of Freetrade
in coffin-shaped boxes, is the strongest of all—and,
on the whole, the best acted. The ranting orator
and the other types of submerged decimals are all
true to life.
* * * *
“Twelfth Night,” considering its advanced
age, is a most vigorous comedy of love, liquor
and practical jokery. When the Janet Waldorf
Co. played “Twelfth Night” in the middle of an
unfortunate season at Her Majesty’s some 18
months ago, it came as a glad surprise to a good
many Melbourne people, whose remarks on the
subject should excite interest in the present
production at the Princess’s. This arrangement
of the story condensed into three acts
has no special points of difference from
the version Miss Waldorf presented, ex-
cept as regards the staging, and although
one now misses the incomparable unctuousness
of Atholwood’s inebriety, there are several com-
pensating circumstances. Assisted by a fine
orchestra and some capital scenic fixings (that
admit of new “ business ” in Olivia’s Garden), the
Musgrove co. gives a notable entertainment. No
artists could make the loves and misunderstand-
ings of Olivia, Orsino, Viola and Sebastian, look
quite convincing all round, or even half probable,
but the personal appearance of the four young
people appointed to those romantic charac-
ters is at least a handsome apology for
Shakspeare’s fiction. Three of them—Miss
Rignold as Viola, Norman Partridge, the
dejected duke, and Thomas Holding as the alleged
image of Miss R. aforesaid, are about as natural
as the author allows them to be. Miss Nora
Kerin speaks the lines of Olivia in a strictly arti-
ficial way. Her performance is poor. Neverthe-
less the duke’s infatuation is explained and the
fact that a strange young man bites like asharkat
Olivia’s offer of marriage is not a surprising fact.
Miss Kerin’s countenance imparts a beautiful
truthfulness to the Shakspearian incident, any-
how. In the plot to fool Malvolio the mpst
effective figure is Miss Maud Milton, whose talent
for broad comedy has greatly expanded since
she first played Audrey in “As You Like
It.” Athol Forde, as Sir Toby, is less
satisfactory in the kitchen scene than in the
garden. The Aguecheek of Croker-King is more
than passable, whilst Kenningham’s clown has
the advantage of a cultivated tenor voice as com-
pared with the brighter clown that Edmund
Gwenn introduced at the other theatre. Lathbury
and Coe fill their places, and the Malvolio of
W. H. Denny is an admirable performance. The
Countess’s steward bears himself lilte a vain man
of fair intelligence rather than a bombastic fool.
There is no buffooning to the gallery. The actor
rarely or never steps outside of his character
to force the absurdity of the situation. Finally
he turns the audience’s sympathy to Malvolio.
The man has suffered severely for the crime of
over-rating his self-importance—which is a pretty
general misdemeanor, anyhow. A weaker
steward would have been captured by Maria, who
must have laid a good many snares for her ginger-
headed boss before she denounced him as sour
grapes. Melbourne Shakspeare Society should
start to discuss the character of Maria in the light
of her marriage with Toby Belch.
* * *
“A Country Girl” prospers at Her
Majesty’s. It belongs to the numerous tadpole
family of musical comedies beginning with a
swollen head and leading to nothing in particular,
but the head in this case contains a new idea or
two, and the tail is exceptionally sudden. There’s
a lot of mystery to be cleared away when the
orchestra knocks off work. One goes home feeling
interested in several questions which were
taken up and dropped. Miss Young and
Reg. Roberts appear to know what is going
to happen to them, also the creamy-brown Prin-
cess from Bhong has a good grip of an intended
husband, and the Minister for Fine Arts seems*
free from all anxiety. The future of the other
people is not even suggested. What becomes of
the proud but unreposeful lady who can’t pay her
milliner’s bill? Does Lauri marry Rose Mus-
grove and take over the bad debts of the business,
or does he go into partnership with Raikes the
Rajah ? And where does the significance of Maud
Chetwynd come in ? Nobody attempts to explain
Maud’s conduct. She dances on and dances off
like a burst of madness, or a celestial columbine.
*# * *
A realistic thunderstorm commands a
measure of reverence for Bland Holt’s latest Aus-
tralian drama at Melbourne Royal. This is a
fine work of theatrical nature on its damp and
noisy “own,” and it has even a dim connection
with the drama, for when Brown and
Styan are gloating over some stolen papers
in the foreground of the thunderstorm (a
most unsuitable spot for examining docu-
ments) the lunatic heir to the missing Murrum-
bidgee miser meets a mysterious fate in
the background. A flash of lightning hurls him
into a raging torrent, or the earth opens and
swallows him up, whereupon the villains are
understood to congratulate one another, but the
voice of the tempest renders them inaudible. The
two bad men of the drama are always thwarted in
their endeavors to score, although Styan, the
bolder spirit, has a good try for a treble event in
between the periods of comic relief. Having choked
Miss Ireland and dropped Baker down a gold mine,
he straightway proceeds to marry Miss Ross at a
Westralian bush chapel, but Baker comes back
from the bowels of the earth and sneaks the bonny
bride from his mortal foe. Styan being placed
under arrest, Miss Ross decides to marry Baker
rather than let the wedding breakfast be
wasted ; so the parson joins their hands, and
Baker’s honest Australian heart is made happy
by the thought that he has trumped the knave’s
trick in “ A Desperate Game.”
** * *
“ Little Red Riding Hood,” at the Bijou,
has settled down into a likeable little panto., as
pantos, go. Three or four, or five, matinees were
included in last week’s work, and the members of
the company now present a hard, muscular
appearance when they grapple with their topical
wheezes and struggle to maintain harmonious
relations with the orchestra. If the orchestra
were as bright as the ballet of children in the first
act, “Red Riding Hood” would supply the
annual want more pleasingly than at present.
* * #
Wirth’s Circus, which arrived on top of a
delusive heat-wave, and is likely to be chased out
by a blizzard, will conclude its wet and dusty
Melbourne fortnight on Friday (Bth). Till then,
Pagel will defy the surrounding populace to
rival his feats of strength, and the Howard
Bros, will amaze all - comers at the big
tent by the flowing Yarra. Likewise the
rest of the Circus will be there in all its glory
until the date aforementioned. At the Athenaeum
Hall a Biograph of superior quality is presenting
a new Bull Fight and other pictures of less
lamentable interest. Melbourne has a fitful
craving for entertainment at the Athenaeum. The
Biograph happened to strike the city when a fit
was due.
* # *
At the Opera House (where Vulcana and
Atlas toy with tons of metal as before) a highly
favorable impression was made by Clark and
Hamilton for a night or two after Xmas. Then the
convalescent lady had a relapse, and the musical
sketch, in which she takes only a minor part, was
suspended until further orders. Winton and
M'Ginty came to hand on Saturday, likewise
Johnny Johnson, who plays the’cello and raises
laughs. When a musical artist ventures to bill
himself as “Johnny,” he practically enters into a
solemn contract to be comic. He must excite
merriment somehow, or bust in the endeavor.
** # *
Adelaide Tivoli has re-opened with an
“all-star ” variety co., mostly composed of local
artists. The show fills an evident public demand
for the holiday season. The most startling turn
is an aerial hypnotic item by the Silvesters, the
lady being apparently balanced by one arm on a
single upright support. It is an old trick, but
always effective. Fitzgerald’s circus has been
doing great business with two shows a day. The
lion has to perform without any Pagel to throw
him about this year, and seems much happier for
the change ; but the tiger rides a padded horse, as
before. The tiger relieves his feelings with his
teeth on the pad ; an expectant public is waiting
for him to bite the horse. Ada Crossley is
due for a big farewell at the Exhibition.
** # *
The only performance at the Adelaide
Royal has been in the newspapers. Lessee Pol-
lock, having engaged the Beatrice Esmond Co. to
play “Fauntleroy” on Boxing Night, was faced
by a demand for arrears of salaries about 5 o’clock
that afternoon. As he had had nothing to do
with the co.’s previous wanderings he naturally
refused to oblige, and the whole crcwd, except
Miss Esmond and the manager, went on strike.
Public opinion, at first strongly against the
strikers, veered round on their announcing that
the salaries for their up-country touring are about
six months in arrear ; but it is felt that they need
not have fooled the public, and, as a matter of
fact, a fairly successful holiday season in Adelaide
would have given a chance to the treasury and to
the players.
On Xmas Day, Dean Yance, and on
Jan. 2, Canon Goodman (Vic.), celebrated the
jubilees of their ordination.
The annual “Commemoration” of S.A.,
on December 28, saw the “roll call” at Glenelg
signed by six men out of those who came out in
1836 with Governor Hindmarsh in the Buffalo.
The proceedings are held, as far as possible, under
the same gum-tree where he read his proclamation
on landing. Several others (including one lady)
also date from 1836, and of the “pioneers” (up to
and including 1840) there are still nearly 100
living and able to attend.
Christmas carol singing has become a great
nuisance in Adelaide, and an expensive nuisance
at that. Chief Justice Way inserted advertise-
ments asking that no midnight warblers should
butcher sleep at his door, and a popular Adelaide
parson, whose income is more restricted than his
family, says it cost him 30s. to pay six dreadful
bands to go away in the early hours of Christmas
morning.
PHIL MAY IN AUSTRALIA.
An Edition de luxe of a book under this title, con-
taining nearly one hundred pages of reproduc-
tions of the best and most interesting drawings
made by Phil May in this country, will be pub-
lished by The Bulletin Newspaper Company on
17th January.
The contents are examples of May’s work in
portraiture, caricature, social and political car-
tooning, and humorous subjects. Many of the
pages contain three or four drawings ; so that
the total content is more than two hundred
separate subjects.
These drawings are of unusual interest to the
artist and connoisseur. They exhibit the develop-
ment of the talent of the most brilliant pen-
draughtsman of his generation—possibly of any
generation—in the various phases through which
he reached his unrivalled original style. Apart
from the artistic interest, they have a unique
Australian interest, in that they contain portraits
of most of the Australian celebrities of the
’eighties, and of many who are still living to-day.
Most of the original drawings reproduced were
recently sold at an average price of 3 1. 3s. each.
A few others, not exhibited or sold, are now
included. Three-hundred and fifty proofs of some
of the subjects have been sold at from 2s. 6 d. to
7s. 6 d. each.
The price of the present volume, to subscribers
whose names and remittances are received before
January 17, is 10s. 6d. net. The edition is limited
to 975 copies. The special copies (25) at 11. 11s. (>d.
each have been subscribed, and are already quoted
at 21. 2s. and over.
On publication, the price of the 10s. 6d. edition,
if any copies remain not subscribed, will be raised
to 11. Is.
The book of Phil May in Australia is printed on
art paper, and bound in half-cloth with a decora-
tive panel. The size is demy folio (18in. x 12in.)
—that is, the full size of The Bulletin, and the
book has been printed in first-class style. Other
features are a special end-paper (Australian
landscape) designed by Percy Lindsay; a very
full iconography ; and a prefatory account of the
life and work of May.
The book is one of the most ambitious and
interesting yet attempted in Australia, and the
publishers’ cost for engraving and printing will
scarcely be repaid at the subscription price. On a
comparison of relative values with similar im-
ported books by foreign illustrators, the book is
well worth a guinea.
The book will not be reprinted in its present
form, and subscribers will thus obtain a rare
edition, which is practically certain to be en-
hanced in price and value as the years pass.
Rough sample copies may be seen at The
Bulletin offices, 214 George-street, Sydney, and
Old Exchange, 369 Collins-street, Melbourne,
where subscriptions will be received. Owing to
the size and weight of the book, delivery will be
made at these offices, or as arranged. Where
copies must be sent by post, it is necessary to add
2s. 6d. for postage.
The Wild Cat Column.
It is easy enough to see the small, isolated hard-
ships incidental to the working of the N.S.W. Arbitration
Act; it isn’t so easy to see all the trouble it prevents.
This week a reduced hewing rate has been introduced at
Newcastle without any ructions, notwithstanding that the
men are said to be almost unanimously of opinion that
there is a good case for resisting it. Without the Act all
that they could do would be to strike ; with the Act they
simply proceed with work at the lower rate of pay
(3s. lOd. per ton, as against the previous 4s. 2d.) and
appeal to the Arbitration Court to decide whether it is
fair or not. The owners say that the selling price has
fallen so much from the 11s. per ton on the basis
of which the 4s. 2d. rate was fixed in 1901 that
no profit now remains, hut in many cases a loss;
while the men say that, if there has been a fall in the
selling price, it has been avoidable, and the result of
cut-throat competition among the producers. There is a
conflict, also, as to what the selling rate really is. The
whole dispute will be before the court within the next
two months. Meantime, work proceeds as quietly as
usual. Contrast this with the year of struggle, the
bitterness, the loss, and the suffering which, in the
absence of an Arbitration law, has followed in Victoria
upon an almost precisely similar difference of opinion at
the Gippsland collieries.
* * * *
In making his final report to Sydney City Coun-
cil after being for two years Mayor, Thomas Hughes showed
how the Gas Co. had tried not only to dodge the rates
which the courts lately said it must pay, but to actually
penalise the city for attempting to collect the money.
For 60 years this most profitable monopoly has had the
use of the streets for nothing. Others have been taxed
on their improvements ; the Gas Co. has laid its mains in
the streets, paid a 15 per cent.'dividend, and escaped
free. The courts finally said that it was liable to pay, and
the amount was fixed at £2233 per year for the
past two years. Thereupon, notwithstanding that
the price of gas to the ordinary consumer has been
reduced since the last agreement with the co. was made
for street-lighting, the co. called upon the city to pay
£2906 a year more than it has been paying. In other
words, the city, instead of collecting the new rates from
the co., would actually be £673 worse off than before the
court’s decision was given in the city’s favor. That plain
attempt to defeat the law was discreditable enough ; but
the co. did still worse. With the knowledge (for it must
have had the knowledge) that the city’s electric
lighting plant would be ready by the middle of the
year to do half of the city lighting, it demanded
that the city should pay the co. at the higher rate
for lighting the whole of the city for a fixed term
terminating not before 31st December, 1905. The city
which had dared to collect rates from this 15 per cent,
monopoly was thus not only going to be charged so much
more that the co. would actually make a profit out of
being rated, but the co. sought to squeeze the rate-
payers into paying for the lighting of the whole city for
18 months after the gas had been to a great extent
replaced by the electric light. And, but for one little
flaw in its position, it might have succeeded ; for the city
must be lighted, and as the Gas Co. alone can supply the
light, the city seemed absolutely at its mercy. To some
extent, however, that one flaw saved the situation—the
co. had overlooked a letter, sent last July, continuing
the present agreement for a year. Of course, when it
discovered that, it withdrew its claws. But the city
knows now that the claws are there. They are no use
till July next; but what then ? The city only proposes
to do half its own lighting, for the present anyway. For
the rest it will be still at the mercy of the co. with the
claws; and a city which has seen the claws, and only
escaped them by a lucky accident, deserves to be scratched
if it deliberately puts itself at the animal’s mercy. There
is only one thing to be done : to hurry on with the
electric light installation and make it capable, not of
dealing with the lighting of only half the city, but of the

The great merits of the Wilfley Concentrator
are evidently fully recognised by the mining community
of Australia ; over 600 of the tables have now been sold
in the Commonwealth and the Sole Agents and Manu-
facturers, the Austral Otis Engineering Company of South
Melbourne, find some difficulty in keeping ahead of their
orders. It is only natural to expect that such a striking
improvement over all previous machines of similar object ,
would speedily attract the attention of the mining
community, and such literally proved to be the case.
Without attempting to follow in detail its phenomenal
success, it will only, be necessary to state that in the
comparatively short time it has been on the market, more
than 600 tables have been sold in Australia, and very
many thousands in America and elsewhere.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

The year’s loss on the Westralian goldfields
water scheme was about £60,000. Practically that is a
subsidy given by a handful of people to some of the
richest foreign mining co’s. in the world. A goldfields
statue of John Forrest, the originator of the scheme,
is now proposed. Instead of honoring Bigjohn, the
Bulletin suggests that they come to the point and honor
the Big Bill.

“ Vul.” : Good old “Private Enterprise” again
—the same moral Private Enterprise which is so horribly
shocked when “socialistic legislation” is whispered!
Two shindykits which recently got concessions in the
private railway-building line —the Cloncurry railway
crowd and the Burketown-Lilydale tramway co.—have
just taken a firm hold of the Bananaland Government
doormat in a brassy cadge for further concessions. The
Cloncurry crowd had the gall to coolly ask the Govern-
ment to guarantee interest on the debentures issued in
connection with the line ! The Government answer to
this bit of assurance was to politely but firmly put the
co. outside on its ear. The request of the Burketown-
Lilydale co. hadn’t transpired at time of writing, but it
is described as running “on somewhat novel lines.”
Judging from the request of the other crowd, which
wasn't thought novel, the request will probably turn out
to be something like a plea for the Government to pay
the co. rent for condescending to run its line in the
“ beastly country.”

* * « »
R. T. Vale, a Vic. ex-Minister, angry at the
statement, “ made on the authority of our responsible
Ministers, of an alleged deficit of £600,000,” has “ care-
fully examined the figures of the Auditor-General,” and
finds that “in six years ending June, 1902, we (Victoria)
paid off old liabilities and many unforeseen expenditures
to the total extent of lj millions.” The result of Shiels’
croaking, he says, has been that the recent renewal of the
loan was disastrous, and “ the loss of credit, which was not
justifiable, has cast upon our shoulders, over the loan-
renewal, a burden altogether of 1£ millions.” Owing
to Victoria’s foolish habit of issuing no monthly expendi-
ture returns, it certainly is quite possible for Shiels or
any other Treasurer to create a huge deficit in the middle
of the year in order to scare the country into starving the
aged poor, or adopting some other panic policy ; and it is
quite possible that, in faking a deficit for political pur-
poses, great damage may be done to the Country’s credit
—as it certainly was done in this case. The remedy
is, first, to create a sinking fund out of which
loans may be repaid, so that the country will
not be so absolutely at the mercy of the Bull-Cohen who
holds the bill that is falling due ; then, to publish at
least quarterly statements of receipts and expenditure,
so that neither vague bogus deficits nor vague bogus
surpluses may be created for political purposes.
As to ex-Minister Vale’s figures, they don’t
tally with Coghlan’s, and Coghlan is a pretty good
authority. Most of the explanation of the difference lies
in the fact that Vale includes such items as old-age pen-
sions, Duke of York celebrations, S’African Contingents,
&c., among the “ unforeseen expenditures.” The money
was spent, anyway. Ooghlan’s figures for the period
are :
June, Revenue. Expenditure. Surplus. Deficit.
1897 .. £6,630,217 £6,568,932 £61,285
1898 .. 6,898,240 6,928,850 £30,610
1899 .. 7,378,842 7,001,663 377,179
1900 .. 7,450,676 7,280,689 169,987
1901 .. 7,722,397 7,683,079 39,318
1902 .. 7,006,333 7,407,781 401,448
Total Surplus .. £647,769
Total Deficit £432,058
Net Surplus for six years .. £215,711
But as against this there was a considerable sum which
should have been paid for out of the proceeds of land-
sales, but wasn’t, the shortage being pub to a suspense
account. The period covered, however, represents
one of the cleanest in Victorian political history;
George Turner was looking after the Treasury most
of the time, and making the reputation which has adhered
to him ever since. Down to the early part of 1901
Turner was in charge ; then Peacock had a brief turn ;
after him the disastrous Irvine and Shiels. In the main,
then, Vale’s contention is sound enough; on the figures
down to the end of Turner’s regime, anyway, there was
no reason for a loss of credit. But after Turner came
Peacock, and Irvine and Shiels ; and Victoria had to be
judged by them, and not by the plain little man in
brown whom it had the good fortune to have at the
Treasury a. few years previously. And, when Victoria
was judged by them, the result was naturally disastrous.
* * * '*
The shareholders in one of the small proprietary
life assurance offices held their annual meeting the other
day, and the proceedings ran into five hours. Such a lot
of nasty things can be said in five hours, and such a lot
of nasty things are liable to be said when no dividend is
payable, that it isn’t altogether surprising that
libel proceedings have followed. The chairman is the
defendant; the manager the prosecutor. The defence
was reserved, and the accused committed. The public
are likely to get some valuable information out of the
proceedings. Already it is alleged that one share-
holder (the chairman) holds, more than half the shares,
and that he had his dummies on the board.
■-» * *
The first item in the first schedule to the Wes-
tralian Loan Bill “ to authorise the. raising of £1,582,000"
by loan for the construction of certain public works,
&c.,” is “Departmental: Administration, £129,320.”
For the first five months of the financial year the Wes-
tralian revenue fell short of the expenditure by £44,107 ;
and, in the absence of any explanation, it looks as though
the State, which ought to be busy making hay while its
rich gold deposits are being wolfed, contemplates
squaring its finances by charging to loan some of
the ordinary expenses of government. Probably no coun-
try in the world has less excuse for borrowing than
Westralia. In the past four years foreign mining co.’s
have taken nearly £6,250,000 out of the State—an average
of about £33 per head of the total mean population
during that period. Meantime the debt has been in-
creased by £5,245,000, or £2B per head of the mean popu-
lation. The outsider has thus taken £33 of gold and left
£2B of debt. Bull-Cohen has in this way been hitherto
good enough to lend the State some of its own gold
at 4 per cent, or so. Even while the money was borrowed
to carry out works from which a return might be got it was
bad enough business ; but it is worse still to borrow in
order to carry on the ordinary administrative work, in-
stead of taxing the foreigners who are carting off the
country’s wealth, and the other people who are making
large incomes out of those high ground-rents which are
also the result of ripping-out the rich ore.
Another small item in the Westralian Loan
Bill badly needs explaining. An amount of £63,000 is to
be applied to “ Discounts on Current Loans.” If this
means what it seems to mean, Westralia proposes to fall
into line with those financial fakirs of the Eastern
States who have been paying interest out of
loans. They all deny, of course, that they
do anything of the sort ; but their denial doesn’t
alter the fact. By offering a low rate of interest Wes-
tralia has had to accept loans at a discount—it has got
probably £96 for every £lOO of stock. The amount of
cash actually received being thus short of the amount of
the loans, it is now proposed to borrow to make up the
difference. If there were no discount a higher rate of
interest would have to be paid; discount, therefore,
represents the difference between the two rates of in-
terest. To borrow in order to pay discount is thus
exactly the same as borrowing to pay interest. It is bor-
rowing to pay interest, in fact. And Westralia has no
drought to urge as an excuse. Its prosperity (reckoning
by production per head of the population) is phenomenal.
Unless, therefore, the Loan Bill item means something
quite different from what it apparently means, its appear-
ance in the Bill represents a most disastrous state of
affairs.

the net interest burden were thus lightened.

It isn’t a new discovery that Queensland can
grow good cotton. At the London Exhibition in 1862 the
State secured a gold medal for a local cotton exhibit—the
plants-were grown within 10 miles of Brisbane. Yet, all
these years, when there wasn’t any Labor party in power
and no Socialistic legislation, the capitalist did not come
along to establish the industry. In fact, it is only now,
just when a semi-Labor party has got into office, that
British cotton people have begun to move in the matter.
It looks as though they had been waiting all these years
for the Labor party to arrive.

*■• > • *
For the first time in the 14 years of its life the
Broken Hill reservoir has been full and overflowing.
There was always a doubt as to whether the weir and
other works would stand under a full flood, but they did.
The co. will not sell much water for many a month ; but
the unusual rains will, no doubt, have drowned the
agitation for a supplementary supply, so the co. can still
afford to smile. Broken Hill soon forgets, and doesn’t
start to remember again until it can almost eat its water
with a fork and feed it into the boilers with a shovel.

B.H. South had a record output the last week
before Christmas ; it milled 3731 tons crude sulphides,
468 tons old tailings, and 193 tons old slimes —in all 4392
tons, bulking 17‘4 per cent, lead, 9'Boz. silver, andlB’Bper
cent. zinc. From this 676 tons concentrates, averaging
69*5 per cent, lead and 29'40z. silver, were obtained.
There were thus 764'2 tons of lead in the ore, and 489*8
tons were saved ; of silver there were 43,0410 z. in the ore,
and 19,8740 z. were saved. Of the zind none was turned
to practical account. Tut into sterling, £5477 worth of
lead was won and £3OBO went into the dumps; of the
silver £2153 was saved and £2510 wasn’t. And all the
zinc wasn’t. Yet the B.H. South does unusually good
mill work. It only shows how far from perfect the mill
process is.

Mount Morgan (Q.) has only had a moderately
profitable half-year, so far as either output or divs. go,
but prospecting has given some important results. The
yield was 50,9320 z. from 123,817 tons, an average of only
BJdwt., or 2dwt. poorer than for the preceding year.
From tailings re-treated another 15490 z. were got;
total, 52,4810 z. The average per half year, in 1902,
was 71,7920 z., and in 1901 73,8140 z. Divs. were
£75,000; total to date, £6,354,166. But while these
results do not compare well with those of the past;
prospects are better. The steam shovel is removing
large quantities of overburden, and making somewhat
richer ore available ; while the drill proves that payable
copper-bearing ore exists at 750 ft. An estimate has been
made that this ore covers two acres, and that for every
10ft. of depth there are 62,000 tons, averaging Bdwt. gold
and 3j per cent, copper—more profitable to treat, the
manager says, than the material which has lately been
worked.

In a recent month the newest mine tool, Mt.
Morgan’s huge steam shovel, shifted something over
26,000 tons of overburden from above the 196 ft. level.

Though Mt. Morgan (Q.) has only 12th place
in London Economist’s list of the greatest gold-producing
mines in the world, the writer (J. H. Curie) says :—“ The
‘ greatest ’ gold mine is claimed for probably 20 localities,
although I think, taking its past, present, and future
into consideration, that Mt. Morgan still holds that
title.” He still regards Fingall as “intrinsically the
best” of the Westralian mines.
*'« * *
The long-promised Admiralty test of Callide
(Central Queensland) coal has been disappointing. For a
given duty the consumption was 30 per cent, more than
of other Australian coal, and there was also an objection-
able rush of sparks. Still, it is surface coal, and may
improve with depth as other coals have done—Tasmanian
coals, for instance. As to the rush of sparks, Callide
coal has now to be carted 80 miles by road, and conse-
quently the test parcel was delivered much broken, and
probably required special feeding. One excellent feature
of the new coal is its low percentage of ash.

“ W.0.L.”: A new shaft-sinking record has
just been put up on the Towers (Q.), at the Queen Cen-
tral Block, where manager and 20 men, all on wages,
sank a four-compartment shaft 140 ft. in four weeks, tim-
bering 31ft. The sinking was solid grey granite (and C.T.
grey granite is solid). Three shifts were worked. Could
our Chow-loving friend Bull come within cooee of this in
any of his nigger-run properties? Ever and anon some
fatted British mining expert wails that it is impossible
for white men to work in tropical Australia. Yet Char-
ters Towers is tropical enough.

* * * *
Lancefield, Laverton (W.A.), recently pur-
chased by Bewick, Moreing and Co. for £50,000, is to be
equipped with a 12-drill air compressor, and a splendid
40-head mill, acquired from the defunct wild cat Euro.
The mine has been worked for some years, and has paid
monthly divs. of £360. Only three levels have been
worked, the lode ranging from 18ft. to 26ft., and worth
from 8 to The shaft is 236 ft. deep, and all the
ore so far has been oxidised. Even with the enlarged
mill it is unlikely that the, profit will exceed £l5OO
monthly, so the mine cannot stand a very high
capitalisation. It is the lowest-grade proposition
in W.A. that has been worked at a profit, and its
acquisition by John Bull may be an object lesson to the
mineowners of the Rand, who argue that colored labor is
essential to cheap gold-mining.
* * * »
Bellevue, Mt. Sir Samuel, will, before long, be
one of the dozen big gold-producers of W.A. The new
40-head mill is expected to treat some 5000 tons monthly,
as the old 20-head, though admittedly obsolete and badly
erected, has got through nearly 2000 tons monthly.
Above No. 3 level it is estimated that over 53,000 tons of
ore are in reserve, and the chute has been proved to be
over 900 ft. in length. The new main shaft is being
pushed ahead, and will be ready when the mill starts.
The co. has been earning a profit of £7OO monthly, but it
is expected to be much more when the new mill talks.
The co. owns 197 acres, and has a capital of 400,000
shares, of which 10,000 are held in reserve. It started
two years ago with a working capital of £70,000, a good
deal of which must have gone in buying and transport-
ing the new plant. The weakness of the position is that
the Gold Finance Corporation and Berwick, Moreing,
and Co.’s firm hold 230,000 shares between them, and may
start to realise on any advance.
• • « •
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.) is in a rather precarious
position; twice in a fortnight the machinery has been
hung up through lack of water for the boilers. A start
has been made to sink a water shaft about four miles
away, but this will take time and money. Through ex-
cess of “ economy," it is also stated that the reserves are
not so large as they should be, and that it is taking the
men all their time to keep the mill fully supplied. The
mine is being run very economically, and, unless the
water supply gives out, it is unlikely that returns will
fall, as development can be easily pushed ahead by
engaging more men.
• • * *
North Boulder, Kalgoorlie, reports that a bore-
hole from the 600 ft. level has cut a lode 4ft. wide assay-
ing 24dwt. per ton at a distance of 328 ft. The history of
boreholes has been so unsatisfactory that no one now
takes them seriously. The market, evidently, views this
borehole with indifference, as the scrip shows no tendency
to move. There is no known lode of any value either
north or south of the property, and it is safest to wait
until exploration proves such a lode to exist.
* * * *
Great Boulder Prop., Kalgoorlie, intends,
immediately after the holidays, to sink a new main shaft
on the north block adjoining Boulder No. 1. On this
lease boreholes put down from Jansen’s shaft intersected
the Ivanhoe East lode at 400, 850, 1050 and 1350 ft. The
lode'averaged 10ft. in width, and ran from 8 to 25dwt.,
the best values being got at the lowest point reached.
The new shaft is to be 14 x sft., the biggest on the mine.
Jansen’s shaft, immediately north of the Ivanhoe, is
being enlarged, and the work will shortly be completed.
The co. .evidently intends to go in for a much more active
policy, and, as it holds 85 acres, or double that of any other
of the big mines, and all splendidly situated, a more go-
ahead policy is fully justified. The lode at 1750 ft. fully
maintains its width and value. There is talk of doubling
the treatment plant, but nothing very definite so far.

further 19 tons for 3190 z. over the plates. Total to date,
37430 z. from 219 tons, for less than two years’ work. The

Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), continues to
prove that the mine is not nearly so worked-out as>share-
holders were told. The latest return published reads
3860 z. from 11 tons, bringing the total since Octoher to
12620 z. from 142 tons. This is a better monthly return
than was got by treating lOdwt. ore at practically no
profit, and shows what noses tributors have for gold.
Developments at four levels on the co’s new property,
the South Gippsland, are still being pushed ahead, and
the mine looks fairly promising. The 10-head mill is
running full time in conjunction with the cyanide plant,
and the full strength of the property should shortly be
known.

Hampton Syndicate, Coolgardie, has just
treated 23 tons of wash for 2390 z., bringing the total,
including dollied gold, to 6390 z. London shareholders in
Hampton Plains Estate are nearly frenzied over this find,
and the secretary has been obliged to circularise them to
appease their appetites. The find is not a lode, but
merely a lower deposit of alluvial than that worked by
the .Italian wood-cutters some years ago. Had this fact
been cabled, instead of it being stated “ have cut reef at
lot of unnecessary excitement might
have been avoided.

The Westralian copper output is now something
considerable. They hear more about it in London than
Australia does. Early in November this cable was pub-
lished there regarding the output from the Anaconda
(Murrin) mine : “ Shipped, per Oratava, 180 tons matte.
Estimate will ship 150 tons 16th Nov., 400 tons 30th Nov.,
400 tons 14th Deo.” The matte runs from 50 to 52 per
cent, metal, so that these lots would contain about 570
tons of copper, worth £32,000. For some time, the dis-
trict monthly output has been nearly 2500 tons of ore, and
the total yield so far about 35,000 tons. It was lately re-
ported, nevertheless, that there was a considerable
amount of wages unpaid ; and, if that is so, it looks like
evidence of bad management somewhere

A man from Melbourne writes :
The Coogee-Fortunato Figari collision in Bass Strait on
Christmas morning was perhaps (it is impossible to say
definitely) another of the results of that noble system of
bustling competition. All sorts of rules and regulations
for the conduct of navigation in fogs are carefully set out
by the shipping authorities, and are as carefully set aside.
Of course, shipowners do not say to their captains, “ You
AT THE PARTY.
“ Now, darling, they're going to play Zoo, and want you to be a lion.'
“Don’ wanter be lion—wanter be sick.”
mustn’t let a fog make any difference to your speed.”
What happens is this : In the worst fog there is always
one captain, at least, who sprints through, and so long as
he gets through it is assumed that his course of action
was quite safe, and that the captains who didn’t sprint
were over-cautious and inefficient. So the captain who
doesn’t trade in very crowded waters finds it best to
sprint. The late Captain Carrington had frequently
come up the Bay on time in a fog so dense that almost
every other vessel had been brought to anchor. . When
that sort of thing comes off it s good seamanship—infact,
it is good seamanship whether it comes off or not. At
the same time there are risks about it. Carrington must
have been a man of iron nerve and wonderful presence
of mind. He could only have seen the Fortunato Figari a
minute before the collision occurred. It was a paralysing
the Coogee would have struck the Fortunato square on
the bow and cut its nose clean off. In doing so it would
have shattered its own bows, and it would have been a
race between them for the bottom of the sea.
The respondent in the M‘Master divorce
case (Maoriland) is a vivacious, graceful lady of
38, while the gentleman cited as 00., Isidore
Kino, sharebroker, of Sydney, was described
as a man of irreproachable deportment, aged
60—“ an elderly man, with the manners and
conduct of an elderly man.” Petitioner,
Ronald M‘Master, is a sheep-farmer, with
turfy tastes ; and when he separated from his
wife in ’95 he allowed her £5 a week. The lady
joined a married sister (whose husband was in
Westralia) in Sydney, and they lived at Petty’s
and the Empire, and afterwards set up house,
Kino, who had boarded at both pubs, mentioned,
joining them and paying £3 a week board-money.
Kino’s Stock Exchange knowledge proved invalu-
able, and Mrs. M'Master’s speculations were pro-
ductive of auriferous results. He also gave her
racing tips, but she didn’t mention how they
panned out. The couple occupied contiguous
rooms at the Empire, “quite by accident,” and
had a sitting-room in common, and although he
called her “Fan,” and she called him “ Isidore,”
there was never the slightest impropriety between
them. “He had too much respect for her even to
kiss her.” Some interesting photographs, how-
ever, taken from a building opposite the hotel, as
well as other evidence, made out a strong case
for the petitioner, who got his verdict, though a
.doubt was raised as to whether it was possible to
have taken the photos, in the circumstances in
which it was alleged they were taken. The case
lasted five days, and counsels’ addresses occupied
over 10 hours in all.
* * * •
“Pint”: As year succeeds year the
annual 25th of December festival degenerates
more and more into a mere drunk. This Christ-
mas in Melbourne was more drunk than usual.
We are driven to drink by a thermometer at 100°
in the shade, and by the memory of the Irvine
Government. Holiday drunks vary. On Christmas
Day we get gloriously drunk; at New Year we
get drunk on principle ; but Good Friday is a
sad, solemn sort of inebriation —drunk by force of
circumstances, not from choice. Apropos of
nothing in particular, the City Court bench broke
all holiday traditions by fining the Christmas
drunk the ordinary work-a-day dollar and costs.
Does this not bring the J.’sP. under the all-
embracing condemnation of being pro-Boers ?
* * * *
In Melbourne, the other day, an ill-regu-
lated religious person stood for a while in the
middle of a city street preaching and giving away
money to the poor. A constable who asked
questions was informed by the preacher that the
Lord said in the New Testament, “ Sell all thou
hast and give to the poor.” He promptly
arrested the preacher for insulting behavior. On
the way to the watchhouse the unfortunate
suddenly lost the power of speech. Given a paper
and pencil, he wrote, rationally enough, that he
was an ex-public servant who had resigned to
preach the gospel. He was taken to the Melb.
hospita] and there recovered Ins speech ; then lost
1 • ’ mi i ~A 4. \/r •
it again Then he wrote asking that a Minister,
whom he named, should be sent for to identify
him. The Minister came and spoke to the tem-
porary inmate, and while the latter was writing a
reply he suddenly lost the power to write, and his
cd draggled inanely over the paper. When
r r , ~b 8 . , , ~ , , ~
he faced the court next day as an alleged lunatic
he had recovered and spoke oleaiiy and rationally,
He asked if the New Testament, which he was
literally obeying, was supposed to be a deranged
publication. The court refused to argue the
One of the most remarkable features of the law is
the off-hand way in which it decides that any
literal adhesion to the New Testament is a sign of
lunacy.
A CHIEF JUSTICE’S HOLIDAY—AND
YET HE KEEPS IT IN CURL.
At the conclusion of the Webster insurance case
His Wax is left with a whole library of account
books and several volumes of MS. notes to thresh
out the issue.
Auckland (M.L.) has put up an Austra-
lasian record in the way of a tramway accident.
In one of its precipitous streets a tram car, whioh
had been brought to a stop, started to slip back.
The brake refused to act, and in the end the car
bolted down the street at 40 or 50 miles an hour,
and collided with another loaded car coming up.
Three persons were killed at once, and about 100
injured. This is the second time that Private
Enterprise has supplied Maoriland with a ghastly
tram accident. It is alleged that the driver had
previously reported the brake as not working
well, but that Private Enterprise, presumably
with a view to keeping down expenses, reckoned
it would do well enough. It is a way Private
Enterprise has.
Those people who threw heavy rocks
at the management of Auckland (M.L.) Costley
Home —kept going by a bequest left by thrifty
old man Costley, who died some years back—have
had their allegations more than sustained by the
Royal Commission set up to investigate. The
Commission paid a surprise visit to the Home,
and seems to have found a shocking state of affairs.
The bedding was filthy ; the aspect of the dormi-
tories desolate and comfortless; clothes dirty;
food bad, and not enough of it; drainage defec-
tive ; staff insufficient. Further, there was abun-
dant evidence that some of the parties concerned
had for years been afflicted with a chronic thirst,
and had taken an inordinate amount of whisky to
suppress it. The master and matron have been
dispensed with by the Hospital Board, but the
Commission, while admitting that these officials
were to some extent blair.eable, said they couldn’t
be expected to work the Home satisfactorily with
so small a staff. The report is really a strong
indictment against a bumptious Board of self-
satisfied gentlemen, arrayed in fine linen, who
have for years been bossing a disgracefully dirty
show without ever finding out what the place was
like.
A quaint tale of simplicity was un-
folded to theEssendon (Vic.) magistrates,
the other day, by a lady, who solemnly
assured them thxta hard-faced individual,
whom she charged with obtaining £1 by
false pretences, had called at her house a
day or two previously, when her husband
was out, and had been much moved on
being informed of his absence. The gentle-
man with the cast-iron countenance had, he
said, a “dead bird,” a real “moral,” and
though he was an entire strar.g< r to both
her and her husband, it was such a fine
opportunity for them to make a few pounds
with absolutely no risk whatever that lie
almost wept on the doorstep at the thought
of the lost chance. His grief so moved
her that she gave him £1 to “put on.”
He didn’t tell her the name of the horse
or cow or goat that was such a “moral”;
he just strolled away to “put it on”—and
she wanted her pound back, or condign
punishment meted out, or something. Then
the Law remarked that the money was
given to be “put on,” and there was no
evidence it had not been so placed ; in fact,
it might be on even then for all she knew ;
and the case was dismissed.
• * * *
That self - satisfied, sanctimonious
person, Justice Hodges of Victoria, declines
to take the repeated advice of Inspectors-
General and other competent authorities
as to the evil effects of solitary confinement;.
At last Melbourne Sessions he had to deal
with four cases of housebreaking and two
of false pretences. All the accused w T ere
under 28 years of age. His Honah, being in
a drastic mood, handed out terms of two years, with
nine days’ solitary; 15 months, with six days
solitary; three years, with nine days’ solitary ;
two years, with ditto; and nine years, with 96
days’ solitary. The last prisoner—a youth of 18
and the housebreaking-apprentice of the previous
prisoner—was practically a first offender though
there were four charges against him, but llis
Honah was in good working order by now and
promptly passed sentence of eight years gaol with
16 days’ solitary. A very solitary man is Justice
Hodges.
• • * *
The Anglican Church is an institution
which sets great store by its clothes, and it is
much more frequently troubled over questions
concerning the proper hang of its skirt than
about living up to the doctrines of the New
Testament. The days when its petticoat bulges
ungracefully are days of gloom for the church,
and it is ofttimes torn asunder by deep questions
relating to frill, and by bitter tumults con-
cerning guipure and insertion. The dark hour is
upon it again, and once more the church’s bangle
has got it down and bitten it, and the Sydney
clergy have memorialised the Archbishop about
“illegal vestments and other ornaments and
ceremonial” to this effect:
The toleration of these illegalities, on the one hand, is
alienating much sympathy, and is causing spiritually-
minded men of faith and prayer to leave our church, as
being unfaithful to its historical position in relation to
doctrine and ritual; and, on the other hand, is exposing
us as its ministers to the charge of breaking the law, on
the supposition that the Mass vestments are the legal
vestments of our church for the celebration of the Lord’s
Supper.
This break-up of “ spiritually-minded men ” over
the proper angife of the church’s crinoline is a
pathetic circumstance.

CHRISTMAS COURTESIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is something in the Christmas-tide so pleasant ;
’Tis the one benign hiatus in our lives.
It is lovely to receive a Christmas present
Which is purchased (with our earnings) by our wives 1
We awake on Christmas morn, as usual, snarling ;
But our wives are wearing faces wreathed in smiles,
And they murmur : “ Guess what I have bought you
darling ?
Such a lovely tray-cloth—quite the best of styles !’’
Or perhaps they buy a tiddly-winking tea-set,
One with cups which go a hundred to the quart!
That’s the kind of Christmas present that gets me set —
Cups that hold a thimble-full—you know the sort 1
Or, maybe, they buy a pretty front-room curtain,
To adorn the room their husbands never use 1
And they always seem so absolutely certain
We should thank them, that we cannot well refuse.
Oh, it’s always “ something useful” that they buy us ;
“ Something useful ” that’s no earthly use to us t
And we have to play the part of Ananias,
And express delight while inwardly we “ cuss.”
But of course it doesn’t do to be unpleasant;
And I’m growing weary of the barber’s shop,
So I’ve bought my wife a useful New Year present,
■ Which includes a pair of razors and a strop 1
W. T. GOODGR.
NEW YEAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Is it friend, or is it foe,
Drawing near?
Is it weal, or is it woe
I should fear ?
Hear those bells—now loud, now low—
’Tis New Year!
Old, old Year, your course is run ;
Toil and heat—
Rising moon and set o’ sun,
Sour and sweet;
Passion blighted, task begun—
Pride—Defeat.
Gazing at the Year new born
Moans my soul:
“ Must I ever be forlorn
Miss the goal ?
Seek the rose, and find the thorn—
Lose the whole ? ”
Hark ! those bells—how loud they ring 1
'Tis New Year 1
Shall I sob, or sigh, or sing,
Laugh, or fear ?
What’s my Fate (I’m wondering)—
Joy ?—Despair ?

The trouble about Melen Myers’ lost
jewellery, a great deal of which was found on the
premises of Moss Aarons, helps to rescue Mel-
bourne from total stagnation these times. Melen
Myers’ stock was insured against burglary, and it
■was burgled last October to the extent of £3200.
According to the daily paper report:
A detective said the aperture in the ceiling was llin. x
llin. Under the aperture was a show-case about 12ft.
Delow the ceiling, and it bore no marks of having been
Stood on, nor was there any trace of matches or candle-
grease about, and the room had not been disturbed.
Apart from the hole in the roof, and the back door having
apparently been forced open, there was a total absence of
any evidence such as might be expected to be found in
such circumstances.
As the hole in the ceiling was rather more than
half the size of a page of The Bulletin, Moss
.Aarons is understood to rely on his proportions as
evidence that he didn’t personally fall through it
into Melen Myers’ premises. The additional fact
that very few pawnbrokers would drop 12ft. on
to a show case without leaving some marks is also
in his favor here. Moss Aarons says he bought
the jewellery from a complete stranger, and has
rio recollection how much he paid for it. Anyhow
it was found on his premises, and he fainted
about six times while Melen Myers was identi-
fying it. As regards that portion of Melen
Myers’ jewellery which wasn’t found on Moss
Aarons’ premises, a good many items had been
pawned at different shops in Melbourne and
suburbs. Whether they were pawned by the
man who sold Melen Myers’ jewellery to Moss
Aarons for an amount which Moss Aarons can’t
locate just now, isn’t yet known. While the
detectives are on the case, they might try to find
out how two men come to be named Moss and
Melen. Moss is a name that might grow on
a man if he lived in a cool, damp spot, but Melen
is a mystery.

At Temora (N.S.W.) a police officer had
to nurse the babies of some of the women voters
■whilst they marked their papers. Again, the
logical complement of the woman voter is the
woman policeman. The average male constable
won’t submit to having nursemaid duties added to
his already long list of woes.
• • * *
Somebody flew off the handle in the Age
office over the Coogee-Fortunato Figari collision.
After a touching reference to “the calni confi-
dence of life and the easy carelessness of mirth or
amusement” being “ broken into by the swoop of
imminent death,” the Age threw off this very fine
and large sentence just as easily as anyone else
might throw a speck of fluff off his ear:—
At this season of the year, which above all others calls
for the display of all that is tender, sympathetic and
generous, when the impulse to embrace the whole world
in peace and goodwill is strongest, and when we approach
nearest to that brotherhood of man which is still the
dream of poets, the sweep of Azrael’s wing casts a very
dark and impressive shadow on those whom it brushes
yet spares.
Then came a statement that the route followed
by the Coogee is “as well known to mariners as a
suburb*,n railway line is to an experienced driver,”
immediately followed by this sentence: “This
route is almost like the path leading to a seques-
tered nook, the meeting place of lovers.” The
concluding passages were sublime :
There was a sudden shock as of the irresistible meeting
the immovable, a grinding, crunching noise, like the
crash of an avalanche upon a forest, and all hearts stood
still for an instant, as the paralysing conviction was
flashed home to consciousness that a collision in the open
sea with all its fearful possibilities of fatality had
occurred. There seemed to be a moment of silence dread-
ful in its intensity, and then the catastrophe which had
come upon the steamer out of the mist was rendered even
more horrifying by the hissing escape of steam, like the
shrieks and moans of one in dying moments. The
passengers asleep rushed on declc, realising but imperfectly
at first the peril which surrounded them. They saw a
huge threatening shape enveloped in the fog, and
bewilderingly turned to one another for sympathy and
information.
THE SERENADE.
“ Vul.” : Wonderful the large quantity of
grateful bug tbe beio of romance usually gets,
while the hero in real every-day life gets a very
unexpected sort of reward indeed. At Adelaide,
t’other day, the first mate of a ship took his life in
his hands and dived to the rescue of one of his
seamen in the water. He got the drowning one
on hoard with difficulty, but had hardly time to
shake the sea from his whiskers before the rescued
man was on his feet forcibly inquiring why he (the
mate) wanted to interfere in what didn’t concern
him; then he- drew a knife and slit his rescuer
from shoulder to waist with a deep gash.
When arrested, later, he admitted the deed, but
softened so far as to allow that what pained him
most was the news that the mate would probably
recover.

Apropos the “ strike ” of Sydney Rabbis.
It was not until the thirties that the Hebrews of
Sydney associated for religious purposes. The
building first used as a synagogue still stands in
Bridge-street within one door of George-street.
In after years it was used as a store by D. S.
Gordon, and later by Charles Bath, who also kept
the St. John’s Tavern next door. Nowit is a
whisky shop.
* • • *
Boxing Day is a lost institution in S.A.
It gains no respect whatever. Even the name is
becoming obsolete. It has been absorbed into
28th Dec. —Commemoration Day. On that day
the best part of 100,000 people steer for Glenelg.
Here they roam aimlessly over a sandy piece of
Gulf shore, a patch of scrub, and a long jetty.
The chief attraction is an old gum tree to which
the first batch of colonists tacked a declaration of
dependence or something of that sort. This tree
is religiously examined on the 28th, and speeches
mad 3 round it. Nearly every Adelaidean has
seen it before, but a look at it breaks the
monotony which is otherwise relieved by a duck-
hunt, a swimming contest or something else
equally exciting. These 28ths of Dec. have
unanimously agreed to rise to over 100 in the
shade, and people who make the trip mostly swear
that they’ll never do it any more, but next year
sees another big crowd, another duck-hunt,
another big heat, and another desolate inspection
of the gum-tree.
