“ F.” writes :
Gov.-General Tennyson has suggested _to Premier
Deakin that the time has arrived when the history of the
Australian contingents in South Africa should be written.
An excellent idea! Let the narrative run into as many
volumes as may be necessary to do the fullest justice to
the subject. Let it contain, inter alia, exhaustive tables
showing the number of farmhouses burnt, pigs
bayonetted, fowls scragged, pianos and pictures in-
cinerated for billy-boiling purposes, Bibles purloined,
&c. ; the number of crusaders employed in each exploit;
the rate of pay they received for thus furthering the
cause of “ Empire ” ; the number of bars they demolished
and peaceable citizens they bonnetted when they came
back to Capetown ; together with photos, of the heroes
who “ captured ” the whiskers and toe-nails of slaugh-
tered Boers as mementoes, and copious extracts from
“ letters from the Front ” in the Australian press, wherein
the valiant writers described their exploits, and said, “ I
enclose an ear.” Yes, let’s have the history ;it will be
interesting.
• • * *
Queen Alexandra’s prudent retreat from
her slightly burning bedroom has revived a num-
ber of stories of royal “escapes.” The funniest
story records a deadly peril that threatened King
Edward, when he was still Prince of Wales. He
was out shooting pheasants with a party of Peers,
and one Peer seemingly must have mistaken him
for a bird. Anyhow, “ the nobleman fell down in
a faint through imagining that he had actually
shot the Prince, as His Majesty was then.” This
was either a shocking exhibition of terror arising
from mental grovel, or a really remarkable
instance of presence of mind in a Peer. Perhaps
he deliberately planned Edward’s escape in order
to make the opportuni’/ for swooning. An earl
who could faint at the sight of his monarch’s
bleeding nose would probably get a dukedom for
his intense loyalty.
* * * »
A Victorian railway traveller writes :
The Australian characteristic of weird expectancy is
being strongly developed amongst Victorian railway men.
They are expecting a busy time this harvest; expecting
much from Tait “ when he gets settled down ” ; expecting
better times when Irvine goes, which they expect will be
soon ; expecting good special representatives to turn up
for them in Parliament; expecting to get the franchise
back after all—in fact, expecting anything. They are
only hanging on in expectation of better times ahead.
Amongst the locomotive men the expectancy runs on
particularly weird lines, which, if generally known,
would make the average railway traveller feel creepy
every time he went a journey. They are expecting the
“ lovalists” to smash up things generally as soon as they
are properly let go. Small things occur almost every day,
but not important enough to reach departmental ears.
“ What is wanted is a good smash and a few influential
people killed ” is admitted by all the employees. And
they are expecting it to come very shortly now.
• • • •
The institution of “ Potato Sunday” sheds
a beautiful sidelight on the poverty of the poorest
part of London. Potato Sunday, at St. Mary-at-
Hill, means that every member of the congrega-
tion is invited to bring a big potato —“ the largest
obtainable ” —for the starving and destitute in
the “Labor Homes of the Church Army in
Whitechapel and Ratcliff.” What sort of parish
must it be where the vicar takes up a church
collection of spuds for the benefit of the starving
and destitute in the “ homes” associated with the'
tabernacle!

Anglican Canon Hopcraft, in Adelaide,
has been getting heavily on to “ pinky’’—the doc-
tored and colored wine that is sold at the
reputed quart. He says that it is fortified with
coarse spirit made from the refuse at sugar re-
fineries, and that, “beside it, malt liquor is a
harmless beverage fit for babes.” “Pinky ” made
a prominent and unpopular appearance in the
evidence at a recent S.A. inquest.
N.S.W, recently wandered in silence past
the 106th anniversary of the discovery of Twofold
Bay—where the Federal city’s port will be—by
Bass. Some day some histori an will do justice to
the memories of Bass and Flinders, _ hitherto
badly neglected. Some tin-pot politicians in
effigy occupy niches in front of the Lands Office
while explorers and navigators of worth are
forgotten.

A man rushed into a Sydney newspaper
office, recently, and gave his name in full as the
saviour of one Jack Smith from drowning at
Coogee. It was found out that Jack Smith was
really rescued, but that the man who wanted him-
self advertised as the rescuer was miles away at
the time.
* * * •
“ G.H.” on dangerous ground :
There isn’t much of an attractive nature about the
“ dens of Venus,” as Lawson puts it; in fact, V. is a very
disappointing and disheartening person when discovered
in her lair—or anywhere else, for that matter. Those who
have trafficked much with the lady will agree that she is
mostly an h—less female, with a discordant voice and an
objectionable fondness for beer and scent. (Beer by itself
is all right.) Most Venuses have short careers and quick
ones ; the life is so mind-rotting and so lazy that their
value soon goes down from pounds to pence ; and very
few of her spend a couple of years in the business without
showing-up in the police court with an abraded eye and a
hat like a smothered dream. Once Venus gets to that
level she may be considered passed out; 12 months more
will, in all likelihood, see her dead, or next door to it,
although some tough specimens certainly battle round
for years. There are necessarily a number of Venuses
who are exceptions to the above rules, but they, as a
general thing, don’t have “ dens.” Instead, they have
husbands.

A few items concerning the foreign and
colored friends of the Freetrade dailies, published

During a disturbance on the barque Queen, at Adelaide
on Christmas Eve, the mate was stabbed in the back by a
foreign sailor.
A serious disturbance between whites and kanakas
took place at Bundaberg on Saturday, there being several
hundreds on each side. When the crowds were dispersed
it was found that one of the whites was dead.
As the result of disturbances between Afghans at
Broken Hill two camel-drivers have been seriously
injured.

ance of Ah Shun, Ah Tong, and Lee Sing at the
Water Police Court, where they were fined £lOO
or, in default, three months’ imprisonment for
smuggling opium.

GET DRUNK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the crowding problems hammer on your brain
Their dreary ditty, “ Here we are again ”
When the Future’s only hue
Is absolutely blue,
Here’s a recipe for easing off the strain :
Get drunk,
Hog drunk;
Amble out and hold a revel,
Leave the morrow to the devil,
Get chockful, brimming-over drunk.
When the lady you’d have gambled on has failed,
And Life’s attractions consequently paled,
Leave her to the clever chappie—
You can bet he won’t be happy—
Then, to block the cheerful flow of thought entailed :
Get drunk,
Dead drunk ;
Go upon a mighty scatter.
What the devil does it matter ;
Get whole-souled paralytic drunk.
M.L.
J. BAPB.
WE’RE STILL ALIVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’re still alive, another year
Speeds by, and yet we hang on here ;
And who can tell the reason why ?
Tell why we live and why we die ?
This tangled life is very queer ;
Perhaps we live to mop' up beer.
The long days, passing, mock and fleer,
The long nights hang their lamps.on high,
The pubs, close up and leave us dry.
We’re still alive.
We feel so very old and »ere,
What time the wan years disappi ar
In old-time garb, and pass us by
With lagging gait, and rheumy e; e,
And winking, greet us with a jeer,
“ We’re still alive.”
Tab.

GHOSTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out in the wind and rain
Summer lies dead,
Ghosts tap my window-pane
Joys that have fled,

(Summer is dead!)
Hope with wide eyes of pain
Staring in dread,
Love by Wild Folly slain
Haunting my bed—
“ Kiss and make mirth again 1"
(Summer is dead.)
See, on the stormy main
Flashes the red
Signal of Morn again,
Silence 1 who said
Out in the wind and rain
Summer lies dead?

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 5, 1904.
My Dear Arini,—
Concerning good resolutions : I suppose most
people do make such things whilst the Old Year
is breathing its last. (And break them before the
New Year has had its first wash.) But of what
avail are 'good resolutions in' a place like Sydney
where the North-Easter blows for seven months
in the year ? That wind is the cause of
more demoralisation than original sin. To
do credit to yourself, and to discredit your
neighbors, you must have a backbone, yet the
majority of Sydneysiders crawl around in N.E.
weather with no spine and a conscience like a
squid. All the fault of that wind. Devoted
friends of mine who have been waiting three years
for an answer to their letters will please accept
this intimation to continue waiting until I can go
to live at Monaro. A writing bloque who has
just returned frbm there tells me that all the
winds are carefully dried and purified, and so
compounded that you can toil terribly and yet
think you are doing no more' than gathering pink-
tipped clover. In Monaro.our nor’easter becomes
as the wind that blew out of Creation’s dawn.
(What offers, Monaro, of a humpy for me with a
run for three bantams and a collie dog ?)
If you question a woman about her resolutions
for the coming year, you will find that they are
generally determinations to “save more.” They
have to do with economies in the matter of dress
and fares. She is going to take lessons in dress-
making, and so save dressmaking bills, and walk
where hitherto she has trammed. She will, when
in town, forego the 11 o’clock (a.m.) ice cream and
chocolate eclair, and afternoon tea and cream
cakes—especially if she is beginning to put on
weight. Occasionally you’ll hear of resolves to
lead the higher life, to be not only good but
great. But tread on the train of the resolver’s
dress, or catch her hair with a rib of your sum
shade, and the golden ladder that was being
reared to the stars dwindles to a pair of wooden
steps. Question a man about his good resolutions
and you'll get —chaff One told me he had
resolved to leave undone the things he couldn’t
do. As, for instance, he would not drink any
vodka, because it is not procurable here. Another
said that years ago he had made a good resolution
and kept it steadfastly, and that good resolution
was to make no more good resolutions.
There are rival claimants, I note, in all the
Australian capitals for the distinction of having
been the first woman to vote under the Woman’s
Franchise Act. Homer’s birthplace and the
truth about the battle of Waterloo are also vexed
questions, and will keep the first-woman-to-vote
item in countenance. Miss Vida Goldstein is
credited 'with being the first to cast the long-
haired and transparent-blouse vote in Victoria;
she is said to have waited some time in the rain
for the booth to open and to have entered on the
privileges of a fully-enfranchised citizen with wet
feet. Whether it was really raining at the time
or not I don’t know. But a gentleman named
Smith writes to a Melbourne daily to say that his
daughter, Miss M. J. (Mary Jane, presumably)
Smith, was the first to vote at St. Kilda. She
was in the booth with himself at four minutes
before eight, and had voted and left the booth by
two minutes after eight o’clock. I’ll back this
lady’s claim. She has a name that is as frank as
daylight—Mary Jane Smith, and she has a father
with a stop-watch. But wasn’t there any lady
who burgled the booth at 6 a. in, and thus got
ahead of all competitors?
Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan is well and eloquent
again. The growth on the tip of his Ministerial
tongue was caused by a burn from a cigar, so
among New Year good resolutions of a lot of our
city men figures the resolve —“Don’t put the
lighted end of your smoke into your mouth ! ”
The Minister went through the operation for the
removal of the growth without chloroform, and
his enemies need not rejoice in any sense, for he
is as fit for action as the Jap navy thinks it is.
A friend of mine, an old Tasmanian warrior
whom I’ll call Mulligan, once had an argument
of so serious a nature with a threshing machine
that he had to have both hands amputated.
Mulligan watched the operation standing erect
and with his pipe in his mouth. Six months later
he was sentenced to a term of imprisonment for
violently assaulting the police. Hooks had been
fixed to Mulligan’s foreshortenings and with these
he had given two policemen a terrible mauling.
By analogy the Owe’Sullivan should be shortly
guilty of a terrible verbal assault on Joseph,
What a lot of industrial enterprises demand, in
their inauguration period, a human sacrifice ! The
latest instance is that of the death of young Baird,
one of the managers of the. Manly water chute.
He was a fine-looking man, of oyer 6ft., im-
mensely liked, a noted athlete and cricketer, and
his death was the result of being struck by a
descending boat a few days after the venture was
officially opened. Mr. Baird, the dead man’s
brother, is manager of the sports’ ground,' and it
gives one heartache to hear the unknowing chuters
ask him in cheerful tones “ how it all happened.”
Shooting the chute seems to have caught on with
the public, which likes to take its pleasure at
lightning speed. Boat after boat is filled as fast
as they can be hauled up, and you, standing at the
end of the lake and watching the (townward race,
may get some emotions and amoral from your obser-
vation. Chuters are mostly young men and women;
but I saw one brisk old bird of quite 60, with a
wife of about as many years, snatch the fearful j
joy quite gaily. That’s the kind of human who,
at 60, can eat a breakfast of cold roast bullock’s
heart, and then run for his train and nob drop
dead.
Apropos of chutes, the Baker’s Creek mine at
Hillgrove has a giddy tramway that is just as
good as a chute. Should you ever happen up that
way, the management, if approached in the proper
spirit, will let you voyage in a truck down into
the gorge, where all is gloom, and the sun sets at
2 p.m. You simply spend four or five minutes
spinning down some rails on the side of a rock
wall in a vehicle, and you wonder all the time
what keeps the vehicle from frankly leaving the
wall and dangling at the end of the rope.
“I am not a Scotsman, but I have done my
best to atone for the mistake (of being an Irish-
man) by marrying a Scotch wife.” That, in effect,
said Captain Purefoy, R.N., in his response for
the Navy, at the official luncheon of Highland
Gathering on New Year’s Day. He gave a cue
at which Brigadier-General Finn and Minister
for Education Perry simply snapped. Both owned
to Scotch wives, and when Mayor Hughes, of
Sydney, rose to speak a voice breathed o’er
the banquet, asking, “Haven’t you a Scotch
wife ? ” But the supply of Scotch wife (with Irish
husband) seemed to have run out as a luncheon
gag and there was nothing left but to insert her
in the toast list, and she was enthusiastically pro-
posed by a gentleman who is, perhaps, married to
a lady who hasn’t a sprig of heather in her whole
outfit. Frankly, though, no wonder a husband
thinks much of his Scotch wife. She is clever and
calm, and leads him by the ear as tenderly as
animals are led, and runs his household with
brains and tact. If you want a superb study of a
Scotch wife and mother read a story called
“Generalship.” That’s a Scotswoman’s forte —
generalship.
New gaud Mayor S. E. Lees was “inducted”
last Monday. A number of aldermen and officials,
among them the Inspector of Nuisances, met Mr.
Lees on the Town Hall steps and paid him com-
pliments so hot that the palms and other decora-
tive plants were, scorched. Old gaud Mayor
breathed a fervent “Heaven be thanked,” and
went home to pack his holiday grip.
A siffleuse, Miss Aileen Neill, a Sydney girl, is
attracting some attention locally. Her musical
“mouths” have mostly been “made” in private,
but lately, at a suburban concert, she whistled
Mendelssohn’s “Spring Song” with such effect
that listeners vowed they heard the twitter of
nesting birds and saw the bursting of buds into
bloom. A lady who can whistle buds and blos-
soms should have a future before her.
Members of two well-known squatting families
were attached in matrimony lately at Bris-
bane, when Miss Louisa Frances Nicholson,
eldest daughter of the late Mr. Frank Y. Nichol-
son, was married to Mr. Robert MacMicking,
second son of the MacMickings, of “Marius,”
N.S.W.
There was a butt-end aspect about New Year
races at Randwick. The gowns looked mostly
early 1903, and the wearers had not yet assumed
the 1904 expression of face. Mrs. Forbes-Sempill,
the bride whose husband (the son of a lord !) is
something better than a stoker on board the good
ship Clampherdown, was one of the few women
who had an overture instead of a finale look. She
was in pale-blue crepe de chine, with her fair hair
flanted low on her neck. Two men helped to
righten things—the one by means of bright-
yellow kid gloves, green satin tie, and a General
Booth nosethe other with his trousers turned
nearly -to his knees, so that he might display
chessboard hose.
Miss Sylvia Blackstone gives a farewell concert
at the Town Hall on the 12th inst. The contralto
will be assisted by a strong combination of artists.
Plan for reserved seats is now open at Paling’s.
The matinee hat has long survived well-
merited odium, but the denunciation piled upon
it is as nothing to the language aimed at the
cricket hat. The cricket hat obscures alike the
just and the unjust. It was seen in large num-
bers at the test match, and behind it was
usually some small and purple person—the
color of the person due to struggles to evade
the screen. The fashionable head-gear, as
worn by the Naicest, is large and round and
agricultural. It totters well back, surmounting
a colossal wad of hair, and when the wearer
carries her head erect, the effect is that of a large
Japanese umbrella. The real cricket-girl, of
course, doesn’t wear anything worse than a flat
Panama. The wearers of the agricultural mon-
strosities are invariably those whose ignorance of
the game is abiding and colossal, and who think
that dear Mr. Foster is so good-looking, and that
poor Mr. Warner got bowled because he is bald.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ A quaintly irreligious incident enlivened the
Xmas morning services at St. Paul’s Cathedral,
Melbourne. A very drunk citizen picked his way
carefully along the centre aisle, and at last found
rest for the beery in the vice-regal pew, which
was about the only place that had any seats to
spare. The happy Ohristmaser behaved with per-
fect decorum what time he was taking the bear-
ings of the show, but presently he nudged Lord
Dick Nevill in the ribs and wanted to know-what
the auctioneer (meaning the parson) was ‘ chinnin’
about.’ The evident discomfiture of poor Lord
Dick and the tittering of the neighboring wor-
shippers brought a verger to the rescue. He
closed the incident by a master-stroke of
diplomacy. Beckoning with his forefinger, he
whispered, ‘ Come and have a drink, it will do
you good,’ whereupon the intruder arose and fol-
lowed the heavenly messenger off the premises,
and was seen no more.
“The glory of the State Parliament has de-
parted. The prorogation of the Victorian House,
on Xmas Eve, with the Waxcellency upholstered
up to vice-regal degree for the event, failed to
draw the frivolous. The precious pink cards for
the ‘ dress circle ’ seats, once fought for so gall-
fully, went begging.
“ Speaker Beazley’s cards for tea and cream
puff after the prorogation were a surprise. We
are more used to the Council President doing all
the table honors on these occasions. But Bachelor
Beazley hosted bravely, and begged womenkind
to forget the heat of the rooms, which would
have boiled an egg without hesitation.
“ The decline of feminine interest in the doings
and shows of the local Parliament is a matter
of melancholy reflection to that evergreen
functionary, George Jenkins. Who marks the
flowing silk beard of the Clerk of Parliaments
now? Who throbs at the sound of his musical
delivery of the Prayer ? Who notes the curve and
outline of his portly form in silken hose and court-
dress? George is still sunny-tempered and jests
in the old volatile spirit with the old Legislative
bucks, but fashionable interest is cut off, and the
‘ dear boy ’ feels it. There’s only one thing for
the great Parliamentary Institution of the Past-
the book of reminiscence. What stories could he
put forth, what tales unfold !
“ With sorry eyes behold any ‘ Block day ’ this
holiday season the red-faced, dusty, limp Phyllis
from trie country as she meanders among the crisp
oharmers of Collins-street. She carries much dust,
and her new hat is askew. She knows nought of
the town-girl’s tricks. Does the mere man sup-
pose that spry Maud of the city remains immune
from the winds and the temperature all the stuffy
day ? Not a bit of it. The absolutely new, not-
been-used appearance of the wily one is due to the
mirrors, and the pin-tray, the crimpers, and the
Eower-puff of a boudoir attached to Melbourne s
ig china shop. For a penny—providing she san
occasional purchaser of a pot or pan (but no one
asks questions)—Maud is privileged to enter this
little apartment and get her tongs heated and her
complexion adjusted. Phyllis meanwhile goes on
collecting grit and heat rash.
“Why are there ever any men employed in
ladies’ baths ? Not half the losses of teeth,
switches, or other sundries would happen to mar
Beauty’s holiday if women only were appointed to
dole out the towels and the zebra-marked gown-
lets, and take charge of the detachable items in a
bather’s personality. For to women a woman
may confide these things, but not ordinarily to a
man, though a smart She did create a great fuss at
the baths of a bay resort the other day on discover-
ing that the beautiful chestnut switch which
crowned her top-knot had been stolen. Another
woman would sulk and shed the inward tear.
But not this one. She talked of her switch with
vehement candor. It wasn’t recovered, so for
two miserable days the robbed one lived in an all-
encompassing sunbonnet. Then she took the boat
to Melbourne to replace the stolen treasure.
“Ada Crossley, singing at that huge Exhibition
concert, looked extremely graceful, even when
viewed from a great distance. To appear willowy
and decorative from afar is no small achievement,
because small prettinesses of face or make-up
count for nothing. Jewellery or similar dress-
dazzlements also fail to score. Sweet smiles and
winning ways can only travel a short journey.
Unless a woman has ease and play of deportment,
she bulks as a doll or a wax figure to the straining
eyes of the far-away crowd. But Miss Crossley,
on Saturday nisrht, when scanned from the out-
posts, was pleasing to watch as a tall, white
lily, swaying lightly in the breeze. Her
appearance, indeed, scored more effectually than
her rather tired voice, which just shows that one
never knows which card will come in useful.
Ada’s best friends in the early days can hardly
have anticipated that she might upon occasions
find herself stronger in the suit of Beauty than
that of Song.
“ The mobs of women who flocked to hear A.da
on the cheap audibly predicted all the evening
that their favorite would ‘ break down when she
comes to “ Home, Sweet Home.” ’ The Crossley
‘ tears’have been as distinctive a feature of their
owner’s campaign as Melba’s ‘tempers,’ in their
time. So far as popularity goes, lachrymosity
seems a better line than ferocity. Other ‘ pro-
perties ’ used with success by our two sweet
singers were Melba’s irritated throat, Ada’s dis-
located ankle ; Melba’s stricken-down father, and
Ada’s invalided mother.
“At her last concerts the visiting Contralto
took to posing with symbolic flowers, opportunely
handed along after the early tootles. She would
warble an oratorio strain of great piety with a
duster of church lilies laid on her devotional arm.
“Ada Crossley saves up the hundreds of
screeds, begging letters, and business notes that
flood her rooms while settled for a few days in any
place. She keeps them for perusal during next
train journey. On the trip to Sale one beggar
gave the contralto a genuinely hearty laugh. The
writer opened with a few compliments and season-
able remarks. Then she proceeded to business.
She wanted to hear ‘the gifted of the gods,’ the
‘big-souled singer’ at one of her concerts. She
could afford a few shillings for the treat —but the
dress! She had no dress. And (O, ingenuous
Eve) as she was about the same height, the same
complexion, the same build as the star, one of her
discarded toilettes would be gratefully received.
“ Miss Conway (the pink, plump, dimpled niece
of Argus editor Watterston) gave an ‘at home’
last week in return for numerous attentions
lavished on her since her return from England.
The Writers’ Club, that quietly sc"d institution,
mustered in force —the hostess is on its com-
mittee, and one of the earliest members. Also
welcomed back from the ‘ Big Smoke ’ was Miss
F. F. Elmes (‘ Lino,’ of the Argus).
“The public is painfully amusing. When
George Lauri stifles back the word ‘ D n!’ in
the ‘ Country‘Girl,’ with a desperate pull on him-
self and a look of horror, the audience seems
nearly tickled to death. But when Rose Mus-
grove comes pat out with the wicked swear word
in that slick song of hers, telling the tale of
assorted partners, the house giggles but faintly.
The stage d—-n is something to be hinted at,
like lovely woman’s underclothing. When boldly
disclosed it loses its humor.
“ Mr. Pat M'Caughan only survived his gentle-
eyed wife two months. Two children remain—
Mrs. W. Williams and Mr. Ernest M'Caughan.
W. Williams is the alert young barrister who had „
a seat in the State Parliament for a recent term. '
Mr. E. M'Caughan is a widower, his wife having
been one of the lovely Collins girls of Hobart.
Her death occurred a few months after the
marriage. Since that time Ernest M'Caughan
has spent most of his time aboard his yacht in
Australian waters.
“A great old character passed away during
Christmas week in the person of Mrs. Outtrim, of
Maryborough, mother of the Deputy Postmaster
in Melbourne, of the ex-Minister with the fighting
front and the lamb-like disposition, and otheis
more or less known. At the age of 93, Mrs.
Outtrim played the piano, cracked a good joke,
and wrote a fine, clear signature, with flourishes.
She never outlived her good-humor or her industry.
“ The Melbourne girl hasn’t rushed to Tasmania
this year with the impetuosity and high hope of
former hot seasons. She mostly spreads herself
over the giddy resorts where Jill is surer of her
Jack, and the moonlight music of the waves
brings joy to the hearts of mixed couples—at
Lome and Sorrento, for example, where leading
hotel proprietors go in for skilful manoeuvring
whereby the she abounds in equal numbers to the
he-sojourner. There is extreme wisdom in an
arrangement of that kind.
“ Melbourne’s present Anglican bishop appa-
rently sees no point in leaving the purple and fine
linen of life to the laity. ‘ Bishopscourt,’ re-
cently renovated to suit his notions, was thrown
open t’other afternoon for the inspection of sub-
scribers, whose combined offerings have housed
the Lowther-Clarkes (four in family) in a mansion,
boasting 20 bedrooms, and chic with all the snap-
piest fittings. The drawing-room paper, one of
the new tapestry designs, has alternate panels of
white and palest-green, chosen with a vb-w to
cooling down the Lowther-Clarkes during our
shocking Victorian summers. O laity, laity ! cast
your pale-green paper, your punkahs, and your
cool cellars into the balance, and your tribute will
still fall short of the deserts of your Shepherd-in-
Chief, carrying his climatic cross as if it were a
crown, leading his flock in arid regions, separated
only by several hundreds of miles from the
tropics 1
“Vida Goldstein, airing her ideas in Vanity
Fair (a little Melbourne monthly which struggles
for acceptance by women readers, and is financed
by a drapery firm), said, “ I see no reason what-
ever to doubt that the proportion of women voters
will be as great as that of men voters.’ She
was right in her surmise, but wrong in assuming
that skirted voters, as a rule, would 1 follow the
skirts.’ They disbelieved in Vida to about the
same extent that the average woman disbelieves in
the feminine doctor. Where her own sex is con-
cerned, the woman-worker is not less Conservative
than your finest madam.
“The rumor goes round pertinaciously that a
widowed railway identity is once more a
bridegroom. There’s nothing surprising about
that part of the story, but the lady nominated as
bride introduces into the affair whatever of unex-
pectedness accrues thereto. A staid, elderly
spinster, the daughter of a sound, old church clan,
once well dowered with property of various kinds
—it was vaguely supposed that failing a bishop or
a dean, matrimony for her was branded 'infra dig.
The alleged successful suitor is not much like a
bishop or even a deacon ! The connecting link,
so the story goes, lay in the fact that, like Adam
and Eve of old, the pair were mutually interested
in landed property.
“All the world over, a snob must be a snob,
but on the testimony of travellers, the Australian
snob is one of the worst, thanks to restricted
outlook. A flagrant example was to the fore at
one of the English cricket matches. She was one
of Melbourne’s naicest, and branded all the outer
world as ‘ people one doesn’t know.’ Whenever
she saw a pretty girl, and there were dozens of
them, she exclaimed in her aggressively high-
pitched voice,'‘Just what I always say, the
prettiest girls are nobodies .... people one
v doesn’t know.’

WEARY, WAN AND WORN.
“Four years ago every trace of good health started to leave me,” says Mrs. Warren, 39 Campbell-street,
Paddington, Sydney. “ Every part of my bo ly sufFered with weakness. I couldn’t look at food, and my head
was always heavy and achy. At night I couldn’t sleep, and in the morning I was fit for nothing. I was too
tired and weary even to dress myself, and it was too much trouble to put one foot before the other. My face
had a care-worn look, and my lips and gums were bloodless. Every day T got weaker and thinner. I really
felt that I would never be well, for medicines did not do me a bit of good. At this time my husband was over
in West Australia, and he wrote and told me to get some Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People without,
fail. He said that the people over there swore by them, for they actually made new blood, and were the finest
tonic one could get. There and then I got some from Pattinson’s, and, after taking the third box, I knew I
would get all right. New blood swept through my veins and brought me fresh strength and high spirits. Then
I began to eat heartily, and to feel bright and energetic. In a word, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills gave me new
life My work is now a pleasure, and I take a great interest in everything. Looking at me now, you would
never think that for years I was barely able to crawl about. And all my present good health I owe to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
2s. 9d. a box ; six boxes, 15s. 3d. Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, N.S.W. Accept No Substitute.

At a recent marriage at Eldorado (Vic.)
the ages of the bride’s father and four of her other
relatives present totalled 400 years—an average of
80 years apiece.
Kalgoorlie Sun amused itself by calling
De Largie “half-educated, time-serving, coarse ;
a bullying, brainless, brawny braggart; a windy,
wastrel wag-jaw,” and so on, before the elections :
and then he topped the Senate poll.
Westralia's most successful commercial
woman, who manages a carrying and goldfields-
Btores business from Perth, lately found time to
sit for her second Trinity College musical examin-
ation, passed with honors, and gained an asso-
ciateship.
Of the N.S.W. Senators one is certainly
wealthy—Mary Ann Gould; and two others
are suspected of wealth. The remainder, were
there no Parliamentary pay, would probably be
competitors in private life for a living at about
Owe-Sullivan’s minimum wage rate.
Rowland Rees, ex-legislator and one-time
Minister of Education in South Australia, who
had been an inmate of a hospital for some months,
came out just before Xmas. He was once
reckoned the most eloquent man in the local
Assembly, but most people have now forgotten
him.
Stated that one of the wealthiest of Aus-
tralian bookies lately attempted to “stiffen” him-
self. There comes a time in the lives of all such men
when big feeds, big bottles, fat cigars and buxom
barmaids cease to spell happiness, and, not having
any other intellectual resources to draw upon, they
wish they were dead.
PROPERLY SNUBBED.
“The Acting-Consul General for Japan, Mr. K. Iwasaki, has received numerous
letters, telegrams, and visits front Australian officers, privates, medical men, engineers,
nurses, &c., who saw service in South Africa, offering their services in the event of
hostilities occurring between Japan and Russia. To all letters up to the present time
replies have been sent thanking the writers for their kindness, but Mr. Iwasaki says that,
during the past two weeks, there has been such an increase in the numbers of these
offers that he asks permission to state through the press that, although the Japanese
Government will naturally feel much gratified to learn that the offers have been made,
they will, in his opinion, not be disposed to consider applications for enlistment from
foreigners.”— Daily paper.
(The face in the corner is White Australia blushing.)
“ Here, yellowy! — we'll help you to fight that white ’un!”
“ Thanks !— I want no help from foreigners !”
In a drawing in London Punch, 30th Oct.,
1901, Phil May sketched himself as a patient con-
sulting a sawbones. He asks : “ What would you
think of a warmer climate for me, doctor?” The
reply is—-“ Good heavens! that’s just what I’m
trying to save you from ! ” The sketch is perhaps
the only one in which May drew himself without
a cigar.
The brilliant, but erratic, George Fisher,
M.H.R., once the brainiest man in the M.L.
House of Reps., has just been committed to the
Waitati Inebriates’ Home for the second time.
Fisher, who is now the mere wreck of his former
self, goes under lock and key for 12 months, and,
if not released before then, will have to miss next
session of Parliament.
Recently deceased G. W. Rusden, who
died aged 84, was seeing through the press a work
on Shakspeare at the time of his death. He
landed in Sydney in ’34, and in the 40’s was a
contributor to the Atlas, with Robert Lowe and
Archibald Michie. He retired on a pension 20
years ago. As a hardshell Tory he could not be
beaten even in Victoria.
When Dr. Mackellar joined the N.S.W.
Senators at the close of the last session, five out
of the six were residents of Woollabra munici-
pality. Senator Millen was the exception. Geo-
graphically, the State’s representation was circum-
scribed. Mentally, very much ditto. And with
the substitution of J. P. Gray for R. E. O’Connor
it is more than ever ditto.

Governor Le Hunte, of S.A., has been
still further showing his versatility by driving
a motor-car on a 30-mile trip. No damage was
done ; but he does net nearly approach the record
of the lordly Kintore, who used to drive a railway
engine whenever he got the chance. Kintore, for
some unknown reason, took lessons in the art of
hustling the locomotive when he was young.
George Ernest Roberts, who defeated
Kyabrarn’s candidate for the Richmond (Vic.)
State Parliament seat, vacated by Trenwith, is a
small lean barber with a loud voice. He has
sprinted like a comet into public notice, having
been quite unknown to Melbourne Laborites
eighteen months ago. He is a total abstainer, is
aged about 36, and is the first pledged Laborite to
represent Richmond.
The Gothenburg wreck, about the most
sensational in Australian history, because, with
101 other people, a Judge was drowned, was re-
called the other day by the death of Thomas
Cockrum, the last of the passengers to be rescued
from the water after the ship went down. Cock-
rum was a bushman, with all the bushman’s
horror of the sea; and that was his first voyage
(he had overlanded it to Port Darwin from Ade-
laide) and his last.
It is likely that Batchelor, of the S. A us.
Labor party, will succeed Chanter as Chairman
of Committees in the Reps. —he often acted as
deputy last session and made a success of it.
Batchelor is the only Laborite who has had a long
spell as Minister ; under Kingston, he was in the
Education Department, in which he had begun his
working life as a pupil teacher. Speaker Holder
was also in the S.A. Education Department, and
stayed in it long enough to be well up among the
head-teachers.
The Owe’Sullivan, writing from hospital a
Xmas article for one of the Sydney papers,
moralised :
A brief meditation led me to the conclusion that, in
addition to politics, there are other openings by which
a man may do gpod to his fellows —such as in the cause
of religion, charity, morality, and education, and hence-
forward my efforts will be devoted to these and similar
objects, as well as to politics.
That last phrase “ as well as to politics ” blighted
the hope, aroused by the early platitudes of the
sentence, that the Owe intended to give up the
Treasury lOU and let the London Cohens know
him no more.

Old Colonel
Bell, “the silver-
tongued orator,”
for many years
Consul for the
United States at
Sydney, is a notable
figure in the streets of Wellington (M.L.). He
still wears the broad black felt hat, the ditto frock
coat, and the evening-dress white tie on a low
turned-down collar, and is yet a man that is twice
looked at. Still tall and erect; age is whitening
and shrivelling the once handsome face, and a real
big lie makes him shake visibly. The Colonel
talks of settling down in Maoriland.
The anniversary of the State of South
Australia was celebrated at Glenelg on Monday,
Deo. 28, when the Mayor, H. J. Pearce, held a
reception of ancient pioneers, of whom 300 were
present. Amongst them was Mr. W. Hodges,
the sole survivor of the brig Rapid, who came as a
boy attached to Colonel Light’s staff. Light,
Surveyor-General, arrived in the Rapid on the
18th of August, 1836.

Miss Long, the clever speaker and staunch
champion of Women’s Rights in Melbourne, is of
about the same age and height, and has the slen-
der, willowy figure of Miss Vida Goldstein, but
she has none of the stylish, fashion-plate appear-
ance of her fascinating little compatriot. A
simple-looking young woman of the people, as she
faces her audience in a homely (probably home-
made) pale-green dress and brown chip-straw hat,
Miss Long recalls Anna Dickenson, the Quaker
girl, whose eloquence so materially helped the
anti-slavery crusade in the U.S. 40 years ago.
“ J.A.C.” : Thus Bulletin (24/12/’03):
A once famous whip, in the person of Edward Devine
(“ Cabbage-tree Ned” of the golden days) returned
recently to Ballarat after 36 years on Westralian fields.
Can there have been two ? I knew, 20 years ago,
in Dunedin (M.L.) an Edward Devine, known as
Cabbage-tree Ned, who bore the reputation of
having been a famous Cobb’s coach driver, and
who also derived his sobriquet from his hat. He
was then barman and manager of the bodega
attached to Donaldson’s Glasgow Pie House,
which was a hotel, not a pie house. [The Bulle-
tin also hears of another Cabbage-tree Ned in
Tasmania. Apparently the world is full of Ned.]
Melbourne’s only Marshall Hall now has a
residence just alongside Kew lunatic asylum.
Every morning he receives a visit from a maniac
who regards himself as a goat, and considers it one
of the essentials of his existence to hang his head
over the musical Prof’s, fence and murmur “ Ba-a ”
at frequent intervals. The Conservatorium boss
finds this a great aid to musical study, and
encourages the lunatic by responding “Ba-a” at
the bottom of his stentorian bass voice. A bond
of mutual sympathy has thus been established, and
the Prof, says that he would miss his pet lunatic
very much in the event of that visitor becoming
an alderman, or following out his destiny in any
kindred way.
One of those old pioneers, of whom the
M.L.C. person prates so much, lately handed in his
checks in Melbourne, having reached the end of
his resources. This was Robert Pearson Harris,
who married a neice of Gov. Latrobe, and, away
back in ’53, had held an auctioneer’s license issued
in Sydney by William Lonsdale, treasurer for the
district of Victoria, permitting the licensee to
auction “in the colony of New South Wales and
the town of Melbourne on payment of a fee of
£29 3s. 4d.” A diary found in the dead man’s
possession showed an entry: “Entd. H.M.p.
service Ist Oct., 1840, aet. 15. Arrd. Victoria,
14th Nov., 1851.” A later memo, was more
significant and pathetic, “—— employed new
man. Cheaper and younger. And lam thrown
out of employment.” The old man (he was 78)
was too proud to crawl to Irvine for the measlv
pittance he calls a pension.
“ Marat” writes in disgust:
Sneaked into Sydney School of Arts the other night;
heard that a rebel named Haynes was plotting against
the Commonwealth. Pictured him as a man like the
Duke of Monmouth, and wanted to see him. There were
100 people inside, mostly there out of curiosity, but I
could detect a few solemn-faced men who were support-
ing Haynes. I knew he had no show if he were running
it on solemn lines '; Cromwell was the only man who
could rebel solemnly. A rebellion wants poetry, life and
gaiety. Everybody-was watching the door, afraid the
police might rush in at any moment and arrest us all as
conspirators. Then the boss insurgent came out on the
platform, and I was disgusted. He’s got no appearance to
work up a rebellion on. He’s a short fat man, no pose or no
style and hisface iscomfortableandcireular. Hereminded
me of a Donegal horse-dealer. I like a rebellion, no
matter what the cause is, and I felt real sorry. Haynes
couldn’t stir up a decent dog-fight. If he had hired a
picturesque, dashing man to act as figurehead he might
have made things hum. I tried to see him after the
meeting to propose mvself as figurehead, but the door-
keeper "told me he had fled. He had probably heard the
police w re after him.

At Plymouth and Ports-
mouth, Loth towns of
soldiers and sailors, the
pictures of Lord

those of other generals
over whom London went

were received in frigid

Templeton, one

for the Senate in Vic-

Horace Buckeridge, who made a fatuous;
attempt to get from Maoriland to America in his
marine toy, the Kia Ora (“good luck” —the
irony of it !), and died as the result of accident,
was only 28 years old, but his life was crammed
full of adventure. He treated death-chances very-
cavalierly—looked upon danger with contempt,
in fact. He served in windjammers on the
’Murkan coast, accompanied private expeditions
in quest of gold in New Guinea and North
Australia, put in two years at a pearl fishery,
eighteen months in S'Africa, fighting under
Buller and Methuen and being wounded twice j
joined the Antarctic expedition at Capetown as
laboratory assistant; returned by the Morning;
accompanied Captain Voss in the fragile cockle-
shell Tilikum, paddled over the fearsome
Waimangu geyser with guide Warbrick, and
finally evaded the customs people who wanted to
stop the Kia Ora, and found his grave at last.
Young Sowden, who accompanied Buckeridge,
and who doesn’t know how he ever managed to
return to Gisborne, was formerly fourth officer of
the Niwaru, and is the son of a one-time skipper
of the Delphio. He is only 20 years of age.

OUR MAINSTAY.
THE AUSTRALIAN FARMER (contemplating yield):
“ The Guv’ment gives us the seed, we sets it,
and there’s a glorious harvest. I tell you,
we're the backbone of the country, we are.”
“ H.A.M.” : Was butchering in the coun-
try once, and owned a very intelligent sheep-dog.
He followed me everywhere, and whenever in the
slaughter-yard we happened to be running the
entrails of the sheep to get the fat from them, the
dog would amuse himself chewing the stuff we
had finished with. One day after doing a bit of
this business, he appeared to go a little silly, and
kept whining and running round the yard.
Following morning his tongue was hanging out of
his mouth, and he could not get it back again. I
thought he must have taken a bait, and treated
him accordingly ; but without any good result.
On the second day the tongue was perfectly black.
I opened the poor brute’s mouth, and could see
that although the tongue was black, the root of it
was still its natural color. The third day the
whole tongue fell off. The cause of the whole
trouble was a thin string of gut which had got
round the tongue, stopped the circulation, and
eventually cut the tongue off. After getting rid
of the diseased tongue the dog was as fit as ever,
but it was queer to see him drinking—he would
fill his mouth with water, hold his head back hen-
fashion, and gulp it down.

“ Pyalla ” : Be “ C.O.’s ” remarks on low
wages in southern portion of N.S.W. Eastern
Division (B. 24/12/’O3). Have just walked from
Bega to Sydney, and have come to the conclusion
that the cookies in said district are the meanest
employers of labor in the State. ‘‘Teat-pulling,”
as it is termed in the district, is about the only
work obtainable. The questions, “Can you
milk?” and “How many cows an hour can you
fut through ?” are still ringing through my ears.
f one is an expert milker he is offered from 6s. to
Bs. a week and tucker, which consists of bread,
spuds, treacle and salt meat. As regards the
swagman, he very seldom plods his way along the
South Coast of late. What few I met told terrible
tales of the niggardliness of cookies, specially of
those hailing from the North of Ireland, of whom
there are many. As regards harvesting, the
swagman has no chance. He may obtain a little
“tussocking” at a contract price which pans out
at about 3s. a day. As for the professional sun-
downer, his cake is dough on the South Coast and
he knows it.
• • • •
“ Big Mary ” : Edward S. Sorenson, after
painting a wonderfully vivid and accurate picture
of dairying delights in the Big Scrub, writes in
Xmas Worker :—“And yet the cow is never mis-
called or misnamed like other animals. She is
always a cow, with the most irate milkman—
mostly with qualifications.” Jusso, and quite so.
But other animals (and inanimate objects) are often
maliciously miscalled and malevolently misnamed
in deep dishonor of the cow. I often ask back-out
coachdrivers and teamsters why they call a
troublesome horse “ a blanky cow,” while no one
ever calls a curse of a cow “a blanky horse.” The
answers are rarely complimentary to the cow.
Mulga Mat said : “Itud be fearful complimentary
to call a cow a horse; the worst prad’s bettern th’
best cow.” Even a refractory bullock is con-
temptibly and crimsonly cow-called. The worst
that Australians can call anything, living or dead,
is a cow. N.B.—This is a fair cow of a day—with
a violent dust-storm, flies, heat. Yes —it is a cow.

“ Ess ” : To the correspondent who in-
quired about bitterns:
Australia has five bitterns, of which the little bittern
(Ardetta minuta) of E. Australia is about the smallest.
The small head, with its long, sharp-pointed beak, closely
resembles that of the New
Holland darter (known in
the bush as “the diver.”)
Coloring varies; breast
feathers on some are dirty-
white ; back of neck and
shoulders, chestnut; back,
brown to black. Others are
streaked white and brown on
breast, and streaked chest-
nut and brown on back and
wings. The little mangrove
bittern (Butorides javanica),
of N.S.W. and Q., is a trifle
bigger than a water-hen, and
has a thin black crest reach-
ing over the neck. The
yellow - necked bittern (£.
Gouldi) of N.S.W. is about
the size of the Nankeen night
heron, with a long, needle-
pointed bill. The neck is
also long and streaked with
yellow ; body spotted brown
to black. The big bittern
(. Botauruspceciloptilus)is the
largest of all, and inhabits
Australia, Maoriland and
New Caledonia. The gene-
ral coloring is brown,
streaked and mottled.
These birds are nocturnal,
sleeping through the day
among the reeds and man-
groves. At intervals as they feed about the marshes and
swamps at night they emit a deep, drumming or boom-
ing noise. Some of the rails make a somewhat similar
sound.

“C.A.W.”: Be the laconic Chow. Met
a Chow cook in Riverina wearing a swag and a
look of weird expeotancy. He produced a scrap
of paper bearing the name of a station. On
learning he was miles off the track, he merely
said “damn,” and proceeded to camp for the
night. (N.B.—Haven’t seen a sketch in the
Bulletin of a Chow “ traveller ” yet. This one
had bluey, nosebag and billy.)

“ Spol.” : Surprising is the number of soi-
disant ex-army officers one meets among the
knights of the billy and bluey. Recently met
three in one day, all of whom had “been in crack
regiments.” They all wore corks. One’s gram-
mar left a bit of a doubt in my mind, while
another had the name of his alleged regiment
wrong side up. They belong to a crack regiment
now, anyhow.

“ Sundowner,”
who writes of
Australian
snakes. Some ex-
extracts :
On a snake farm
the buck reptile se-
dulously assists his
doepartnerin bring-
ing up their “ clut-
ches ” of young
snakes. . . . and
helps in the most
intelligent way to
put the budding
brood in the way
they should go. . .
The snake is sub-
ject to many com-
plaints. ... We lost
many on our farm
on the Willondilly,
through what
seemed to be a sort
of reptilian appen-
dicitis. . . .
I had a snake pet
once—a girl gave
him to me on my
birthday, when he
was only about four
feet long—and in
time he grew to
nearly eleven feet.
He was the most
tender-hearted rep-
tile I ever came
across. He followed
me in everything ;
if he saw me laugh-
ing, he wriggled and
squirmed about the
floor, and flapped his tail in glee on anything
that would yield a found. Then, if I sat gloomily
in a chair, thinking of some oncoming drought
or other serious trouble, he would glide up to me
gently, put his tail in my hand—just as your girl or wife
will place her hand in yours when you are worried about
anything—and throw sad glances of sympathy at me out
of his little beady eyes. . . .
That little snake 1 You should have seen his gratitude
when I scraped the sticky web from his snout and let him
breathe freely once more. He crawled round about me,
flapped his tail gleefully on the boards of the floor, and
occasionally crawled over to a tin can that stood in the
corner, and struck his tail wildly against it, by way,
nnparently, of giving loud or musical expression to his
0/. . . .
ouuietimes when he wanted to play and saw that I
had no hairpin handy, he would crawl off to where one
of the grown-up girls was sitting, climb up the back of
the chair, and make a dab at her hair to secure one of
the coveted playthings.

“ Wanderer” tells what happened to him
at Gobbleturkey (or something like that):
I walked to the store and dropped my swag outside,
whereupon a scared-looking girl retreated to the interior,
and, when I entered, she looked as if she would like to
get under the counter for safety. “ I want a pen, miss,”
I said, but she only stared blankly at me, and then dived
under a bag-curtain hanging in front of a door. “He
wants a pen, mum,” I heard her say. “ A what ? " said a
woman’s voice. “ A pen, mum.” “ Oh, ah 1 ’E must be
one o’ thim shearer chaps. Tell ’im we ’aven’t no pens,
nor sheep neither.” “ I don’t mean a shearing pen,” I
explained to the girl; “I mean a pen to write with.”
Again she disappeared, and again I heard the voice:
“ Well, you just tell ’im we don’t keep such things. No-
body don’t never want to write in this town.” Advance

“ Quandong ” writes from Westralia :
Now that the two Grand Old Men of the Aboriginalities
page, M'Oooey and “Scotty the Wrinkler,” have passed
away, I wish to make myself famous in your columns. I
am a drill-sharpener and blacksmith under the spreading
kurrajong, with a bough shed to keep off the afternoon
sun (the kurrajong keeps off the morning rays). It is 38
miles to the nearest pub., and four months since I drank
anything stronger than painkiller, but intend going on
the bender at Christmas at Bulong, or some other big,
lively town. I sharpen drills all day, and my muscles are
strong as fin. hexagon steel bands. It takes me all my
time to get through 45 per hour on my own, week in and
week out, for eight hours a day. You can hear my 22in.
bellows roar, while my mates look in at the open door
and watch the flaming sparks that fly into my eyes, and
theirs too, if they are not quick. I challenge some of
those Kalgoorlie experts to do 70 per hour. One man
says he can do 120, and I am certain such liars will not
be heard singing in Paradise. I defy any man to sharpen
more than 50 per hour for blue diorite. I can only do 45.
I am honest—my brow is wet with honest perspiration. I
earn whatever I can in the shape of overtime, but get £4
a week regular, and look the whole of this district in the
face as have strapped up nothing yet, although owing a
few pounds elsewhere. Sharpening is hard toiling, with-
out much rejoicing. Each morning sees J ton to begin ;
each evening sees it all in the skips ready for the night
•hift. Good-bye 1

“Z.”: On November Bth, 1902,1 hiked from
Horsham to Minyip (Vic.), and counted 78 blades
of grass, 138 dead sheep, 14 dead horses, and
about twice as many that would have been dead
but for the desire to save cartridges. The rest of
the scenery consisted of sand and then more sand.
This year, at the same time, I re-visited the same
place. The grass formed a barrier 4ft. or sft. high
on each side of the track. Not an animal to be
seen—too fat to walk about. Wheat too thick for
the reaper to cut, and a grin on every cocky’s face
like the opening of an incubator.
•. • • •
Meston, without whose permission no
Queensland aboriginal can legally and truly die,
has been counting up bis friends. In 1896 he
thought there were 25,000 of them; but he is
pretty certain now that there were not nearly as
many as that. Last year there were only 8341
who could be reached by the annual blanket, and
he doesn’t think there are more than 5000, or
6000 at most, outside the blanket-area. That
makes a total of 13,000 or 14,000. Seventy years
ago a rough count gave 150,000 at least. At this
rate of going, another 25 years will about see the
end of them.

“ Sparemedays ” : Be strong men. . Con-
stable Horn, of Maryborough (Vic.), once, for a
wager, carried successively 65 bags of wheat from
waggons on to a stack 15 feet high, having to walk
up a steep board to the top of the stack. I saw it
done. Let any man, even if accustomed to carry-
ing wheat, try this feat without resting between
times (Horn went straight through with it) and he
will see the size of the business. Horn once
offered to carry lOcwt. of flour, strapped round
him, 500yds., but was too well known to be
taken on.
THE BALD-HEADED MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were liars in the bar
(As there generally are
When the pub is not deserted
And the beer is flowing free).
They were telling yarns of luck,
And of heroes and of pluck,
And I heard them all politely.
Then I thought, “ It’s up to me 1 ”
So I started in to tell
Little yarn I knew quite well,
And although it wasn’t truthful
It was true as all the rest.
And they seemed quite satisfied
When I graciously supplied
All the details that they asked for—
They were really quite impressed.
Then a silence like a pall
Fell upon those liars all
As they brooded o’er the story
That had knocked their fiction flat.
Till a small bald-headed man
Rose and murmured, “ Well—you can
Ask me anything you like, sir ! ”
And the small man stroked his hat.
“ Now, you said you heard a scream,
And there, in the foaming stream.
You observed a maiden drowning,”
Said the small man who was bald.
“ Then you swam quite half-a-mile,
And you saved her with a smile,
And her father gave her to you
On the next night when you called.
“ Now I think I heard you say,
In a quiet and modest way,
That it was in February
Of the year one nine nought two
That you did this noble deed ? "
“ Yes,” I answered, “ if 3'ou need
Any proofs I’ll gladly get them ;
But—the story is quite true !’’
But the small man bowed his head.
“ That will do, my friend,” he said,
As he wandered towards the doorway
With a deep, heart-breaking sigh ;
“ I would really be afraid
To—er—doubt you got the maid.
But —the river where you did it
At that period was dry ! ”
Straight I seized a vacant chair
And I flung it through the air ;
But the bald one wisely vanished,
And was never seen again.
If we ever chance to meet
I will slay him in the street.
Now—l tell the truth or nothing ;
But I hate bald-headed men.
N.S.W.
KODAH.

“Ciro”: From the des-
cription of the night sounds
heard by “J. Carew” (B.
1.7/12/’O3) I would say they were
bittern booms right enough.
The bittern of southern Victoria
is of a brownish color, something
between the eagle and sparrow-
hawk, with a wealth of feathers,
especially on breast and neck’
which make it look as big as a
101 b. bustard, but it is in reality
only half the weight. In swampy
country along the coast between
Williamstown and Geelong they
were very numerous in the
seventies, and their drum was a
familiar sound to me for many
years. It is impossible to locate
them by sound—they are the
ventriloquists of the bird tribe,
and are as elusive by day as by
night. They are said to be
related to the crane family, and
their wading habits are similar.
They seldom or never come out
in the open, and with the aid of
the best dogs I found them diffi-
cult to put up. On watch, all
that can be seen of them is the
head with bill stuck straight up
—like Governor Clarke’s chin —
just above the rushes where they
abound. They are only to be
found singly, and when you are
recognised as an enemy, they
duck down, and, in a crouching
fashion, disappear as swiftly as
a land-rail. They show great
fight when wounded. In those
lulled I noticed the larynx was
always extremely large, and the
cavities of the throat very broad
and open.—[A very reckless
correspondent suggests that the
booming sound “John Carew ” inquired about
was the howl of the porcupine—perhaps blowing
through its quills.—Ed. B.j
THE LATE G. W. RUSDEN .
Australian historian and many other things.
Photo, by Maull & Fox, London.
“ Hiphen ” on contrasts :
It was one June evening in Paris that I saw a gendarme
hook a man out of the Seine at the end of a long pole.
It was a touching sight when the rescued Frenchman
recovered sufficiently to thank his preserver, who vai
throwing chests close handy. He hurled himself on to
the uniformed bosom, flung his arms round the gold-
braided neck, kissed his rescuer on both cheeks several
times, sobbing and babbling hysterically the while. The
dignity of the scene was somewhat marred by the
gendarme’s anxiety to keep his beautiful uniform dry.
Still he seemed to enjoy it, and after a few minutes of it
I had to go and have an absinthe. . . . Near Bairnsdale
(Gippsland) I saw a cocky pull a mate out of the
Mitchell at the risk of his life. They both stood dripping
on the bank, looking somewhat sheepish. “Damn
cold!” observed the rescued man. “My oath,” mut-
tered his rescuer. Then in stolid silence they adjourned
to the nearest pub to have a drink—the rescuer paying.
To all curious queries as to the moistness of their
condition, they preserved a stony silence. Outside they
shook hands with eyes averted bashfully. “So long,”
said one. “ See yer Sunday,” said the other. Then
they parted, looking very- uncomfortable and self-
conscious. But there was more in that hand-grip outside
the bush hotel by the Mitchell than in all the frenzied
embraces by the Seine. That was a twelvemonth ago.
When another few years have passed, if both are alive,
some further casual remarks may be made about that
wetting—certainly not before.

“ M.G.” : Concerning the slipshod methods
Of the average Australian cooky. Travelled from
Melbourne to Wangaratta last week
witli a successful Gippsland farmer,
about to extend operations to the
Northern areas. He related a Gipps-
land experience. Having used ensi-
lage with profit, he induced his next
door neighbor to experiment. A silo
was built and filled, but when the
time came for using its contents the
neighbor announced that when he
opened it he found all the ensilage
s \ rotten. He added that he knew
if all along that the thing was no
v, good, and blamed the other for
putting him on to such a notion.
Five years passed, and the
stickler for antediluvian methods
met the usual fate of his kind, and
was sold up, his farm being pur-
chased by my travelling companion.
In getting things into order, latter
determined to clean out the old silo-
for further use. Opening it up and.
scraping off the outer portion of the
abandoned ensilage he found inside
the whole of the contents sweet and
fit for use after six years waiting.
The original owner had simply looked:
at the outside and jumped to the
conclusion that the quality was the
same all through.
* *' *
“D.H.R.”: At Walgett(N.S. W.)
there is an artesian bore which was
put down two years ago at a cost of
a few thousands. The water from
the bore is flowing to waste ; in one
place into the river, which is almost
a banker. The other night a eon-
. . Bagration destroyed a large hotel and
a printing office and machinery. A few yards;
of hose, with the water laid on, would avert all
such calamities. The people hold meetings and.
discuss things, and decide to reticulate the town,
with bore water. Then they go home and sleep,
and wake up and wonder what day of the week it;
is.
* * * *
Q.” : After the authorities had fixed up a.
sensible close season for black duck, the down
south so-called sports worried flabby Bent until:
he altered the opening day back to Dec. 27th,
within a week of the old slaughter-time. In a
couple of years a black duck will be as rare as the;
moa, but Bent’s tribe appears to be increasing,,
worse luck !
* * * *
Wonderful are the ways of the West..
The tin and bagging metropolis of White Cliffs,,-
where they used to bring in mud on camels and
sell it at 3d. a look, has been dropped on by a water-
spout or f something equally wet, and the remains- ‘
of the place had to be gathered up in a jug. A
Broken Hill coach has been washed away at a°plaoe;
where there had been nothing but sand for ten;
years. There have been drowning accidents at.
the Barrier, which was lately getting its water by
train. Various people have been bushed in the
grass, and it is impossible to find them except by
cutting down the vegetation with axes. A
new grass-climbing dog is being invented
to hunt for lost prospectors, and horses are
standing up on their hind legs to see over
the top of the verdure. And a new class
of western writer is springing up—the one whose
hero is bogged on the Old Man Plain, and is
rescued a.t the last moment by Bill with a lifebelt
or by Jim swimming across the desert, after
which they are married and live happily ever after
• —with a boat tied to the front verandah in case of
emergencies. This hero and his wife have ten
children, who grow up to be the best swimmers in
Milparinka.
* * * *
The one hufc and the solitary public-
house on the Broken Hill-Menindie route are
now praying hard for dry weather. The mail
coach gets in two or three days behind time ;
the drivers state that roads have not been in such
bad condition, or creeks so treacherous, since
1892. Along the river, around and below Menin-
die, the accumulation of many years of sand has
been washed away, exposing aboriginal and other
interesting curios. On the site of one of the old
Burke and Wills camps the Govt, official collected
several mementoes of the explorers—relics that
had been hidden for over 40 years.
A buah-puh. episode:
The tall man had just finished relating his pet atrocity
to the guileless selector from Dingo Flat.
“Don’t look like a cannibal, do I?" he asked, insinu-
atingly, and the cocky gazed into the depths of his beer
and shook his head. Then the tall man proudly wiped
the froth off his draggled moustache and told it all over
again.
“Yes, as I tell yer, we was overlaifdin’ in ’B2, an’
some’ow we got off th’ track an’ lorst in th’ ranges. We
’ad only a few days’ tucker left, an’, at th’ larst when we
couldn’t stan’ it any longer, we draws lots an’ I won an’
’ad to eat me pore ole mate 1 ”
The cocky lit his pipe again for the fifth time, spat
doubtfully on the floor, and shook his head. The tall
man noticed the action and fiercely accused him of
doubting the story.
“I believe yer!” murmured the cocky-, soothingly.
“ I know yer tellin’ the truth 1 ”
The tall man seemed dazed, though pleased. Putting
a grimy hand on the trustful one’s shoulder, he patted it
caressingly. “ How’s that, ole boy ? ”
“Becos!” muttered the dreamer from Dingo Flat, aa
he watched a soaked fly drowning in some dregs. “Becos
I was th’ man yer eat! ”
Then the fight began.
MRS. O’HOOLEY’S MISAPPREHENSION.
MR. B. SEEDY: “ Why are you waitin' here so
long, Mrs. O’Hooley?”
MRS. O’HOOLEY: “ Sure, an’ didn't that po-litical
chap tell me to come to the poll early? But
divil a one o’ me knows what to do now I
am here."
THE DYING SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLRTIN.]
The sunset filled the western sky with splendor,
A glorious end to Spring’s last dying day,
Low, light clouds trailed their rosy garments slowly
Through halls of clearest azure, far away,
Until the light departed sure and swiftly,
Leaving the afterglow all chill and grey.
Only a few large stars were gleaming brightly,
A half-moon looked from out a darkening sky,
A gentle breeze was stirring very lightly
Among the crimson roses climbing nigh,
As Summer softly stepped, with half-closed wing,
Into the palace of the dying Spring.
vio.
KATULSEN WALKER.

Ben Bolt: The Bulletin grew tired of the pre-
sent N.S.W. Labor members because they quite forgot
their democratic principles. That’s all. And you ask if
it would have them put the Oarruthers crowd into power ?
Do you think they must always be putting one incapable
or another in power ? Let them reach out for power
themselves, get a sane, vigorous, solvent financial policy,
throw out See and co. by their own vote of censure, and
try if they can’t attract enough progressive men to give
them a majority. If they can’t, let them go into direct
Opposition, and make an effort, with a decent financial
policy, to carry the country at next election. They spend
their time in condoning an awful waste of public money,
and in return they get no concessions nowadays—except
the Fitzroy dock scandal... .Zizi: Sorry none quite up.

VULCANA, Rickards’ New Strong Woman.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney & Melbourne.
“ The fire of speech becomes the ashes of
cold type.”
At a ,sale held lately at Cobbora (N.S. W.),
a 32-year-old horse brought £l4 10s. The old
moke became famous some 28 or 30 years ago by
swimming the Talbragar during a heavy flood,
and has lived on that reputation ever since.
On the premises of an East End, London,
dealer 6,749 rotten eggs were recently found. The
ingenuous proprietor explained to an incredulous
and unsympathetic magistrate that he used them
to clean his children’s heads. One month, hard !

Business, Robbery, etc.
“ Nym ” : A little-known industry is that of the
itinerant old-stamp buyer. Struck one of the gentry this
week, and he assures me he makes handsome profits in
“ trucking ” his wares—he generally hawks jewellery or
books as well—for philatelic treasures off old envelopes,
deeds, &c., as to the value of which the owners are gene-
rally ignorant. In mining townships of the “ has been ”
order there is treasure-trove for the wandering stamp-
collector, especially in the hamlets that had a fleeting
importance in the roaring days. A jewellery-hawker
once struck a patch at an old mining town police-station,
securing the stamps from long-forgotten documents,
to the value of £72, for a 12s. 6d. gold brooch for the con-
stable’s missus. Many country postmasters take up the
craze, which has many “wrinkles.” Every sheet of
stamps has one on the sheet, or maybe one on a line,
different in some slight detail, which is the Government’s
safeguard against forgery, and sometimes these have a
value. There remains a wide field fit to be “ fossicked ”
over by itinerant philatelists. The old agricultural
settlements of N.S.W., such as those in the “ Oxbury ’’
and Cowpasture districts, should be worth exploring;
ditto the Vic. Western district—all the places, in fact,
where oldest inhabitants grow.
*mi • * * *
“C.C. : The simple Tasmanian farmer appears
to be exporting a larger quantity of his tight little island
than usual this season. Recently had a washing up of a
bag of “ Circular Heads,” which panned out exactly 441 b.
of potatoes to the cwt. The rest was dirt. This explain*
why the North-West Coast spud fakirs drive in 50-guinea
buggies and record their votes in favor of economy and
reform. A recent importation of 160 bags of “ oat shells,”
ex Wakatipu, from Devonport (Tas.), also clears up the
mystery of a bag of Tasmanian oaten chaff, in which
present writer could find thousands of “ shells,” but
nary an oat. Good old “backbone of the country!”
There is one man who always chuckles with joy when the
primitive bush-spieler is taken down by a clever city
brother, for there are no redeeming features about the
brutally inartistic swindles of selling “shells” for oats
and mud for potatoes.
* * * •
How is this for sweating? In the Sydney
branch of a huge English softgoods firm the following are
the rates of pay :—The head-warehouseman, who is ac-
countable for all stock and the execution of orders (prac-
tically the manager) receives under £2OO a year. The
accountant, a highly-qualified man, draws but £lOO a
year ; whilst the rest of the staff earn from 7s. 6d. to 20s.
a week. The “figurehead” who is responsible for this
scandalous parsimony draws £l5OO a year. In any decent
softgoods house in Bradford, Manchester, or London
doing a similar amount of business (in fact, in any of the
English branches of the firm in question) the salaries paid
would be twice or three times as much. In the case of the
accountant, indeed, five times the amount would not be
considered extravagant. Yet there was a time before the
Big Debt came, when young Englishmen shifted to Aus-
tralia to “ improve their positions ! ”
* * * *
“Nym” : Some foreign firms, have been build-
ing up a large and profitable Australian trade in watehes.
The modus operandi has been to swamp a district with
circulars advertising the merits of certain high-class
•sporting and repeating watches at from six to 40,guineas.
Enclosed with the batch of circulars is a coupon, having
upon it the name of Mr. Bill Plodd or Mr. Jim Cow-
whacker (taken from some municipal voting roll or other),
which states that a watch like the splendid and valuable
centre-seconds chronograph, only in a less expensive
case, has been reserved especially for Mr. Bill Plodd or
Mr. Jim Oowwhacker, and will be sent on receipt of a
post-office order for 12s. 6d. Dozens of people rush to be
first at the post-office counter to send away the 12s. 6d.
for the reserved watch. Three months later they get a
letter apiece from the Customs to the effect that there is
a packet at the office on which there is ss. duty to be
paid. The dues roll in, and each glad recipient finds he
has sent all the way to Bunkum for a low-class watch,
inferior to the kind the local horologist ha* been selling
for years at 10s. 6d. duty paid—cost ss. 3d.
IN THE DAYS OF PROHIBITION.—TALES OF A GRANDFATHER.
“Ah, boys, them was the days. It was a liquor like nectar is — Beer, I think they called it;
you could buy it like milk, as much as a quart-pot would hold, for 4d.”
“Hairy David”: I have described my initia-
tion into the Melbourne Beggars’ Association, and why I
became paralysed in Prahran, or rather in my right arm,
and so I will now relate how it was I came to throw over
the Association run by the scoundrelly ex-everything.
There was a copper in the force up my way who made
it his business to annoy us street beggars, by telling
people who were likely to give us a “ tray ” that one of
us wasn’t paralysed at all, t’other wasn’t blind, and
another didn’t have a wife and sixteen starving children,
and so on. He was telling a lady some sort of yarn one
day about Blind Johnny, and Blind Johnny saw him and
said something very sarcastic (I forget what it was
anyhow, he’d been saving it up for a long time to throw
at the cop.), and the lady laughed, and the cop’s cheek
went as red as his beery nose, and he looked like
an ass in a uniform. But he had his revenge, and
Blind Johnny was put away for a fortnight for vagrancy
not long after. We other fellows suffered too, for when-
ever we took up a stand P. O. Murphy would bump us
into the gutter We held a meeting and discussed our
grievances, and proposed remedies. Someone suggested
writing to the papers ; another that the authorities be
requested to transfer the cop to some other district; but
both suggestions were negatived. Then I rose and
suggested that someone should wipe the ground with
him, and that the Union should pay the fine. It went at
that, and the ex-everything, who was chairman, tore up
a number of slips of paper, and numbered all but one of
them, borrowed my hat, and —well, I drew the blank. It
took me a long time to make up my mind to tackle that
copper, but one day he came along in his usual
bouncing style, trod purposely on my toe, and pushed
me into the gutter. I raised my paralysed arm and
smote him fair on the nose, and ran —into another
copper. I was tried. The ex-everything Johnny and-all
my friends were in court, and when I pleaded “Not
guilty” they cheered till the sergeant ordered “ Silence
in the Court.” But when I pointed (not with my had
arm) to Murphy, and denounced him as “ a dirty, low-
born son of an Irish scavenger,” my friends in the gallery
again cheered and stamped their feet. Regardless of
the sergeant’s cry of “ Order ! ” they continued to make
a noise, till they were all ordered out of court. I almost
fainted’when I got 14 days without the option, cracking
stones- surely thev must have forgotten my paralysed
arm When I cam e out I asked the ex-everything for the
money subscribed for my fine, and he refused to give it
to me : so, being desperate, I wiped the floor with him,
and resigned from the Union.
Not every woman could show such presence of
mind during a fire scare as did a Maorilandress recently.
When the alarm rang late one night she gave a hasty look
around, grabbed her purse, and raced down the street in
her night-gear and her bare feet to wake up the insurance
agent and pay her overdue premium. The agent pro-
tested, and said he supposed the outbreak was in her own
house. But it wasn’t; her next door neighbor was
getting roasted.
