“ Ku-Klux ” : I had always believed the old
saying “ the camera can’t lie," but lately I am beginning
to doubt it. Canvasser for enlargements called at our
place the other day. Wife showed him an old photo,
which she wishes enlarged, and he offered, for a consider-
ation, to put a gold chain round the neck, gold bangle on
the wrist, and do the hair in the very latest style. He
would have put a crown on the head for an extra 6d.
* * * *
One of the industries of the poor in England is
mending nutmegs. Worm-eaten nutmegs are taken
from the factories, and mended with nutmeg-dust, mixed
with glue or gum. When mended they are carried back
to the factory. Threepence-farthing a gross is paid for
mending nutmegs.
* * * •
The latest successful small swindle in country
towns is worked thus : Well-dressed man arrives and puts
up at the chief hotel. He inquires for letters, but there
is none. Next day a letter comes, and Blank opens it in
presence of the publican ; it contains an authority from
a Sydney wool firm for Mr. Blank to buy skins up to
value of £lOO, and encloses several of the firm’s printed
cheques for various amounts in payment. Then he begins
buying, giving, for instance, a £4O cheque for £25 worth
of skins, and taking vendor’s cheque for the balance, £l5.
A couple of days later Blank disappears. Sometimes he
gets away with the skins and the change ; sometimes he
drops the skins in his flight, and gets away with the
change only. So far he has always got away with some-
thing.
» * • •
“Myrtle”: Recently ran across a man (just
out of gaol) who had for some time done good business
in the corset line. Under the guise of a woman he took
corset-orders to measure for the head firm, and every-
thing went well till he got on the razzle one night.
Next morning he laboriously got into his woman’s gear
and went forth to measure the fair sex for its new corset,
but in his confused state he forgot to shave. A lady with
very visible whiskers was arrested that forenoon at a back
door, where he was talking in a thick voice to another
lady, and urging her to let him see her present corset, so
that he could point out its many defects.
* * • *
“ J.F.D.”: The ways of the man who retails
bananas “ eighteen in a bag for sixpence ” are as crooked
and darksome as a side street in the catacombs. Thought
I would invest threepence, a few nights back (the banana
is very effective in filling a vacuum), and I keenly
watched the retailer count them into the paper bag. He
grabbed three and jerked them in. “Six,” he shouted.
“ Hold on,” I said, “ you only put in three.” “ Betcher
threepence I put in six,” he said. “Done,” said I, pro-
ducing a “ tray.” Then he upended the bag and out
came the three he had put in while I was looking and
three mouldy, diseased bananas that a respectable hog
would refuse to chew. “ ’Ad yer that time,” he said as
he pocketed my threepence ; “ got all my bags fixed up
like that. ’Ad an old bloke same way last night.” I
walked away and he roared out, “ The old chap selling
mangerines is on the same lay.” I only had a penny left
so 1 bought a scone. They don’t give you a bag with one
scone ; if they did, I suppose it would be a case of nine
scones and three rocks to the dozen.
* * » *
Strange how some people rush an alleged
imported article, and are prepared to pay a fat price for
it on the mere say-so of anybody who happens along with
something to sell. A travelling genius managed to per-
suade a Balclutha (M.L.) man to give him £1 u for a couple
of fowls (Wyandottes) on the represent.",t.on that they
were imported from ’Murka, and had crowed under the
tar-bangled Hannah or whatever the flag is called.
Turned out that they were bought from a neighbor at
ss. each, and the victim secured judgment for the return
of his cash.
“S.”: The well-known (in England) and
exposed Paris portrait dodge has become rea-
sonably common in Australia. A few days ago
I received a printed (imitation typewritten) cir-
cular from the Society Artistique de Portraits, 22 Rue
. de Turin, Paris, with “ the following bona fide offer”
“ On receipt of a photo, of yourself or any member of
your family, living or deceased” (an artistic touch),
“ we will make you from same one of our finest life-size
crayon portraits entirely free of charge.” All that is
asked in exchange is that you show the portrait to your
friends. The circular adds that the society’s object
is .to put artistic crayon portraits “within the
reach of almost anyone,” and a neat and persua-
sive clause says that, in order to introduce them-
selves they are making these free portraits for
a limited number of “ prominent people.” The signature
to the curious circular is “ A. Tanqueray, Directeur.”
In these sort of cases there is no better antidote to the
temptation than the old Scottish proverb, “You get
nothing for n'othing—and very little for sixpence.” Even
the picture at the head of the circular, which shows
three long-haired artists working at portraits—with
palette and brush, and not with crayons—did not tempt
me. I am not particularly fond of seeing my own, or
anyone else’s, photo ; and had a vision of a letter being
received later on asking for a remittance of £2 2s. to pay
for “ the magnificent frame,” which alone could do justice
to my unusual and extraordinary countenance.
* * * •
“J.F.D.”: We are a people at the mercy of a
painted recording machine, which stands on the verandah
or in some corner of the cellar, and silently records any
darned way it pleases the gas we consume or don’t con-
sume, and laughs to itself at our inability to tell whether
it is fast or slow. I think I hear the meter snickering as
I write. If I think ic too fast, I can have it tested by its
owners, the Gas co. If the co. likes to admit that |I am
right it will make a small allowance, which it says is
sufficient; if it chooses to say I am wrong I pay all
expenses. I have never any guarantee that the meter
isn’t the most stupendous lie, fraud, and short-weight
machine in the country. When will some city councillor
howl for an independent inspector. We have sleuth
hounds tracking the grocer and hawker who sell under-
weight, but the machine that regulates our gas bill is
not amenable to law, and if it gives short measure our
only appeal is to the co. that supplies the short measure.

“Mirumbeck”: Talking about opal buying—
the finished article, that is—women buyers down South
won’t look at the creamy opal, with the gleaming flashes
AT THE PARTY.
KIND LADY : “ Oh, the dear little fellow, won’t he come and sit on my knee? ”
UNFORTUNATE CHILD: “Bohoo! Don’t wanter sit on you knee. You’s sittin’ on me tart.”
of red-fire, that costs money. They prefer the inferior
greeny-blue tinge. But the best to sell is the faked opal—
inferior stuff like glass, with a piece of colored foil put
beneath the setting to give it lustre. The unique colors
this fake is capable of would shame the rainbow, and
women like it! Lots of superstitious Australians won’t
wear opals at any price, although they will buy the gew-
gaws to give away as presents. I have had to take
dozens of opals out of brooches because the wearer*
attributed their bad luck —the loss of a sweetheart, child,
horse, or cow, or an accident, to the unoffending gem.
The main cause of superstition regarding the unluckiness
of the opal is because the gem is so easily chipped, and
so difficult to set securely owing to its slippery nature
when cut. Once a stone falls out of an Australian girl’s
ring or brooch she considers the banshee of all things
evil is treading on her skirt-tails. Season in and season
out the diamond is the fashionable gem throughout the'
Common wealth. The ruby is a close second, with the
amethyst and turquoise a long way behind—last.

“Nymsie” : My grocer has just handed me an
elaborate colored card setting forth the merits of an
imported condensed milk. It embodies a picture of the
dairy where the cow that presumably supplies the milk
hangs out. The dairy is paved with cobble stones, and
there is a stream of some fluid trickling along the floor.
The place is largely occupied by cows, bags and dogs. I
am “ off ” this brand henceforth ; there are too many
dogs in the dairy. I am not taking any risks with my
milk, for however essential to punctuality in the milking
arrangements dogs may be on a milk-farm, they have no
right howling around the cow-yard and inspecting the
milk-pails as in this “ ad.” The Australian tinned milk
has my patronage till it puts dogs, cats, or snakes on the
label—then I’ll abstain from milk altogether.
The Inky Way.
0.0.0. : The late William Curnow had many attractive
features in his character. A well-known verse-writer—
like all Australian verse-writers, fearfully hard up-
dropped into the S.M. Herald office on the afternoon of
31st. Dec., ’9B, with some suitable verse for the New
Year. Ournow received him with his matchless com-
bination of dignity and courtesy, took the MS. and the
scribe’s explanation with some dignity, and told him to
call back about 10 p.in. Ten o’clock and the anxious
poet arrived together, and at Ournow’s first word the
scribe’s hopes rose. “Yes, Mr. Blank,” said the editor,
majestically; “I shall accept your contribution. The
verses are very smooth—very fine— unusually good,
indeed!” The scribe bowed his thanks, and went over
to Pfahlert’s for a pint of Bass. Next morning early he
seized the S.M. Herald and searched for his New Year
effort. No sign. Nor next day—nor the next. But
lo ! on Jan. Bth out came the verses, and the despairing
bard—who then had no idea of the leisurely
methods of S.M. Herald journalism—took heart of grace
and advantage of the introduction, and wrote two or
three more or less literary articles which were accepted.
At the end of the month he saw Ournow again, asked
about remuneration, and was told to send in Ills account
and charge 30s. per column for all matter that had
appeared. The New Year verses had just turned a half-
column. Therefore the scribe hinted at some specific
payment for them—“say a couple of guineas, Mr.
Ournow.” Curnow gazed at the bard severely. “ Mr.
Blank,” he said, “we never pay for poetry. The Sydney
Morning Herald does not often print verse, no matter
who the writer may be. We consider it sufficient honor
and reicardfor any young man to have his verses printed
in the Herald ! ” The astounded and impecunious bard
looked so disappointed that the old man presently re-
lented. “ Well, Mr. Blank,” he said, “ I cannot break
our rule, but you may charge for the poem with the rest
— but only as space /” By the way, how many papers of
standing in Australia don’t “ pay for poetry”?

“ Scribo ” : The first Australian newspaper, The Sydney
Gazette and N.S.W. Advertiser, was issued on sth
March, 1803. It gave the date of the commencement of
the colony in 1788. A rude little wood engraving, repre-
senting a ship, with a Union Jack, and an allegorical
female seated on the shore, adorned the top of the first of
the four pages of foolscap. The latest intelligence from
England was that of a dreadful fire at Woolwich on 20th
May, 1802 1 Many of the issues contained no local or
colonial news ; and suspension of publication was fre-
quent, owing to the irregular supply of paper. At one
period the editor complained that he was reduced to the
necessity of printing the Gazette on China paper, “ which
doubles the labours of the printer, as only one side can be
used.” Then both type and ink gave out, and an illegible
issue was apologised for. When subscribers fell into
arrears, as nearly all of them did, the Governor wag
appealed to, and “His Honor” issued a proclamation
recommending punctuality in the discharge of newspaper
debts. Would that “ His Honor ” would do that now 1
* * * •
The chief difference between the two Adelaide papers
is that the Register is always pretending to be more
Conservative than it is, and the Advertiser is always
pretending to be less. At bottom there isn’t much
radical difference between them. There are miles of
difference, though, between the two men who supply most
of their brains. W. J. Sowden, of the Register, is a man
of affairs, who likes to knock about, and be seen aDd
known ;he is hardly anything of a literary man. He
began as a country compositor, and, though still young,
is now a partner in the Register. There are not many
days that his name is not in the paper in connection with
something or other. Fred. Robertson, of the ’Tiser, is,
on the other hand, a literary recluse. If he has any
knowledge of business, it is only theoretical. Few know
him his name is hardly ever in the papers, and he
blushes violently whenever it does get there. He is so
nervous that he could not speak six words in public,
while Sowden is happiest on his feet—he is probably the
best speaker among working journalists.
* * * *
“ H." : I invite contributions towards a fund for sup-
plying Norman Lindsay, Alf. Vincent, and half-a-dozen
other Australian artists with one axe, one pick, one
spade, one hammer, and one shovel a-piece. When N.L.
starts out to draw an axe, or other domestic weapon, he
makes the most awful bludgeon of it imaginable; and
Vincent and the others run him very close. If each had
a set of the above supplied to him, there would be no
excuse, and Australian black-and-white work would get
a big technical lift. “ Hop.” alone depicts the imple-
ments of everyday life with fidelity. Why is it, by the
way, that a reaper-and-binder, or a plough, or a set of
harrows, or a threshing machine never figures in a Bul-
letin drawing? [Principally because they are not
required.—Ed. B.]
* * * *
Bits from a Barrier boy’s examination essay on news-
papers :
“ The business of a newspaper is to report the thousand
and one things which are known to be going on in the
world, and also the things without it, as the fall of
comets, stars, and any other such sensational aerial
proceeding or happening. ... A war correspondent has
the pleasantest duty out of all the newspaper business.
He is a happy fellow; that is, of course, before he is
knocked on the head. He may even be happy then. It
all depends. He is generally expected to be in the thick
of the fray, wherever he is, and that’s where he is if he is
a Britisher. . . . Somewhere I read a story of a war
correspondent, who, when his part of a battalion was
suddenly surprised, was seen calmly sitting on his horse,
calmly jotting down notes. I don’t know whether
this is true or not. The correspondent was a Russian.
His name ended in ‘ ‘ off.” . . . Reuter’s message is some
kind of telegraphy specially used for newspapers. Ido
not know much on this subject, nor much on others
either. ... An editor is the most important man in the
town, except, of course, the Mayor. He often has great
trials and wrongs to fight. Often this is very trying to
the editor. He loses his health, and very often he has to
go away for a trip to better his health. An editor has
been known to commit suicide. . . . But the pay of an
editor is very great. His residence, especially if he has
charge of an important paper, is not unlike a million-
aire’s, on a small scale.”
* * * *
lieightley Snowden, in September London Magazine,
gives some facts re London Times. Its style in the first
half of last century was distinctly Nortonesque. A
sample of Billingsgate, addressed to the Irish agitator,
William O’Brien (Nov. 26, 1835) :
Scum condensed of Irish bog,
Ruffian, coward, demagogue,
Boundless liar, base detractor,
Nurse of murders, treason’s factor I
Spout thy filth, diffuse thy slime,
Slander is in thee no crime.
Safe from challenge, safe from law,
Who can curb thy callous jaw?
Who would sue a convict liar ?
On a poltroon who would fire ?
Kinglake once said : “ Never offend the Times. A sea-
captain in the Navy told me that the cause of his non-
success was his having on some occasion refused to let
one of the Times’ correspondents come onboard his ship.
From that time, whenever he distinguished himself, the
Times misspelled his name, which prevented his ever
making any mark.”
* * * *
The Opal Miner (White Cliffs, N.S.W.) temporarily lost
its hold of things recently, and let the Archimedean lever
run wild. The only explanation offered was this notice,
printed in large type on yellow paper :
“ Ladies and Gentlemen.—l much regret that the Opal
Miner will not be published this week. My compositor
has neglected his duties, for which I’m in no way respon-
sible. I hope to have a compositor here in time for the
next issue.
“ Thanking you for past support,
“ I am, yours truly,
“FRANK HARTLEY.”
Presumably the lone comp, had gone fishing. The West
has suffered greatly from insurrection and swelled head
since it has had water in its dam, and has had reason to
suspect that there was a tittlebat in the water.
EDITORS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The World is full of fine Romance
Of Science and of Art,
But some of us have lost the chance
And some have lost the heart.
Saint John had luck, it seems to me,
Saw circuses galore,
And yet that Beast he did not see,
The Fine Art Editor.
He wants your sorrows in a song,
Your raptures in a rhyme,
And all your thoughts of Right and Wrong
Turned to a pantomime.
And if you have to die in verse
You must not die at length,
Because he has his Motor-Hearse
At forty jackass strength.
Before your little fire of life
He warms a frozen hand....
I wish I had a little knife,
Or poisoned salmon canned.
I would not see him die in pain,
Or gasp in tortures slow —
But there were Thoughtful Men in Spain,
Some centuries ago.
And when to Paradise my way
I take—with husky cheer
Peter will welcome me and say,
“No Editors are here.”

His verse was vile :
He polished his style
—And his heart was grey.

Intelligent utterance by a particularly
Tory Victorian M.L.A. during the recent discus-
sion on the Redistribution of Seats Bill:
shows the folly of attempting to swallow a gigantic
Bill like this before it has been properly digested.
Shipowner Smith, when he was unsuccess-
fully standing for the Senate, spoke at Kyneton
(Vic.): “The Labor party can see but one side of
the case.” “Yes,” shouted a stentorian voice—
“ the inside ! ”
In a suburban school-house, used as a
polling booth, the lady teacher left written on the
blackboard, “ Please do not curse in this school-
room.” The returning-officer did, and ordered
the chalk insult to be wiped out.
A few months ago Facing-Both-Ways
Reid declared “Mr. Charles Kingston is auto-
oratio and tyrannical enough to be the Czar of
Russia.” To-day he describes him as “ a politician
of liberal views and inolined to impartial judg-
ments.”
Headings from a recent issue of London
Daily Expbess:—
REFORMED ARMY.
DUKE OF CONNAUGHT TO BE PROMOTED.
These two lines express the reason why the lum-
bering, costly, unready British army is just as bad
after it is reformed as before. The first idea in
reforming the British army is to promote a duke.
If the case is very bad, they promote two dukes.
If the country were invaded and the enemy swarm-
ing on London, they would promote 16 dukes.

FATMAN (outside Brisbane COURLER office): “Yes,

At Broken Hill, the straight-out Socialist
candidates polled four times as many votes as the
Foreign-trade three did, and Griffith six times as
many.. Nearly a fifth of the whole straight-out
Socialist vote came from that one way-out
centre.
“Vul.” : Since the elections have hardly
heard anything about that wrecked crew that the
Herald and Telegraph were so broken-hearted
over. Are these pathetic, brown human beings
®knging desperately to the sea-washed rooks
off the Victorian coast somewhere?
Immediately after the election the S.M.
Herald came out with “suggestions’’ for the
Freetrade party. There was a long list of them,
but by some amazing oversight the suggestion
that the Freetrade party should commence to
advocate Freetrade wasn’t among them.
A paltry seven-and-sixpence, forwarded
the other day, was the first “ conscience money ”
the Federal customs department has received for
many months —almost the only refund since
Kingston, left the Customs. That sturdy battler
for the right was a better conscience-awakener
than a dozen revival missions !
The Bill legalising cremation—engineered
through Victoria’s House of Landlords by Grim-
wade, M. L.C. —has become law, and the first
crematorium is expected to be built at the new
Spring Vale cemetery shortly. All that is wanted
now is a stout, combustible man who can be easily
spared—one like Bent —to start with.
Federal members’ salaries begin from date
of election ; N.S.W. State members’ from the
swearing-in. In the latter case, however, where
the interval between a bye-election and the
meeting of Parliament is considerable, the
member will, if he pleases the Premier, be put
on the Estimates for the missing sum.
“M.”: Tasmania, apart from the mining
fields, is pretty solid for a Piebald Australia.
The shearing sheds in the midlands are mostly
piebald ; the big sawmills in the South are mostly
piebald ; apart from the active workers in the
democratic forefront, Hobart is black. Tasmania
is badly in need of a little political instruction.
Robert Harper (Vic.) said he could not
address the electors of Mernda owing to the limi-
tation of expenses law, and Bill Lyne contends
that it is a mistake to confine the expenses of a
candidate for a seat in the House of Reps, to
£lOO. All the same, Trenwith used to boast that
his election to the Federal Convention only cost
him £3 2s. 6d.
At the end of the old year N.S.W.
Premier John See allowed the Opposition to get
on his nerves and broke out in the daily papers—
They are the Liberal and Deformed party, a lot of
political rotters. It seems to me that they are perfect-
blighters. These calamity-howlers and humbugs . . .
It is part of the Sydney daily paper game to
enrage John See and goad him into saying silly
things, which are then carefully printed. And
John See is a remarkably enrageable man at all
times.
Little Oamaru (M.L.) is one of the most
heavily-taxed towns in Australasia. Its tiny
population pawned its shirt to build a break-
water. Now it has a breakwater along with a
yearly revenue of something like £16,000, and a
solid, implacable, stare-you-in-the-face debt of
over £300,000. And the shipping news generally
runs like this

Arrived—Nil.
Sailed—Nil.
The importing swindler and “ mistaken
invoice ” faker has now
got a special court all to
himself, with the State
Collector as magistrate
and tricky Bill Lyne as
judge of appeal. Yet his
plea of “honest mistake”
is not one whit more jus-
tifiable than that of many
bakers who occasionally
give light weight. The
manager of a co-operative
Bendigo bakery whose
profits are divided among
customers, and whose irre-
gularity, as he made no
profit by it, was neces-
sarily an honest mistake —
was lately summoned and
fined in the ordinary
course. He paid up with-
out complaining, and who
is the Importer that he
should be treated so deli-
cately and have a special
court all to his own cheek ?
Recently, in a case in England, after
counsel had addressed the jury, it was discovered
that, although all could speak Yiddish, not a
single juryman could speak English. If one goes
into Regent or Hyde Park it is difficult to realise
it’s England and not some species of flattened-out
tower of Babel, to such a level has Freetrade in
goods and peoples brought Britain.
The Morgan Government (recently inves-
tigating the condition of Queensland’s Public
Service, with a view to economy) found men with
high salaries doing the work of junior clerks.
Another quaint feature was the existence of young
officials, drawing a salary of £lOO per annum, and
having under them assistant supernumeraries
drawing a salary of .£l5O. Remnants of the
departed regime of “beezness ’’ men.
“ Myrtle,” from Yeppoon (Q.) :
There are some strong exceptions to “Periwinkle’s”
statement (B. 17/12/’O3) that most women did not care
whether they voted or not. Here, on election day, one
woman took possession of the platform, demanded a vote,
and was going to wait till she got it. Six feet of the Law
informed her that her name wasn’t on the roll. “ Name
on the roll!” she said. /‘The roll!! What’s the roll ter
do with me ? ” Then (with some recollections of a school
roll), “ Me boys ’as been goin’ ter school this long time.”
Despite Victoria’s anticipated big wheat-
yield, the cookies are not able to pay for their
seed wheat and free carriage of stock, and repre-
sentations are being made to the Government on
all hands to let the debt stand over until next
harvest, and Government will accede to the
request. The rack-renting landlords, the usurer,
and the machine-agents must all be paid, whilst
the Government that came and helped when all
financial friends failed, must stand out in the cold.
Chanter, defeated at the Riverina by five
votes, alleges that one of the Freetraders’ lies
which turned the scale against him was a state-
ment, made to the aborigines on the mission
stations, that under the White Australia policy
they—the original natives of Australia—were to
be driven out of the country. If that statement
can be proved, then any man who made it deserves
to be hounded out of public life and out of decent
society, as something too dirty and disreputable
for honest men to associate w ith.

The appointment of Hugh Nelson, Presi-
dent of Kanakaland Legislative Council, to the
Lieutenant-Governorship of the State establishes
a precedent worth following. The gubernatorial
position is one requiring little intelligence, and
would suit the average President of a Legislative
Council to the ground. Such appointments would
result in much profit to the State, saving, as they
would, wear and tear of the Chief Justice, who is
supposed to have brains.
Sydney Telegraph’s effort to end Bill
Lyne’s parliamentary career by boosting M‘Lean
for the Hume fell with a sorry thud. The D.T.
man who went round to misrepresent the situ-
ation wrote with monotonous regularity about the
“silent vote” that M'Lean was going to receive.
Day after polling, and before the final returns
were in, the paper received the following wire
from an Albury man • “Hurry up with M‘Lean’s
Silent vote. Urgently required at once.”
The Yic. Government is going to retrench
in the Education Department by closing the small
country schools, unless the parents consent to
contribute towards the cost of maintaining them.
It’s a wonder the calm, cool equity lawyer and his
henchmen, to produce a surplus, don’t propose to
abolish the "charity” of free education alto-
gether. A few dames’ schools would do just as
well, or couldn’t the police manage to sandwich
school-teaching in with their other duties ?
“E.R.X.”: I have been strolling round
feeling the pulse of some of the public on the
Labor victory. The almost invariable answer is:
“It’s a grand thing; but what an effect it will
have on the English money market. Where are
we going to get money to keep things going?”
I’ve come to the conclusion that' the Australian is
evolving a borrowing instinct, ijnd that it is only
a question of, avfew years when 4 he will have to
prove possession of this instinct or lose his vote.
Gameue'ss - - and energy distinguished the
campaign of the Victorian Political Labor Council.
It had to meet the solid opposition and misrepre-
sentation of the press, but this was countered
partly by the systematic stumping of the whole
State by the Senate candidates, one to each
district. As each man barracked for his three
mates they managed to cover the ground fairly
well. A few days before the polling the party
issued election broadsides, which were scattered,
free, all over the State. The result was a splen-
didly-solid vote, which made the election of a
single Tory impossible.
Dr. Embling, one of the most lamentable
cases in the Victorian Legislative Council, embel-
lished his last speech before Xmas with some
scornful references to The Bulletin and other
journals which refer to Embling and co. as “48
Rich Landlords.” David Ham, brother in spirit
and flesh to Cornelius Job Ham, rebuked his
indiscreet colleague by interjecting, “What do
we care what they say ? ” But Embling was not
to be baulked of his protest. He insisted upon
stating that he, personally, is not so rich a land-
lord as he would wish himself. Therefore, The
Bulletin’s term of opprobrium touches him in
his tenderest feelings.
Vic. Premier Irvine has done
tardy justice to the executives of the unions which
supported the engine drivers in their disastrous
strike. After the finding of the Williams test
case (which is to be taken to the Privy Council)in
favor of the Government, the commissioners re-
instated ten of the executive of the Amalgamated
Society and the Carriage Builders’ Union. The
vendetta is still extant against the drivers and
firemen, who are not to be taken back, nor is
Williams, plaintiff, nor Secretary Smith of the
Daily Paid Union, who presided at the meet-
ing which decided to take the case to the
Privy Council. The standard bearer generally
pays a heavy price for the honor and glory of flag-
flapping.
One proposal which the N.S.W. Labor
League’s annual conference will consider in Syd-
ney this month is that, in future, all seats for the-
Senate be contested by the party. While it has
been a tail-of-the-hog party instead of a Whole
Hog party it has been satisfied to run one or two.
candidates, and the results have been bad. Three-
candidates could do three times more canvassing
than one ; and in that case the results are likely
to be a good deal better. The proposal ought,
therefore, to go through. But then, if it is going-
to be a Whole Hog party at the polls, it must be
prepared to be a Whole Hog party in Parliament.
“Hypo”: Among those disqualified to
sit in the Australian Parliament is—
Any person who (1) is under any acknowledgment of
allegiance, obedience, or adherence to a foreign power,,
or is a subject or a citizen, or entitled to the rights or
privileges of a subject or citizen, of a foreign power.
As consul for Japan, is not Malcolm M‘Each arm
“ under an acknowledgment of allegiance, obed-
ience or adherence”? A consul is “on# com-
missioned to reside in a foreign country as agent-
for or representative of a government” (Chambers*'
Dictionary). At the very least, Mr. M‘Eacham.
should have his resignation, either as consul for
Japan or as member for Melbourne in the House of:
Representatives, written out for prompt delivery-
in the event of war between Russia and Japan.
Those two or three blighted little political,
scraps who are running what they call a Separa-
tion movement in N.S.W. are something-
stupendous in their assurance. This is how one-
of them wrote to Sydney Telegraph :
Manifestly the outcome of the preliminary meeting to
be held at the School of Arts on Wednesday night, 30th
inst., will be the organisation of an influentially-signed'
address to Mr. Reid, not merely inviting him to head the
movement in favor of withdrawal from Federation, but
urging him to do so, coupled with the assurance that he
will have the enthusiastic support of the entire com-
munity.
And then when the meeting got together it was
found that a dog-kennel could have held the-
entire “movement.”
There will be a bitter fight for the Stawell
seat in the new Victorian Parliament. The
Crooked-Irvine crowd which gets such a hand-
some name for honesty did a bit of deliberate
gerrymandering. It was a natural thing that the
two old seats, Ararat and Stawell, should be
thrown into one in the new scheme of redistribu-
tion, and that Tontcher, who represented Ararat
and opposed the Government, and Menzies, who-
represented Stawell and supported the Govern-
ment, should battle it out. Apparentlyit was feared
that, if this were done, Toutoher would beat Men-
zies. So Irvine and co. cut out about 700 voters
from that part of the old Ararat district in which
Toutcher was accustomed to poll heavily, and
tacked them on to another district. That this
was done as an after-thought is proved by the fact
that the new district into which these voters have
been hustled exceeds its proper number of electors
by about 1000 votes.

THE UNIONIST!
“There was unrestrained jubilation in Park Lane and
other abodes of the London millionaire when the S’African
Labor Commission announced its decision favoring the
importation of Chinese labor for the Jewhannesberg
mines. ‘South Africa is saved from the spectre of trades-

The debate in Victoria’s House of 48 Rich
Landlords on the Unused Roads Bill revealed a
quaint habit of thought on the part of some of the
extensive landlords. Sirnmie (curious name,
Simmie) plaintively lamented the desire for
• change which was coming over the community.
“ Our fathers,” said he, “ used the public roads as
their own private property from time immemorial*
and why shouldn’t we ? This eternal desire for
change, this reaching out after something new, is
merely an evidence of a tendency towards Social-
ism and should be suppressed,” or words to that
effect. Also, solemn Dowling bewailed the finan-
cial necessity that compelled a State to raise
money by leasing its own property to “persons
who for 45 years had had the undisputed right to
the use of the roads.” He seemed, like many
others, to think the roads belonged as a right to
the landowner ; yet if a boundary-rider to whom
Dowling lent one of his station horses for a month,
or two claimed the animal as his own because he
had had the use of it, the haste with which
Dowling would charge that man with horse-
stealing would be something staggersome.

To Mr. S. A. PALMER, 27/3/1901
Dear Sir,—
My son, who is now 14 years of age, has been very ill off
and on since he was four years, and about three years
ago lie was so very weak and ill that the doctor told me
he was developing consumption. He had a terrible cough,
which was distressing to listen to ; he had fallen away to
almost a skeleton. At this time Mr. Luxon, Sydney Road,
Coburg, advised me to try Vitadatio which I did. After
the first bottle I saw a great improvement, I continued
the medicine, he gradually improved until he became
quite well. I consider Vitadatio a wonderful medicine,
as it has made a different boy of my son, whom I never
expected to see healthy again. I can recommend it to
anyone, and will be glad to give any information to any-
one suffering the same complaint.—l remain,
EMILY MACKEY.
Coburg,
27th March, 1901.
I hereby certify to the above. I have known the boy
for four years, during which time he was in very bad
health. I advised his mother to give Vitadatio a trial,
with the above results.
Yours faithfully,
P. H. LUXON,
Grocer,
Coburg.

S. A. PALMER,

Mr. John Cameron, M.L.A., of Kensington Downs, a Director of the Q.N. Bank
•>d of the Queensland Meat Export Co. repprted on Colinton on 7th 1900:
“It is impossible to ride over therioh lands of Colinton without coming to the conclusion
that no just or acourate idea of their value can be arrived at by merely treating them as
grazing estates, as the large proportion of arable land embraced in them justifies the
belief that in the future a very muoh enhanced value must attach to them for cutting up
purposes.”
COLINTON is composed of a variety of sloping well-grassed ridges, with a
splendid percentage of Lucerne Flats, and has long been known as one of the Warmest
Winter Buns, as well as one of the beßt fastening Buns in Southern Queensland.
COLINTON la magnificently watered by 18 miles double frontage
to tbe Brisbane River, as well as by upwards of 48 miles of Per-
manent Creeks.
COLINTON has been kept free from pests suoh as lantana, thistle, and
burrs, and there is no nut grass and, above sell, no ticks,

Melbourne could have better spared many
a more useful identity than the late Pat
M'Oaughan, who died on Xmas Day. Pat— to
paraphrase a, line in one of the Brough and Bouci-
cault comedies —was “ the man of 50, who curled
his moustache, and thought every young girl was
in'love with him.” A “Block ornament of the
most imposing type, the M'Caughan strolled
around in white spats and rakish head-gear,
taking himself very seriously, and claiming the
right of a private citizen to be exempt from news-
paper ridicule. When the Argus published some
Saturday verses re his appalling appearance at a
fancy-dress ball, the elderly beau “pulled the
journal for libel, and celebrated his triumph by
inviting the editor to dinner. Again, when Alf.
Vincent caricatured his ornate figure in the Melb.
“ Block ” picture, of four years ago, -Pat consulted
a solicitor with a view to “pulling’ the
Bulletin, but was persuaded to take the ruthless
portrait as a compliment. All the same, he
couldn’t laugh at it. He was a comparatively
humorless Irishman, like Fitz Gibbon, the Bard of
Works, therefore Nature (as in the case of Fitz)
decreed that his harmless personal vanity should
serve the purpose of a merry wit. Melbourne
Owes ten thousand smiles to the memory of pic-
turesque Pat M‘Caughan.
David Syme, after all, bestowed a half-
hearted Xmas blessing on his ex-protege, Icy
Irvine. The late Vic. State Parliament “will be
remembered in all future time with a sense of
gratitude for its Parliamentary reform work,”
said the pale inconstant Age. Also:
For what the Government has done we can highly com-
mend it. For what it failed in we have no harsh words,
though we may have poignant regrets. . . . We feel far
more pleasure in dwelling on the success of the Parlia-
ment than on its failures. The great measure of Consti-
tutional Beform, achieved in the teeth of the Labor
party, which ought to have been the first to hail it as a
new magna charta of Liberalism, is a solid gain which
nothing can take away. It was attained only by the
exercise of Ministerial resolution, &c.
All of whioh means that the worried David
doesn’t see his way clear to barracking for some
one else than Irvine at next election. And as the
Argus can hardly turn dawg on the bloodless
citizen who Stood Firm in the dark democratic
hour of the railway strike, it may be taken for
granted that Peacock’s party will get the scorn of
both journals. Had Peacock been a less flag-
rantly incapable Premier 18 months ago he might
have hoped to be carried back to office on a wave
of anti-newspaper feeling. Under the circum-
stances thbre is a chance for the Labor party to
achieve a fairly strong working minority in Vic-
toria’s new Parliament.
No member of the Victorian Leg. Assembly
is sorry that Max Hirsch, the excitable little
German Foreign : trader, was defeated for the
Wimmera seat in the House of Reps. Hirsch
gained the warm dislike of all his fellow members
of the Assembly by his aggressive and arrogant
speech and bearing. When he followed a member
with whom he disagreed he almost invariably
opened with this sentence: “Mr. Speagur, I
vill show de hon. member dat he ez absolootely
and entirely wrungk. I vill show, Mr. Speagur,
dat he ez eether ignorant of de foxts, or dat know-
ing de foxts he villfully misleads de House.” A
story is told of 'how Trenwith got even with
Hirsch for an insult during the time they were
publicly debating the fiscal question. In two
debates Trenwith was held by the audience to
have won. The third debate had been arranged,
and wlien Trenwith came into the room just prior
to the opening of the debate-he offered to shake
hands with Hirsch, who slewed round to avoid
him. Trenwith’s sight being very defective he
did not at first notice the attitude adopted by
Hirsch, and followed him around for a second
with outstretched hand. Then he saw the Ger-
man’s insult, but said nothing. On the platform
Trenwith led off. He quoted from a scrap of
paper, which he afterwards put on the little table
at one end of which Hirsch was sitting. Hirsch
reached out for the paper as soon as Trenwith put
it down. Trenwith noticed this and, turning
quickly, he shouted, “ No, you don’t. You won’t
shake my hand ; you don’t shake my paper.”
When the thing began to soak in there was a
tremendous outburst.


“ Z.” : Re decline of some English in
industries (B. 26/11/’O2). There are trades depend-
jng largely upon recent scientific research in
chemistry, and thanks to the suicidal policy of
withholding advanced education from the people,
these trades are rapidly falling into control of
America and Germany. The English systena of
only teaching the poor the three Rs, while
Germany and the United States induce every
child of ability to enter for scientific technical
training, can have no other result. But there is a
feeling in England that the pom might lose their
reverence for the grand old nobility if they got too
much education, and the preservation of the grand
old nobility is the first essential in England.

There’s one thing appropriate in Princess
Louise’s notion for a S’African War memorial,
and that is the quotation from Isaiah, which
refers to the people of Israel being gathered from
all quarters of the earth to victimise the Gentiles.
It’s the most direct recognition, to date, of the
Jewish origin of the S’African War.

A moan from Melbourne :
Those bountiful rains that filled out the Victorian
farmer’s grain and sent up his wheat yield millions of
bushels over the highest previous record brought ruin to
many market-gardeners and fruit-growers, especially
round Melbourne. Melbourne has had no summer, or
even spring, as yet; December was almost wintry—cold,
drizzling rains sweeping the country side day after day.
The cherry crop was almost ruined and plums, loquats,
peaches, and even apples were spoilt wholesale. Lured by
a few days’ sunshine, the gardeners cut their hay, and
before the stooks could be got in, the rain soaked them,
and kept them web for weeks, till they were only fit for
burning. There seems, somehow, always to be a fly in
the ointment of the “magnificent natural resources”;
and even a generally good season emphasises the neces-
sity for supplementing them with manufactures which
don’t wither when it doesn’t rain, or burst when it does.
Considering it was a first attempt, and
allowing for the prejudice against women sitting
in Legislatures, pretty Miss Vida Goldstein polled
well. The sex is never prone to take advice, but
any seasoned political fighter would have told her
that she was making a mistake in urging women’s
rights so persistently during her campaign, and
dwelling on their disabilities. She should have
taken the rights as already conceded, and have
enunciated her platform just as a man would.
Vida has a lot to learn yet, especially on fiscal and
financial matters. Next time, probably, she will
not mention that she is a woman, just as the male
candidate recognises that there is no need to men-
tion that, he is a man. She will be simply a can-
didate.
Several things have been made plain by
the Federal elections in Victoria. The first
which sticks up like a church steeple is that
Toryism has fallen down on its job badly. The
splitting of votes gave the Tories a last chance.
They made a dying kick. In its way it was a
fairly decent kick but not strong enough to boot
the four old Tory hats through the Senatorial
goal. M‘lntyre was the only one of the four who
got anywhere near the seat. Derham’s vote of
77,000 odd was the full strength of the Tory party
in Victoria. MTntyre got a sympathy vote and
also a sprinkling of the Liberal vote i:i view of the
early day Liberalism from which he long ago fell
away. That the Tories polled every available
vote possible, and stuck to their ticket better than
any other party, is shown by the tact that less
than 8000 votes separated the lowest from the
highest man on the ticket.
The Freetrade press had to forego a lot of
interesting copy owing to the necessity for boost-
ing up the Dry Dog party through thick and thin.
When the Globular One was speaking at Red fern
he started to discourse fluently on the increased
cost of living under the present tariff, compared
with what it was under his own alleged ‘ ‘ Free-
trade ” regime. His eloquence was rudely baulked
by a young man passing up price-lists from
Anthony Hordern and grocer Anderson, of
William-street, for both the periods under review,
with the request that he would read some extracts
from them. The Globular One refused to do so,
and when he had finished speaking the stranger
proved from those trade lists that tilings are
actually cheaper now, under the alleged burden
of Protection. Two items, rice and chow-chow
pickles, were one penny dearer than under the
Reid tariff. These were the only cases of in-
creased price that could be discovered.
“ Angourie,” from Maclean :
The election of Lee as the Freetrade M.H.R. for the
Protectionist constituency of Oowper is another striking
proof that the A.P.D.A. is the tool of the Freetrade
party. Protestant Protectionist M’Kinnon was running
against Catholic Protectionist Clarke, and the local
P.D.A. support was going with him. But the Freetrade
Executive in Sydney sent Lee, a perfect stranger to the
electorate, as a candidate, and the local members of the
P.D.A., under orders from their Freetrade bosses in
Sydney, dropped M’Kinnon and took up with Lee. The
latter’s political address consisted mainly of a recital of
his connection with a butter agency, which had assisted
in the establishment of some butter factories, and on
this account he claimed to be a benefactor to the dairying
industry. He did not remind his hearers that these
factories were established when there was a protective
duty on butter. When he reached the Clarence and
found himself in a nest of Protestant Protectionists, who
hesitated to vote for the abolition of the maize and sugar
duties to spite their Catholic neighbors, he offered to be
guided entirely by their wishes in regard to those duties,
and to drop any of his alleged principles that might
prevent him getting in. So he got in as a Freetrader
who was going to inflict the curse of Protection on his
own constituents.

“P.T.F.” writes :
Was watching the election returns being posted outside
a Sydney newspaper office on polling night. Beside me
stood three men whose appearance stamped them as
workmen, and their conversation interested me. They
were discussing the Labor members. Senatorial candi-
date Griffith held a bad position. Said one man : “ Ah,
Griffith’s lost his £3OO a year ; he made a mistake by
resigning, and he’s sorry for it now, I bet.” “Yes,”
said another, “they’re all alike ; they only want the
eight quid a week.” “ Them Labor members has got a
good thing on,” said the third. “ Now, there’s Hughes
and Jim M'Gowen ; they’re makin’ money out of it. I
knew M'Gowen when he was only a workin’ man ; and
now look at him. He’s makin’ money out of you and
me. Now, a man like George Reid don’t make money
out of it ; he lives up to his salary. Anyhow, M'Gowen’s
been there long enough ; he ought to get out and give
someone else a show.” In that scrap of conversation I
thought I saw the root of all the ills that have shadowed
the workman’s existence, and that root is an ignorant,
mean jealousy on the part of a type of workman for any
of his fellows whose intellect and virility have raised him
above the ruck. Once a workman, always a workman—
that is the religion of this contemptible, begrudging
type. “ Bah ! what is he ? I knew him when he was
only a working-man like ourselves.” Haven’t you heard
that phrase often ? I have.
“ Cliass ” : lie Bulletin par on mad spots.
Know a small village near Geelong (Vie,) a 1 wavs
remarkable for weird suicides One man tied the
gun trigger to his toe and went out that way.
Another hung himself with a dog chain, and a
third cut his throat with a hay knife. The next—
a woman—hung herself behind the door in such a
manner that when it was opened, the corpse was
lifted up and looked at the intruder over the top
of the door.
The political libel suit in Westralia opens
up quite a new field of possibilities. Mines
Minister Gregory, with his colleagues’ approval,
sacked a goldfields warden, and, in an “inter-
view,’’ he explained and defended the Govern-
ment’s policy in the matter. That was the libel,
and the ex-warden got a verdict for £2750 for
it. If Governments are to be sued for libel (the
Minister admitted that the State was supplying
the defence), then it will not be long before
Governments start to sue for libel. When, for
instance, a paper, for election purposes, accuses a
Government of inhumanity towards a shipwrecked
crew, and alleges that Ministers, in their collec-
, tive capacity, committed a crime that wasn’t far
short of murder, it looks as though, before long,
that paper will have to prove its words before a
judge and jury.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Fully 20,000 people gathered round M.C.C.
oval before the start of the second test. The proceedings
for the first 45 minutes or so kept the audience deeply
interested. Then the maiden overs began to lose their
charm, the excellence of the ground fielding was
accepted as a matter of course, the heat grew oppressive,
and a general sense of dullness set in for the day
“ Great ” cricket as played at the Melbourne ground on
January Ist is a deadly game of patience, especially for
the onlookers. The batsmen can hardly be said to try
to get runs. They wait for runs to come, whilst the other
side labors to keep runs from arriving. Coldly con-
sidered, it is the reduction of skilled cricket to an
absurdity. If the eleven men who get first use of a
“perfect” wicket are capable of playing—every
one of them—the “ correct ” game for hours at a stretch,
and if the fielding is so close and indefatigable that the
cautious batsmen don’t get enough running to tire them
out and impair the efficiency of their defence, they start
the match with a chance of dragging the innings out for
three, even four, days. They are playing to wear
the perfect wicket into a state of imperfection before
the other fellows can get them out. The highest ambition
of one eleven playing the “true” game, the “ legiti-
mate” game, the “great” game, under typical Australian
conditions, is to achieve an innings of such enormous
length that the pitch, when they have done with it,
cannot possibly permit of true, legitimate, great cricket
being played by the other lot of batsmen, even though
the weather still remains as it was at the beginning. In
short, the sort of batting commended by cricket cranks
is essentially mean in spirit, unfair in 'its results, and
wearisome to the general public who support these gate-
money shows in the hope of getting reasonable entertain-
ment for their money. Slow, stonewall batting of the old
Bannerman order is good in theory and bad in practice.
Carried to its extreme limit by competent professors, it
would be insufferably unfair to the side that got the
worst of the luck. The game as Jessop plays it is bad in
theory and essentially good in practice The issue
between two teams of Jessops would depend as much as
possible on the men, and as little as possible on the luck
of the toss and the chances of the weather. To have
seen a Jessop bat, and to have observed that his average
for the season ranks among the best averages in first-
class cricket is to know that the apostles of the “cor-
rect ” game are talking dishonest nonsense all the time.
They don’t know it for dishonest nonsense, because they
are deficient in brain equipment. Their little minds are
so full of pavilion cant—the cant of leg-breaks, breaks
from the off, deceptive flight of the ball, fine strokes,
masterly placings, &c., &c., that they have no room
left for a proper understanding of the game under
discussion. A cricket match is won bv the side
that gets most runs, and it stands to reason that a short
game is likely to be a truer Test than a long one.
(Cricket is the antithesis of billiards, therefore fools
regard them as kindred games—but this by the way.)
Jessop’s average in Test matches in Australia was 18.44,
scored in the average time of about lOmin. per innings.
Two teams of Jessops, provided with as much bowling
talent as we get in an Australia v. England Test, would
score 800 runs and complete the game in less than two
days. And if anyone knoweth any cause or just impedi-
ment why 22 Jessops should not be permitted to play a
Test match right through on a wicket which neither
weather nor wear-and-tear did corrupt, let him explain
why one Jessop is qualified to play, or let him forever
afterwards hold his peace. Scientific slogging is a beau-
tiful satire on scientific pottering. But the business
policy of showmen and newspaper reporters is opposed
to any vociferous statement of the truth,
* * * *
The “bad behavior,” as the Argus called it,
of those Melbourne holiday-makers who, after waiting
three hours in a mild drizzle for the players to make a
start on a recent Monday, swarmed over the ground,
pulled up the stumps, and—one or two of them —dug
their heels into the pitch, is a good deal more excusable
than it looks. Many had paid one or two shillings to see
some cricket, and the return of their money had been
absolutely refused- which, no doubt, struck them as
plain fraud. Some had been offered pass-out checks good
for the following day, but which were no earthly use to
men who would be working next day. So they got
annoyed. The whole trouble arose through the absurd
system of the V.C.A.—which, not the M.G.C., controls
inter-State matches—of giving complimentary tickets, to
both ground and stand, to all the associated clubs.
Had! the gatemen been told to return the money,
half" the deadheads present—and they. were a
goodly number—would have demanded the money
they never paid, and there wouldn’t have been
enough to go round. There seems to be no way of
coping with the deadhead, either, unless he is shut out
altogether or put in a special paddock by himself. The
simplest way would be to abolish the club deadhead and
to restrict the M.O.C. members to their own reserve.
It can’t be too thoroughly understood that it isn’t the
“ member,” at a guinea a year, or the committee’s rela-
tives and friends, who maintain cricket and keep the
M.C.O. and ground green and fat; it is the general
public, which pays Is. or 2s. 6d. a time. The Melbourne
Club member can attend everything and take a couple of
his womenfolk in at an actual cost of about sd. a time,
and for this he has the pick of every seat in the ground.
As for the Associated club member (Fitzroy, Carlton,
etc.), he has, for his guinea or half-guinea, the use of
his own ground, club house and outfit; and then he has
the nerve to attend a big match “on the never,” and
often take a front seat, while the man who pays has to
stand.
* * * *
Sunday’s heavy rain and early morning con-
tinuation third day played havoc with possibilities of
Englahd-Australia match, and Monday (when 12,000
looked on) saw 18 wickets go down for 196 runs, of which
two batsmen supplied .122 —contributions from the other
16 totalled but 74. Remarkable thing of the day was
Trumper’s fine innings (74;—the effort of his life, all
things considered, and which saved his side the follow on
—by seven runs only. It was a risky experience, but such
risk had to be run in the circumstances, and, having,
averted the certain defeat that going in a second time,
immediately would have meant, runs were not so much
required as the necessity for getting the English-
men on the badly soddened pitch before it im-
proved, so Australia’s innings closed for 122 to”
England’s 315. At stumps draw, the visitors had
five out for 74, and were then 267 to the good. Tyldesley
was 48 (not out), and looked like giving further trouble,
but Warner, Hayward, Braund, Hirst anr( Rhodes were
done with. There was more bad weather on Tuesday,
and England’s second innings finished for 103 j leaving
Australia wanting 297 to win, and with a depressing
wicket. Much the best howling on home side was
Howell’s 4 for 43 ; 14 of his 34 overs were maidens, such
was the wonderful consistency, of length. On opposing
side Rhodes trundled remarkably though four chances
were given off him by Trumper and missed, he bagged’ 7
for 56. It was the first sticky pitch Rhodes had a chance
of performing on in Australia, and he showed himseH to
be all his reputation indicated. Finest hit of'the'match
occurred in Englishmen’s second innings when Tyldesley
lifted one of Saunders’ clean into the pavilion for 5.

“0.K.”: Went to Melbourne Cricket Club
ground last Saturday to see pome test match. No play
before luncheon time, owing to intermittent
1.15 the rain clouds blew over and the sun shone brightly
so, after a further wait of ten minutes, the urrfpires saun-
tered t 6 the pi tch and looked at it—a most absurd inspec-
tion," considering that the luncheon bell wasaboutto ring.
The teams might as well have lunched .lialf-an-hour
earlier than usual —but they didn’t. They, were not even
sharp to time after their meal, for it was 20 past. 2 ere
play began. Braund lost his wicket, ana walked slowly
back tO pavilion. Await. Presently Knight appeared,
and marched slowly to the wicket. Clean bowled by '
Howell in his first over, Knight retired slowly to pavilion.
Wait. Then Hirst come forth, and was almost arrived ht
the scene of actloO when the umpire at the pavilion end
discovered that bail had been broken when the
previous man vvas\ bowled. Another wait whilst
umpire went to get another bail. Hirst got caught out
and returned to pavilion—very slowly. Wait. Rhodes
made appearance, batted for about 10 minutes,
mu 9 .bvw. at seven minutes to 4 o’clock.
The fieldsmen seized this opportunity to add seven
minutes to the usual afternoon tea adjournment, and
retired to the pavilion at a comparatively quick pace.
They were due to resume their labors at 4.15, but in the
meantime, the clouds burst into tears. The oval remained
deserted until 5.10 p.m., when the umpires slowly went
forth to inspect the pitch, and slowly retraced their steps
to the pavilion, praying (probably) for a further fall of
rain. No rain fell, therefore the crowd waited for play to
recommence. At 5.30 the umpires revisited the wickets,
and found everything in exactly the same state, as was
to be expected. The proceedings were then formally
closed. The record for the day was 93 minutes of test
match, intersected by several long pauses.

Had a man like South Australian
Hack or Queenslander M'Donald been in with Trumper
in the first test match, when the champion was going fast
in the second innings, Australia might have scored first

know what a tower of strength he was to his side when he

rock. Hack is the man who seems to have picked up the
bantam’s mantle.

“B.B.”: Somebody should gently tap George
Giffen on the shoulder and ask him to resign from Inter-
State cricket before he loses an otherwise great record.
Years ago, he was S. Australia’s stand-by as batsman
and bowler ; but the Wheat State appears to be unearth-
ing a few good colts. And, on the question of encouraging
young players, an Adelaide paper states that “it is a
curious commentary on Giffen’s position as a selector
that 3 7 oung cricketers have to make their dibut on
grounds other than that of Adelaide, when he declines to
make the Eastern trips, and that they have to stand down
at home after having justified their places.” That is
rather a dog-in-the-manger attitude, but has S. Australia
but one man on its State selection committee ? Giffen
cannot choose a team “on his own," and if the exclusion
of young players takes place it must be with the acqui-
escence of another committeeman.

The Victorian Js.P. are a curious crowd.
Among other recent remarkable performances, the Js.P.
at Flemington have just agreed to take £lO down and
the balance by instalments spread over years, by way
of settling a fine of £lOO imposed on a betting man. The
fine was imposed last July, so it was six months before
the betting gentleman even so far recognised the law as
to :pay a trifle on account.

Sydney Tattersall’s Cup, Saturday, saw Fanfare
first choice, 3 to 1 against, at flagfall, with Summer Cup
winner Bridegroom half a point longer, and Long Tom at
s’s from beginning to end of the betting. Little Mary
and Tatterdemalion were eleventh-hour scratchings ; the
latter’s elimination was a surprise, as only the night
before he had been backed to win a fair amount, and
’twas known that someone had a “leg in” (through
Pendant's Carrington Stakes success) with him for a
heap of double money. Moreover his trainer was very
sweet on Tatterdemalion’s prospects, but owner, ’tis said,
insisted he should be struck out. Others more or less
fancied were Faithful (B’s), Ivan (10’s), and Donovan (12’s).
Praleen set the pace for ljm., and subsequently Sir
Leonard, Ivin, Faithful, and Donovan were in the lead.
Rounding the home turn Bridegroom and Faithful were
together, with Long Tom and Fanfare close up. At the
half distance Fanfare’s nose showed ahead of Bride-
groom, who tired some, while Long Tom, with a lucky
run on the rails, shot to the front and won three lengths
from Fanfare, with Bridegroom a none too good third,
and Faithful fourth. These were the best backed four of
the field. Bridegroom’s was a much better performance
than it appears on paper ; he had lOlbs. penalty, and in
5£ furlongs came from absolute last. Time for the two
miles, 3.32 J, was slow compared with speed of previous
raciss. Postillion got over the ground nearly 3sec. faster
last year, while Hoop Iron’s win was 2£sec. better.

Carrington Stakes, 6 fur., the choice thing of
first day of Sydney Tatt.’s meeting, attracted 21 starters.
Features of betting were complete knock out of Sarto
and advance of Kogarah-trained Pendant from 12 to 1
against to first favoritism, 9to 2. Next best backed was
Maitland sprinter Suivre (5 to 1), then came Bassini, who,
despite his bad- showing in Villiers Stakes (1 mile) a week
before, carried a.heap of money, invested previous night
and on the course. Sir Leonard, Suivre, and Neruda got
going first, but inside initial furlong, and despite a bad
start, Pendant was streaking ahead of everything and
momentarily increasing the gap. In the straight the
speedy daughter of Far Niente and Necklet was four
lengths clear of Suivre and Nerudai, with Idolise and
Possum close, and that remarkable mare Air Motor just
beginning to show out from the ruck. At the end
Pendant won easily by three lengths from Air Motor,
who, with the steadier of 10.5, made a brilliant effort.
Prince Lyon was third, while Suivre, Possum, and
Yanawa beat the rest. Bassini was never seen, but
Idolise shaped excellently to the half-distance where she
dropped back. ‘ • v

failed to do anything for him, and then went into the
possession of Mr. James Brennan, of the National
Sporting Club, for whom she won Carrington Stakes
12 months ago, the V.A.T.C. Oakleigh Plate, and some
minor things. Pendant’s time (1.13 f) beat her own last
year’s figures in same race by and Was only a
fraction of a second slower than Precaution’s 1896 record,
when the lastnamed had a gale of Wind behind her.
Pendant’s 9st. is the biggest load borne by any Carrington
Stakes winner.
* * * *
Alleged that owner of Pendant had £4OO on
his mare, which, if true, seeing she was backed from
twelves down to 4J to 1, means he must have made a
huge haul from his brother bookies. Stated also that
stable connections generally were very confident the nag
would win. Commission was worked cleve : ly by different
people. Remarkable how the public generally, “books”
particularly, and majority of “guns” and touts get so
badly left when Pendant is “after it” ; ’twas the same
with Carrington Stakes last year (though not to such an
extent), and subsequently with V.A.T.C. Oakleigh Plate.
Owner denies having invested, but the money came from
somewhere, and a very well-informed quarter too,
judging by the way it was put down.
Bassini’s poor displays in Villiers and Carring-
ton Stakes last week, though heavily backed for latter
event, and Dumont’s bad last in Welter on Saturday,
despite that a good amount of coin went on him, prompts
the belief that this pair are going to turn out as did their
sire Orzil, who shaped brilliantly at two years, but fell
off considerably afterwards. Last year saw Bassini and
Dumont among the most promising of N.S.W. youngsters,
but they’ve been found very much wanting so far this
season.
* * * *
Recent four days at Randwicb saw Barden
most successful among jockeys. He scored five times,
one more than Bracken and two ahead of Houseman.
Bradshaw, J. Montgomery and T. Clayton won twice
each. Of sires Grafton was easily best with four winners,
while Ayr Laddie, Projectile, Fusilier, Haut Brion,
Courage and Orzil had two each.
* * » *
New Year’s Day fixtures at Flemington had
some interesting results, although there were no tight
finishes. The easy victory of Silenus in the Standish
Handicap recalled to mind the bad performance of the
same horse at Caulfield six weeks previously, when his
owner and jockey were “ cautioned.” In the Bagot
Handicap there might have been a fine struggle between
the two favorites—Patronus and Miltiades—but Rupert
Clarke’s 6-year-old broke down when the pinch came,
and the well-treated son of Patron won comfortably.
* * * *
Doncaster Handicap and Sydney Cup nomina-
tions closed on Monday. For former event there are 112,
as against 89 last year, and for the Cup 76 have been
entered, as against 63 12 months ago
* * * •
Died, Sunday night, at Parramatta, well-known
old trainer, “ Billy ” Miller, after a lengthy illness. Rose-
hill was his headquarters for years, and there he trained,
among others, such past good ones as Sloth, Uabba, Bal-
moral, Precaution, Pretence, Raven, Jack, Killarney,
Bettina, Yanko, and Selkirk.
* * * *
J. J. Miller’s Diary and Datebook for 1904 is
the same instructive little pocket companion as usual, or
even more so. The approximate populations of principal
towns in Australasia and Maoriland are listed, together
with all sorts of time-tables and ready references.
* * * *
Cypher, winner of rich Perth Cup, beat a field
that was only one short of the record—when Carbine
scored 13 years ago. She is a 4-year-old by'Malvolio
from Initial, by Emulation from Monogram, by Talk o’
the Hills, and full sister to Novitiate, who closed on the
same race last year.

At the Caledonian Society’s, sports held at
Grafton (N.S.W.), on New Year’s Day, the athletic events
were won by T. Donohue, D. Donohue, P. Ryan* P.
O’Keefe,'T. Donohue, P. Ryan, J. Donnelly, T. Donnelly,
and J. Noonan. Think of Donohues, Donnellys, Ryans,
and O’Keefes scooping up guid Scawtch bawbees ! Not a
Mac amongst them. P. Ryan also won the Cumberland
wrestling. The real Scotchmen must have drunk them-
selves unconscious in the early part of the day.

Fact of Wheat State bowling team winning
once and playing a draw in the “ rub ” against Victoria,
last week, demonstrated that their victory at home last
Easter over the Cabbage Garden’s picked 16 was no fluke,
and also evidences remarkable advancement, for they’ve
only had bowling in Adelaide a few years—the game
received its first fillip from Waverley (Sydnev) Club.
* » ♦ ’*
Sixteenth annual prize meeting of Western
Rifle Association opened at Bathurst on Thursday and
closed on Saturday. Features were an absolute plague
of flies all three days and success of hoary-headed old
shots—front-rankers when most of those opposed to them
were at their mother’s apron-strings. Ancient Lieut.
Rickwood performed remarkably, and was top scorer
right through ; he got away with aggregate in King’s
match (first day), Western champion aggregate (300 of
possible 340) second day, and rapid-firing match, and
another event on third day.
* * * *
Match at Sydney Gaiety A.C. Monday night,
’tween middleweights Ike Stewart (black) and Melbourne
Jack Willis, didn’t draw up to expectations ; but it was
a good fight always, and exciting frequently. Willis led
mostly, and once (in the third round) floored his man.
But the negro came again, full of sand, and banged the
white round the ring till the latter pulled himself
together, and, boring in strongly, scored freely and
effectively. The tenth round saw the end —one on the
jaw, succeeded by a heavier punch in the wind, broke
the ink-hued bruiser up completely, and the towel of
submission was dropped into the ring.
* * * *
Dan Creedon-Soldier Jack Thompson fight at
Sydney National S.C. last week filled the hall and raged
fiercely from end to end. Creedon (35 years old) looked
as if he had lived 50, but for all that had gotten himself
into fairly good condition. His clear eye and healthy
tinted skin endorsed his statement that he had trained
honestly for 10 weeks, but he’s physically too old for fight
now, though not old in years. Thompson never stripped
nor fought better: he weighed 11.5 J to t’other’s 12st.
Their work was clever always though they scrambled
occasionally. First couple of rounds were Creedon’s and
third, fourth and fifth went Thompson’s way, mainly
because his comparative youth stood the strain
better, albeit every one of those jolts and short-
arm punches, with all the weight of the bigger
man’s body behind them, left an impression. “ I’d sooner
fight Cripps twice than Oreedon once ” remarked Thomp-
son ; “those little hits of his take it out of you,
one on the head in the second nearly settled
me.’’ The sixth round brought a sensation. For some
seconds before, betting men had offered 2 to 1 on
Thompson without response. The combatants battled
roughly as badly rattled men could, and were close up to
each other always, when suddenly an artistically placed
l#t short-arm jolt (the effort of a master) landed hard on
the point and Thompson went down. He essayed to rise
at “ 8,” and Dan moved as if to meet him ; then Thomp-
son went down again—he was “ out ” —but the referee
remained silent,, and they battled through to the end.
The minute’s spell brought an admonition from the con-
trolling official. “ Oreedon, you had better be careful! ”
he said. Why? Everyone looked astonished. They
hadn’t been at it long in the seventh when the referee
called them off and declared the match “ no fight,” which
paralysed the house and brought hoots from 2600 throats.
Sydney has seen no more genuine, and few more exciting,
pug struggles. The referee must have been dreaming ;
there wasn’t the merest tinge of justification for his
verdict.
* *
Half-caste Maori pug Ruenalf and lathy negro
Felix served up a sensation in their fight at Melbourne
Queen’s Hall, Monday night. Though Ruenalf showed
well occasionally, t’other was always his master ; but
fact that Felix’s fighting kit is short of anything like a
knock-out punch caused the thing to become cruel, for
the elongated bruiser dude kept jabbing till the Maori’s
left eye closed, a couple of teeth were removed, and the
bones of his left hand broken, when police interference
ended it. Ruenalf hadn’t it in him to fend the other’s
onslaughts, much less attack. And this is what Austra-
lasian heavyweight supremacy has come too. Any good
middleweight (’Murkan Jack O’Brien, who is expected
shortly, f’rinstance) could beat the whole bunch in a
week without much effort, now that Billy McColl is un-
fortunately out of the game. There are no Foleys,
Jacksons, Slavins, or Goddards these times—not even
the faintest indication of one.
* * . * *
The standard of Sydney speed swimming is
very high just now. Combination of five from one club
only (East Sydney) for next Saturday’s 500yds. Flying
Squadron Championship includes two men capable of
breaking a minute for the hundred, a third who can get
over the journey in 1.2 1-5, while the other two always
beat 1.8.
» * * *
Major Taylor reappeared at Sydney Cricket
Ground New Year’s night, following afternoon and Mon-
day night, and pedalled remarkably, all things considered.
He figured in Xmas Handicap Half-mile (first night), but
couldn’t bridge the gaps. N.S.W. Wheel Race (lm.) saw
him better placed, and as the result of a hair-raising
sprint, with a great “ jump ” at the end, he qualified by a
wheel from Boidi, amid intense excitement. Condition
wouldn’t permit of a start in five miles scratch event.
Saturday saw the black out three times without one heat
win, despite that he made desperate efforts always and
flashed brilliantly occasionally, but his bodily fix wasn’t
equal to sustained effort, which will, however, judging
from appearances, come soon. A few weeks hence
may see the flying son of Ham at his top. Stripping
thrice Monday night Taylor did no good though he made
a big bid once. In Holiday Handicap (lm.) he picked up
30 yds. on Mutton in half a lap and, later, withdrew after
some hard pacing which undoubtedly secured the event
for Mutton. Negro’s prospects in final of N.S.W. Wheel
looked and proved hopeless, he turned it up after I+.
laps. Five mile scratch race would surely have been
Taylor’s but for his being pocketed, and even then he
only went under by a wheel.
* * » *
Yankee bike-rider Lawson has a genuine
grievance against the Melbourne race-promoters. He
states that “ the officials were so afraid that either he or
M'Farland might win that they not only handicapped
them cruelly, but also placed them in heats wherein there
were impossible gaps to be bridged.” Taking the state-
ment as only fairly'accurate (since visiting riders mostly
have grievances) it points to a system of unfairness and
prejudice which should be unworthy of a promoting body.
The cracks draw the huge attendances, and if they are
unnecessarily hampered the coffers are certain to suffer.
• » » *
“F.C.” : One hilarious item relieved the solemn
monotony of a long, sad day’s test cricket. The umpires
had come out between the showers on Saturday and in-
spected the pitch, viewed it sideways and obliquely, and
punched it, then solemnly and silently retired. The rain
stopped for awhile, and the crowd kicked its heels and
yelled. Then a couple of mellow individuals jumped the
fence at the far side, and, arm-in-arm, solemnly marched
up to the pitch, tested it with their ginghams, sifted the
dry sawdust out, then beckoned the players from the
pavilion. The 17 constables who promptly rushed the
ground had no sense of humor, and hustled the two
jokers off. Then, amid a chorus of hoots, the real
umpires appeared and pulled up the stumps. Test cricket
is a great game, undoubtedly; but it's too beastly ex-
hilarating.
An interesting joke, just as The Bulletin
goes to press, is John Haynes’ letter to the Sydney
papers, stating that the mournful gathering of a
few tired persons and sundry scoffers at the
Sydney School of Arts was a vast, overwhelming
demonstration in favor of secession, and that the
cause of secesh is booming. Thus does John
break into adjectives : . ,
The magnificent response made by the electors last
Wednesday at the preliminary meeting to arrange for a
mass demonstration at the Town Hall was a sign of the
national upheaval against the monstrous injustice of the
Federation conditions as affecting New South Wales. To
find that a meeting originating among the people them-
selves, and not sanctified by what some people call repre-
sentative men, should attract crowds to throng every
available space in the hall, to fill the aisle, and compel
hundreds to go away unable to approach within yards of
the door, was an event of astounding significance.
John’s new idea is to get up petitions for signa-
ture. Which is surely the mildest way to revolushe
yet invented.
Joey Carruthers was interviewed by
Sydney Telegraph by way of an early New Year
festivity, and though he spread himself to about a
column and a half he can be boiled down to a very
small space.
(1) Joe, who was a member of that Parkes-M‘Millan
Ministry which had a deficit every year of its existence
(its shortage one year was nearly £500,000) and which ran
up N.S.W.’s borrowing (with no big land resumptions to
pay for) to nearly £5,000,000 a year—Joe, the remnant of
the most vilely profligate Government N.S.W. ever had,
is shocked at the finance of the present Government.
(2) Same Joe, who belonged to the Reid Government
which had a deficit every year save one (one of its
deficits was over £600,000), is not only shocked—he is all
broken up.
(3) Joseph, whose party has lived for years on the
Yellow Pup and is now supported mostly by Dill Macky,
complains that the Labor party has brought the sectarian
element into politics.
(4) Joey says he is amused at the “crass ignorance ” of
the Labor party, in saying it doesn’t understand what he
means when he advocates “ Restoration of the Principles
of Responsible Government.” Here he has something to
go upon, and his opponents do show ignorance. What
Carruthers means is, obviously, that he hopes to get office
by cringing to some third party for its support, as he and
his friends did for five years in Reid’s time.
