Sands’ Sydney Directory is this year larger
and more comprehensive than ever, and, as far as it can
be tested, just as accurate and rather more useful. It
contains all that the experience of 44 years has suggested
to the publishers ought to be contained—not merely lists
of names and addresses, arranged first alphabetically in
the bulk, then under professions and trades, and finally
by streets, but also lists of magistrates, of pay-days,
country traders, of Government institutions, etc., and of
pastoral occupiers. In the Sydney and suburban section
the publishers say there are now 97,796 names and
addresses, and The Bulletin will take their word for it.
They add that it is “an indispensable work of reference
to every business man in the State,” and The Bulletin
doesn’t need to take their word for that. The fact
speaks for itself.*
Bruce Smith scored the biggest majority
of any of the Dill-Macky-George-Reid candidates
in N.S.W. And how he must-now kick himself
at the thought that he went to the unnecessary
degradation of explaining that he never sent a
child of his to a R.C. school—as if he had been
accused of keeping a house of ill-fame! The
Bweets of victory must indeed be spoiled to many
a Reidite candidate as he looks at his big majority
and calculates that he needn’t have been nearly as
mean a dog as he was to get in. (
With J. W. Tyas, the registrar, now dead,
only one of the Adelaide University’s early staff
is left. Most of them passed out comparatively
young—David Kelly, the classical professor;
Roby Fletcher (Literature); and Tate (Science),
but he was older. Horace Lamb (Mathematics)
resigned and went to Manchester (Owens) Uni-
versity. Thomas Elder and W. W. Hughes,
the men who put up most of the money for the
foundations, are also both gone. So far, perhaps,
the greatest scholar this youngest of. Australian
Unis, has produced is Hudson Beare, who won an
early travelling scholarship, went to England, and
stayed there ; he is now Regius Professor of Engi-
neering at Edinburgh University. Most of the
other brilliant scholars are dead. Brilliant
scholars have a way of dying young.
On various days from April 5th to 10th, Isles,
Love, and Co., of Brisbane, will auction the well-known
Colinton station, on the Brisbane River, Queensland, in
farm lots of from 100 to 3251 acres each. The country is
described as most things that farm country should be—
well-watered, well-grassed, free from animal and vege-
table pests, and handy to rail; and the terms are
remarkably liberal. Illustrated pamphlets are now
available.*
Announcement Extraordinary.— On account
of the vast volume of medical business accruing from the
Southern and Western States of the Commonwealth, the
principals of the Freeman and Wallace Medical Institute,
Elizabeth and Bathurst-streets. Sydney, have opened at
the corner of Bourke and Elizabeth-streets, Melbourne,
opposite G.P.O., a large branch of their Institute.
Patients residing in the above-mentioned States may in
future consult at that address some of the most eminent
medical authorities in the treatment and cure of Nerve,
Blood, and Stomach diseases practising in Australia.
Consultation and advice is free, and each patient will be
under the especial care of a thoroughly qualified medical
man registered by Australian, English and American
Medical Boards.*
One of the Christmas boxes Melbourne
got was a reminder of its boom finance: Fred.
Illingworth, fresh from being elected Chairman
of Committees in the Westralian Assembly, came
across to ask the Vic. Insolvency Court for a
release of his estate. When, as one of the princes
of the boom, Illingworth left Melbourne early in
1890 he reckoned himself worth about a quarter
of a million. He owed the Bank of 8. Australia
£101,352, and the Real Estate, Mortgage and
Deposit Bank £154,404 ; the securities they held
were supposed to be worth £362,594 when he gave
them, and they nominally increased afterwards.
But Illingworth felt that the infant he was
nursing was likely to give trouble, so he went to
Europe in the hope of finding somebody who was
wanting a child of that sort. He had no luck.
On the way out the bubble burst, and Illingworth
got off the steamer in the West and stayed there.
All that the trustee ever got out of the estate was
£105, and he had to refund some of that. What
the two banks got is another story, and neither
of them is alive now to tell the tale. Illingworth
got his discharge.

ONE WORD ONLY.
If you stop and think a minute, you will realise how impossible
it is to depend upon certain words in the English language. There
are some words which have been used so promiscuously that they
have lost all significance. .....
Take our English word “GlRL”—it means a female child
anywhere between 3 and 21 years of age; but, nowadays, we call
them all “girls” up to 100. When the baby’s sex is asked, we say “IT
is a girl,” and, when you engage a 65 year old cook, she is still “a
girl. • •••••••
Go into a Restaurant and ask for lamb, and you will probably
get a two-year-old sheep—ask for chicken, and you are sure of
getting hen. . . . . I
If you are a smoker, you will have found out by now, that
according to the Tobacconist, everything is “THE BEST,”
“COOLEST SMOKING,” “SWEETEST,” and a few other super-
latives in common use. The fact is, that you cannot, in the
least, depend on adjectives when tobacco is up for discussion;
but use one word only . , ...
“HAVELOCK”
and you nave solved the problem. You will be handed a Tobacco
that needs no adjectives, no bolstering up, and no other evidence than
your own taste and observation. . . . . .
EVERYBODY SMOKES “HAVELOCK”
because it just SUITS everybody, and that’s all there is to it.

The Tantanoola (Vic.) tiger, which was
thought to be a vision of the past, has been making
Xmas reappearances at Snake Valley. This time
the reports on the irrepressible animal are less
conflicting than of yore. It ranges from “a pan-
ther rather than a tiger” to an “undoubted tiger,
although much smaller than the Bengal species.”
But, thus far, it has continued to be one of the
things that people see when they are abroad with-
out a gun.
“War E.” : Seeing that the N.S.W.
Lands Department has taken such violent offence
at some remarks of mine on the Ganmain Reserve,
may I be permitted to ask the “Dept, of Smells
a few impertinent questions :—
(1.) Does it take the Dept 18 months to prepare for
settlement a comparatively small area of 37,000 acres ?
(2 ) Had not the proposed exchange with the foreign
financial co. reached that stage that it merely required
one signature to sanction it? ....
(3.) Was not Cabinet influence brought to bear to
prevent the affixing of that signature ?
(4 ) Why did the Dept, ask Forester Osborne to send in
a report on the advisability of retaining this area of
agricultural land for a Forest Reserve ?
(5.) Why, again, was his glowingly favorable report not
adopted ?
“Athos” on Sunday morning in Mel-
bourne :
It is a strange experience, this quiet of Sunday morning
in Melbourne, after the church bells have ceased their
insistent clamor, calling the well-dressed of humanity to
their weekly mutual admiration meeting. Not being one
of the chosen, I idle along the streets noting a strange
stillness hanging over the city. All the humans (even
the Magdalenes crouched on the kerbstone recounting
their scarce-completed orgy of last night) seem listless.
The vag. spits without his usual vehemence. His favorite
oath is uttered carelessly and without meaning. Even
our poor old Yarra permeates the deadly still atmosphere
with a holy restraint in its smell. Cabs drawn by sad-
eyed Dido-like horses, too deep in their grief to even
heed their blear-eyed drivers, struggle along Ehzabeth-st.,
never apparently picking up a passenger. Even a
Chinese fails to rouse the somnolent pushite from his
Sabbath calm. This is the hour in which the lion lieth
down with the lamb. The constable exchanges opinions
with the hawker he summoned last night, and a friendly
oath follows our Chow to his opium den. All the world
says “ Blime ” in a friendly spirit. It is a very sw;eet
half-hour to us, the flotsam and jetsam of a great city ;
but how soon it ends ! The church doors open. Respec-
r c-OOH lb enuo . i'*'- vuu ‘ , : _ r , ... 1

“ J.F.D.” : Yesterday I ran to earth the
lady who advertises, year after year, “ a magnifi-
cent piano ” for sale. She is always compelled to
sell either on account of “illness, “ breaking up
home,” or “ departing for England.” Some
months ago she had a piano at North Sydney.
I went over with a friend to inspect. She wanted
£30 He thought it worth 30s., so no business
resulted. I next struck her in East Sydney (S.M.
Herald again the medium of introduction), and
though she seemed somewhat changed, I knew
her at once. She was offering a different piano,
which was worse than its predecessor. When I
met her in a railway suburb yesterday on account
of a tearful ad. regarding the sacrifice of a mag-
nificent upright grand through sickness, I knew
I had run her to earth. I told her I was sorry for
her persistent bad luck, and she was amused.
Said she did it in conjunction with other things to
get a living. Her husband bought old wrecks for
next to nothing, and fixed them up. She said she
had sold over twenty pianos during the past twelve
months. I am going into the business myself
now that I know the ropes, and I intend, by
unique advertising, to cut out all competitors. I
have already fixed up these catchy advts.:
Grand Piano to be sacrificed for almost nothing, owing
to Proprietor having been eaten by a lion.
Magnificent Pianoforte, quite new, to be sold for a song,
the property of a widow who is leaving immediately for
Homebush .
For Sale, immediate, owing to stomach-ache in the
family, splendid Piano worth £100, going for £25. If
the stomach-ache gets worse will be further reduced.
Piano, magnificent instrument, property of a lady who
was murdered, price £20.
To be Sold gratis, costly six-barrelled revolving Piano,
the property of a gentleman who intends committing
suicide.

The German seems to have a vast capacity
for losing himself. Every day the “ Missing
Eriends” columns of the daily papers contain
appeals (generally issuing from the Teutonic con-
sulates) to Eritz, late of Breslau, to had himself
■with great haste. Also Hans and Ludwig, of
Glogau and Augsburg respectively, are requested
to come up out of space and send their addresses
to their relatives. It would save much advert sing
expense if Hans were kept on a sort of heel-rope
and allowed to graze only within a certain area.
There is a good deal of the placid cow nature
about Hans, anyway, so he wouldn’t mind ; pro-
vided his peg was shifted occasionally, when the
grass on the original area ran out.
The voice (and feet) of “ T.D.” : —
I have developed malignant corns through having to
use Bent’s suburban trains, which nowadays are so in-
frequent and so overcrowded that the average man who
isn’t a downright churl has to stand nearly every time he
travels. I journeyed by train six times yesterday. First
time there were 19 people in a compartment with accom-
modation for 10 ; next time there were 14 ; next 13 ; and
the next I managed to squeeze into a seat at Flinders-
street, but had to give it up for partition among three
buxom females who boarded the train at Richmond.
Going townwards to the theatre at night, I had to stand
in the corridor of a Gippsland train, and going home
again was parboiled in a compartment with about a score
of occupants, several of whom were corpulent Reformers.
Blessed be Bent and all his works.

The Foolishness of Bookseller Cole.
Since bookseller Cole has come back from Japan
his head has been swe.-ing rapidly, and he ia
steadily taking himself more seriously, until it is
about time that he was carted into a shady,
secluded spot and spread out to cool. Cole’s
hobby, or mania, is a Piebald Australia. Before
he went to Japan he wrote an alleged book on the
White Australia question, which was so childishly
inane that even those papers which Cole adver-
tises in found it next to impossible to print any-
thing good about it. Since his return the foolish old
man scattered a pamphlet on the same subject
all over the continent, for the purpose of in-
fluencing the elections, and he threatens to follow
this up with a new book. The Parliament just
elected, however, is so distinctly White that the
Piebald Australia agitation is dead and putrefy-
ing ; but Cole argues that it will be re-opened
sooner or later, in spite of Australia’s decision, by
the brown and yellow men themselves. In a
communication to the Argus the poor-spirited
Jap-maniac says that the tropical portion of
Australia must inevitably in the future be
the home of a colored race ; and that the
only question really before Australia is whether
it will take this invasion fighting or lying
down. He urges Australia to be horizontal.
It would be humiliating, says Cole, to be com-
pelled, at the edge of the Jap sword, to give “ jus-
tice” to the brown men. If there is anything
humiliating it is the spectacle of an alleged white
man trembling before the bogey of a brown
invasion ; and even the Argus was moved to print
a thrust from a correspondent who wanted to
know what was happening that a British people
was to be frightened by Asiatics into a certain
course of action. Apart from the unwarranted
assumption that the brown man from cold Japan
can live where the sun-tanned Australian can t,
the unspeakable foolishness of Cole’s argument
is beneath contempt. If there were any remote
possibility of the White Australia flag being
lowered, such a statement as that made by Cole
is the one thing that would prop up the faltering
banner. Altogether Cole is so wearisome that it
is time he was appointed Japanese consul, or else
knighted, or made a naturalised negro.
The Australian Tendency to be
Unfinished.
Australia is a country where every man is more
or less of a politician, yet it is, to a remarkable
extent, a land where politics . are not taken
seriously. The Australian is politically unfinished.
He has a curious reluctance to live up to his
creed; he doesn’t go the whole hog, and the
entire swine is a stranger to him. The country is
full of instances. N.S.W., for example, has a
Sunday closing law which is very rigid on paper,
but is hardly enforced at all. The majority of
peoj ’e would be badly shocked if it were
prop... od to repeal the law, and the same
majority, as Pa Brunker discovered, would
be just as much shocked if the law were enforced.
The anti-gambling law is put into effect—just
a little. N.S.W. won’t legalise the tote and won’t
seriously attempt to suppress the illegal tote. It
has a Reform party which lives almost solely on
its denunciations of the present borrowing policy,
but won’t itself promise not to borrow ; it would
be an outrage on the unsettled nature of things if
it gave way to anything so definite. And in this
respect N.S.W. is just Australia in general. Aus-
tralia passed a law to prevent nigger immigration
and the importation of workers under contract,
and almost the whole, country was proud of the
law when it was finished, and said, “This is
Good ! ” The law has been enforced very
mildly, but every time it was enforced there has
been a powerful row, and the Government’s
enemies nave been very much more conspicu-
ous than its friends. The amiable indefiniteness
of the country was badly shaken up; even the
Government’s supporters, in many cases, felt un-
settled through being thus taken at their word.
Kingston grew old and grey with struggling
against the large class which didn’t regard the
law as so very bad, but looked upon the enforce-
ment of it as an outrage on the unfinished tenden-
cies of the land. Australia has a Protectionist
party which protects in a very humble way, and a
Freetrade party which doesn’t Freetrade at
all. Yet if the Protectionist party advocated
100 per cent, duties and the Freetrade party
wanted to abolish all duties the row could hardly
be worse. This tendency to make an elephant of
a row over a mouse of a difference is characteristic
of Australia. The country is crowded with people
who believe in honest finance—there are some
parts of it where you can hardly breathe for
financial reformers—and the vigor of their belief
is something tremendous, but it is a cold year
when the six States can’t muster five deficits
among them. Australia is full of things, like the
N.S.W. Local Government proposal, which seem
to have been killed by general approval—to have
been believed to death —to be impossible of
attainment owing to the enthusiastic support of
everybody. Why these matters are so it is diffi-
cult to say. Perhaps the Australian isn’t more
unfinished than other people, but only looks that
way by some accident.

Perth (W.A.) thought it was reading a
strange title of a new burlesque lately when it
beheld, on the front of the new theatre now
building, a large calico sign inscribed “God bless
my dear son John.” It transpired that city
councillor and theatre proprietor Molloy’s son had
just come home and attained his majority, and
this was the proud father’s way of giving the
glad tidings to the world.
No Australian Need Apply.
In South Africa the situation now seemi to be,
briefly, that no Australian need apply. Many
people are surprised at this, but, after all, it is
only the natural result of circumstances. South
Africa is now mostly run by the De Beers
diamond combination and the Johannesburg
mine-owners. They are a large-vested, large-
nosed people, who did not shout in the forefront of
the battle, as the Australians were ready to do.
But they made notes and said, “ Mein Gott,” and
other things, but mostly “Mein Gott.” They
watched, afar off, the Australians howling up and
down kopjes and burning farms, and they made a
mental memorandum that they were men to be
ejected, with all possible speed, when done with.
What is wanted now is the patient, uncomplain-
ing, cheap, servile, ox-like laborer—the Asiatic for
preference, the Kaffir if the Asiatic can’t be got,
the most crushed kind of European if the Kaffir
is insufficient. What is wanted, in short, is a
laborer without a howl in him, and one who is too
mild to capture an ant-heap, let alone a kopje.
Men who take kopjes are turbulent, assertive, apt
to talk back and upset a mild Jew’s nerves.
Every kopje the Australians took made it more
certain that they were a race who ought to be
starved out of the country—it was far more neces-
sary to sthrve them out than the wooden and
methodical British Tommy, though he is being
starved out also. The Australian is now getting
the natural reward for the kopjes he took. He
was very useful in war, but his very success
showed him to be an off-hand, irregular, disre-
spectful sort of person, whom no stout and quiet-
loving Hebrew magnate could possibly endure
about the place. A swashbuckler may be neces-
sary to protect the rich man in the day of trouble,
but a flunkey is what he wants in days of peace,
and every time the swashbuckler swashes he gets
more on the Hebrew’s nerves, and shows himself
more unfit for the flunkey’s billet. If the
Australian had let the Hebrew take his own
kopje he might have a much better chance of a
job now.
“The Bulletin” Goes to Church.
The Bulletin has just read a sermon by Dr
Watson (lan Maclaren) which puts the position
of the church to-day in a nutshell. Dr. Watson
has laid down his pen, and has taken a
shrewd look around, and has come to the con-
clusion that things are rather unhealthy for the
church, and that a different policy is necessary if
the church is to hold its own. The new prophet,
he says, will preach the gospel of social deliver-
ance :
That the white female slaves who sew from morning
till night, and half through the night, and hardly get the
wherewithal to keep body and soul together, should be
delivered from their bondage, and that every laborer
who is willing and temperate should have his living wage
wherewith to keep himself and his family ; that every
citizen, however humble, should have his own little home
wherein to live in peace and comfort; that the country-
man should not be evicted from the land to make room
for wild animals and rich men’s sport ; that the owners
of insanitary property should be punished--and not
compensated (Dr. Watson must have been reading
recent Sydney history); that every man should have
free access to education, to the country, to health,
and just enjoyment ; and that the burden of weariness
and sickness' and tyranny should be lifted from the
shoulders of them who labor and are heavy laden.
After announcing this breezy and comprehensive
gospel he adds:
The workers have reason to believe that the Church has
not been concerned about how they live, or how they
were paid; that the Ohurcn has not sympathised with
them in their struggle to obtain just wages and decent
homes ; that the Church has sided with the capitalist in
the city and with the landlord in the country. . . . The
confidence of the people is not to be rewon by faith
without works, nor by all the addresses in the world un-
heralded by practical service.
To which The Bulletin, after dropping half-a-
crown in the collection-plate, says “Amen.”
The decision of the Victorian Full Court—in the
case of Williams v. the Railway Commissioners—
that railway employees hold office during pleasure
only (the pleasure, that is, of the Commissioner or
his bosses, the Cabinet), and that they may be re-
moved without a reason being given for dismissal
—that, in effect, the Commissioner may prefer
any charge against any employee whom he wishes
to get rid of, and dismiss him without a hearing—
is so unfair and one-sided as to afford (if that were
wanted) a powerful additional reason why all
State employees should be brought inside the
provisions of the Federal Arbitration Bill. Ice-
berg Irvine showed last May what demands he
could make, and what outrageous powers he would
arrogate to himself if he were permitted ; and any
day, in any State, another and, if possible, worse
Irvine may arise, who may impose conditions on
the State’s servants that no self-respecting body
of men could accede to, merely in order to get an
excuse for wholesale dismissals and the confiscation
of pension rights, or in order to make room for his
own political supporters.

A suffrage problem:
The claim, about which so much has latety been heard,
that the old people in the N.S.W. Government asylums
should not have votes, is not one to be thrown into the
waste-paper basket offhand. If it were possible to create
an ideal franchise these old people would not have votes
—nor would a number of the people who write to the
papers clamoring for their disfranchisement. It is not
because they are, unfortunately, compelled to live on
the community that they might be disfranchised ; as
regards a very large section of them, the country
owes them that much, and a great deal more, because
of their past services. Also, in many cases, they
are poor simply because they didn’t swindle some-
one else and make him poor instead. Besides, so
many persons besides inmates of asylums—so many
persons who keep afloat by following utterly useless
occupations and whose assets are an office, a brass plate and
a confidence-trick—live on the community that the
matter, as regards the inmate, isn’t worth
mentioning. But there comes a time in the lives of
most people when they probably shouldn’t have
votes, and when they certainly should be shut out
of the Legislature—a retiring age, so to speak.
It is the time when the individual becomes so old
that he feels it isn’t worth while improving the country
because he won’t be here long enough to benefit by the
improvement—when it doesn’t matter if things break ten
years hence because he doesn’t expect to be here ten
years hence —when the whole future is narrowed down
to the brief period he expects to live, and it isn’t worth
providing for much beyond that, and foresight and
enthusiasm appear ridiculous, and there sets in the in-
curable Toryism of the man who reckons this is a good
enough world to die in. The exact age is difficult to
define, hut almost every man comes to an age at which
he should be disqualified as a legislator. It doesn’t really
matter so very much whether he is disqualified as a voter
or not. That should also be done, but it is a much
•Mailer affair.
“ Agitator” : A new source of expense to
the Vic. Railway Department has arisen since
the advent of the men who helped Irvine crush the
strike. Some of the “blacklegs” openly boast
that, having the Government behind them, they
will make the old employees “sit up” on the
slightest provocation, and they send in reports
and apply for inquiries over the merest trifles.
One bright speoimen of these “loyalists” has
already cost the country from £5O to £lOO for de-
partmental inquiries.
The situation condensed :
Re these unfortunate revelations about how the day-
labor system is sometimes—perhaps very often—run
on N.S.W. Government contracts: The public may
be sometimes prepared to pay for day-labor work
more than it would cost to get the same thing
done by competitive contract, but it will not be
prepared to pay more for it all the time. It is not
going to pay more simply in order to give employment
to men whom contractors would not employ because
they are not up to the mark. If they are feeble, the
public may be prepared to give them assistance out of
the charity vote, or if they are old it may be prepared to
passion them ; but it is not prepared to give its charity
in <)he form of wages. And the working people, who are
the most interested in maintaining the day-labor system,
ought to be even more interested in seeing that this isn’t
done. Price isn’t the only test, but it is one test, and
it is a test to which even Labor men have assented.
Certainly it is a test which, whether they assent or
not, will be applied. They are, therefore, the most
interested in seeing that the results of day labor
answer that test; and the results never can answer
it so long as men are employed who cannot
stand on their own merits as workman. The
only way to secure such workmen is to absolutely
eliminate political control and lay the whole responsi-
bility of employing and dismissing workmen upon the
officer in charge—to give him, in fact, as absolutely free
a hand in that respect as if he were a contractor. The
rotation system strikes at the very root of this. Under it
a workman is put on a job no matter what his
quality : he is kept on for a specified time; and then, if
a new lot which have had no work are enrolled, he is put
off again. This may be all right for relief work,
where the thing aimed at is merely to provide a
certain number of men with relief in the shape
of wages; but, as it destroys all incentive, for
the loafer is as well off as the industrious, it must kill
any undertaking, judging it by the standard
of cost. Of course it relieves the officer in charge of
responsibility. You can’t make him responsible for the
cost if you‘don’t trust him to select his workmen.
Nobody, in fact, is responsible ; and where there isn t
responsibility there isn’t likely to be either serious effort
or success. ' The only way is to pay a responsible man,
and trust him.

London Daily Mail took a vote of its
readers on the fiscal question, and succeeded in
getting 546,779 answers. Of these, 166,606, or
less than one-third, were for Freetrade.
Gregor M‘Gregor’s position in the voting
tor the Senate in S.A. was remarkable. Of the
seven districts which return each a Representa-
tive, he led in six. He polled an absolute majority
of the votes cast, not only in the aggregate, but in
every one of the seven districts. Guthrie was
second in four and Charleston in the other three
districts ; but the Radical districts sat on Charles-
ton (a renegade from the Labor party) so heavily
that he did not get a place among the final three.
The S.M. Herald, in an article on
“Camp Annoyances” (28/11/’03):
There is usually a yell, and the victim is prepared
to swear that he has been stabbed with a red-hot needle
when one of the family of poisonous or fighting ants gets
hold of him. Many a marriage has been hastened
through the efforts of the bull-dog ant.
Now what does the Herald mean ? Will it
kindly explain the bull-dog ant as an aid to
marriage ?
Even at this late hour the Preetrade
Sydney Telegraph whines about being deprived
of the opportunity to loot the taxpayers by getting
its wares carried on the railways at the public
expense. This is an extract from a recent shriek
published by the rich old cadging paper :
The Sydney journals have nothing to be ashamed of in
the history, an open history, of this carriage business.
They have fought the whole issue out before a prepon-
derantly unfriendly select committee of Parliament, and
the whole matter has been laid bare to the public eye.
Only by misrepresentation of the facts can they be placed
in the wrong, and misrepresentation can be corrected.
They have appealed, and if necessary can appeal again, to
the tribunal of public opinion.
In other words, the hardened cadger threatens to
appeal to the tribunal of public opinion in order
to assert the purity and nobility of his cadge.

In an illustrated booklet of 50 pages the Union
S.S. Co. describes the Canadian-Australian mail
route to Great Britain under two expressive titles—the
“ All Red ” and “ the Scenic.” The tourist who wants to
get an idea of the size of the British Empire will go by it
because it is the one ; he who wants to see the various
beauties of the South Seas, the remarkable scenery on
the Canadian Pacific railway, including the Rockies and
Niagara, will go by it because it is the other. The Rockies
and Niagara are pretty large attractions in themselves ;
just now the forthcoming St. Louis Exhibition (which
the booklet illustrates) is thrown in. Copies can be had
from the co.

The Federal election at Ni Ni, a village in
the Wimmera (Vic.) electorate, was a comic
episode. The deputy returning officer decided to
take a week’s holiday that was due to him and
hang the election, so those cockies who rolled up
either had to go to some other booth, or else post-
pone matters. Mostly they postponed—across
the road, where there was a hotel. Then it was
decided to take a proper poll that day week.
Meanwhile furious arguments raged as to whether
persons from other districts in the electorate who
hadn’t voted on the regular polling day were
entitled to line up in their hundreds at Ni Ni, and
upset all existing calculations. It was decided
they weren’t. Then, while the blessed election
was proceeding, Max Hirsch lodged a protest on
the ground that the booth was not the one adver-
tised. There were only twelve voters all told,
and when the boxes were opened it was found
that every man had voted for the excitable Max.
The Senate voting panned out eight each for the
Aegus four; Trenwith and Solly two each,
Findley, Goldstein, M’Culloch, and Styles one
each. What became of the other eight votes
goodness knows.
Should Russia and Japan decide upon
war, the nation whose leaders obtain their
intellectual stimulus from D.C.L. Whisky must
score through superior wisdom. The little Japs,
if inspired by that scientifically adjusted beverage,
have sufficient pluck and other murderous re-
sources to land them on top at the termination of
the contest. On the other hand, should the
Russians suddenly substitute D.C.L. for Vodka,
the Japs will be wiped out in one'act. D.C.L.:
The guide that leadeth to success.*
Though N.S.W. has voted for the reduc-
tion of the Assembly to 90 members it is very
probable that the next Parliament will be elected
on the same old basis. The present Parliament,
when it meets in February, will have only 4|
months to live. In that time a Bill has to be
passed reducing the Assembly to 90 members.
The State has to be divided into new electorates
and they have to be approved by Parliament, and
at this stage of the proceedings every one of the
125 members usually complains at great length
that the re-arrangement is made for the especial
purpose of killing his chances. Then the new rolls
have to be compiled. If the Bill is reserved for
the King’s assent (which may happen, though it
doesn’t seem necessary) then it will be Christmas
1904 before anything very definite happens.
Biliousness Again Banished.
Bilk Beans a Reliable Remedy.
Biliousness is the direct outcome of the liver
failing to eject all surplus bile from the system,
which, in consequence, runs riot through the
system, becomes mixed with the blood, and
biliousness, with its attendant evils, is the natural
result. That Bile Beans are an undoubted cure
for this distressing complaint is now a well-proved
fact throughout N.S.W. As a striking illustra-
tion, Mr. T. Jepson, of 85 Old Parramatta Road,
Forest Lodge, Sydney, writes : —“I consider your
Bile Beans to be the remedy, par excellence, for
biliousness and the liver. I suffered considerably
with these complaints and was advised to give
Bile Beans a trial, which I did, to the great
benefit of my health. Since then, I would keep
no other medicine in the house, and never fail to
'recommend Bile Beans to all whom I hear com-
plaining of liver troubles. But I find the good
qualities of the Beans are generally recognised and
appreciated in this district, in which I have been
a resident for the last fourteen years.” Bile
Beans have been proved a reliable cure for bilious-
ness, headache, indigestion, constipation, piles,
debility, female weaknesses, nervousness, bad
blood, anaemia, insomnia, loss of appetite, summer
fag, and in fact all ailments that owe their origin
to defective liver action. Bile Beans are obtain-
able from all medicine vendors, price Is. ljd. or
2s. 9d. large box.*
ELECTORS OF RIVERINA,
4
To the Editor The Bulletin.
Sir, —Will you permit me through your columns to
convey my heartfelt thanks to my supporters for their
magnificent rally on the 16th inst., which has resulted in
my having been returned at the head of the poll. I
would also like to take this opportunity of specially
thanking all those ladies and gentlemen forming my
committees, in the different centres, and in particular the
secretaries of those committees, for the splendid work
carried on by them from the opening of the campaign
until its close. Outside my committees there have been,
I know, many who have worked hard and well in my
interests ; these ladies and gentlemen I would also like to
thank most heartily. My scrutineers at the different
polling booths are especially deserving of my warmest
thanks for the way in which they have carried out their
arduous duties, rendered doubly so by the provisions of
an Electoral Act which has worked so badly and caused
such widespread dissatisfaction.
To the Press throughout the electorate I owe a debt of
gratitude for the vast amount of work done in so fully
reporting the different meetings, and for their uniform
fairness in dealing with everything connected with the
campaign.
To those who have fought against me as open and
honorable opponents let me say that, now the battle is
over, I will gladly meet them all as friends, and while
representing this great electorate of Riverina in the
Federal Parliament, I hope I may prove that I have the
interests of all at heart whether they may have opposed
me or not during the campaign just ended.
I have the honor to be,
Your obedient servant,
R. O. BLACKWOOD.
Deniliquin,
23rd Dec., 1903.

At Poverty Point.
Covent Garden Theatre, London, was burnt in 1856, as
a finale to a hal masque, given by Anderson, on the eve
of his departure for Australia. The performances had
extended over two days and nights.
“Melb.”: Of course.it is not possible to worm any
criticism into local papers on the safety of theatres. Our
Princess and Her Majesty’s are good safe houses, as such
places go, and this is not affected by Her Maj.’s having
been h'jzlt on the interior pattern of the disastrous
Vienna Ring Theatre. It has been claimed that the
Ro.yal can be emptied under ten minutes. I only object
to the vestibule with its bars. I object to any theatre
being wholly upstairs, like the Bijou. The Opera House
pleases me just a little better than the Princess’s. Here
is the order in which I place them : Her Majesty’s, Opera,
Princess, Royal, Bijou.
Our old acquaintance, Walter Bentley, has broken out
in a new place. When last heard of he was appearing at
a London music hall in a Shaksperian variety sketch —
i.e., condensed extracts from Richard the Third. Music
hall audiences want their bits of Divine Shake served in
a hot and peppery style, and Bentley gave ’em the
humpiest-backed Richard imaginable. Also, he was
threatening to produce sketch versions of “Macbeth”
and “ Othello.”
“ H.” : Accidents I have witnessed at shows : Was in a
dress circle, with safe retreat, and saw a fire panic at
leisure. Another, time, was badly off in a crowded
gallery, with an infant sleeping peacefully. beside me,
when the scenery caught fire. Actors put it out. Was
at a pantomime when the flies caught fire, with fairy
children tied up among them. Next night, at the
opposition pantomime, the giant’s wig got ablaze, from
a procession jet. At a variety show flakes of real fire
came tumbling down from above. Was in a big circus
when it broke down. Nobody hurt. Saw a comedian
fatally injure himself in climbing over a stage fence,
with spikes. Saw an actor stab an actress in the course
of a play, so that she was laid up for three months.
When I essayed to act myself, the gasrnan plunged the
whole show into pitchy darkness during my best scene.
Chicago’s holocaust possibly tops the record for
theatres, but does not come within cannon-shot of the
champion church disaster —that of Santiago, where, on
Dec. 8, 1863, there were over 2000 people burnt, mostly
women. The Ring Theatre, Vienna, was burnt in 1882,
with a loss of 447 lives out of 2000. The Paris Opera
Comique fire was May 25, 1887, during a performance of
the opera “ Mignon.” Loss of life, 131. (it is a ghastly
coincidence that the Chicago Iroquois Theatre was
modelled on the rebuilt Opera Comique of Paris.) Car-
valho, the manager, was sentenced to three months’
imprisonment, and a fine equivalent to £2OOO. Andre,
the chief fireman, was sentenced to a month. The
manager was also imprisoned over the Ring Theatre
affair. We have never heard of such punishment in
English-speaking communities, and yet it has often been
richly deserved.

“Marat”: A swarm of bees
on the wallaby settled in the
stalls’ entrance of a Sydney

ager, who was in low spirits,

two m lkmen pulled up in front

and the sub. rushed inside,
wrote an eight inch advertise-

about an ‘ office,’” he said to the

and honey.” And there was a

Austin Brereton, in his new
book on the London Lyceum,
says that in 1838 a lineal de-
scendant of Shakspeare brought
out an original play there, for
one night. This piece introduced
Shakspeare, Queen Elizabeth,
and Leicester.
Sydney Town Hall’s is definitely not now “ the biggest
organ in the world.” One has just been installed in the
Seville Cathedral (Spain) which has four metal flute stops,
each 16ft. long. It has bass bourdons which give 32
vibrations a second, and thus make a deeper bellow than
any previously produced.

their own show in partnership with Otho Stuart, who
visited Australia years ago as the white-haired boy of
Mrs. Bernard Beere’s co.
A certain physically robust tenor of foreign manu-
facture regards himself as a heaven-born entre-
preneur, and, when anybody else falls in, shrugs
his shoulders and exclaims, “Ah, but dey would
not take the advice I give.” Also, he approaches
new vocal aspirants and asks, “ When are you going to
make a concairt for yourself? I will man-age for you for
twenty-five guineas. It is sheap.” But the short-
frocked aspirant has usually been deluded into bestowing
all her guineas upon some alleged singing teacher, and
her intense desire to give a concert on her own is satis-
fied but rarely.
“Melb.”: The “Country Girl’’recalls the comedy of
the same name which Garrick adapted from Wycherley’s
Country Wife. Mrs. Jordan’s great character was
Peggy, in the “ Country Girl.” Pattie Browne acted it in
Australia. It was a famous breeches part, so called. In
it Mrs. Jordan fascinated the Duke of Clarence,
afterwards William IV. ; they had ten children, all
copiously provided for by the tax-payer. One of them,
the Countess of Errol, was ancestress of the Duke of Fife,
the King’s son-in-law. Thus the whirligig of time brings
its revenges.
Mons. Ponsel, the dapper little Frenchman who trains
the meat-eating quadrupeds at Wirths’ Circus, g "it
married the other day to a Melbourne girl. It isi . t
every day that a lion-tamer gets married. The Wirths
wanted to seize the opportunity for a nuptial ceremony
in the caged arena, with the lion posing as “best man,”
and the goat gawking around in gala costume, and the
hyena smiling at the happy pair. Mons. offered no per-
sonal objection, but the bride couldn’t like the idea a
little bit. So the Wedding in the Cage didn’t come off.
‘ Little Mary ” : The Broken Hill show was a syndicate’s
sin and a State’s scandal. It should have been called a
horse - parade. The programme mainly consisted of
horses and vehicles. Just by way of variety there was a
bullock-weight guessing competition. Year after year
the good old Government supplies the usual subsidy for
this and similar wretched fakes. Small wonder that
there’s so little money in the Treasury. The only good
the Silver City show does is to promote good-fellowship
of a (hie) kind ; and to cause the local mokes to be
groomed once a year, and the conveyances to have their
annual bath.
The short story of that appalling burning fatality at
the Iroquois Theatre, Chicago. The asbestos curtain,
which should have prevented the fire spreading from the
stage to the body of the house, refused to come down
when wanted—for want of constant use, things like that
generally refuse to come down when wanted. Also, if it
had come down, it would have done no good, as it
turned out it was not composed of asbestos. There
were 40 exits, but the audience only knew of three or
four. Result, as far as known, 878 deaths.
George Musgrove borrowed the biggest bill-posting
brush in Melbourne with which to lay on the jam in a
letter addressed to the Argus. George wanted to contra-
dict a rumor that he was abandoning theatre business in
Australia, but in half a stick of matter he managed to
work in references to “ our intelligent and critical
audiences ” and to “ attractions worthy of the intellect of
Melbourne.”
Cabled that August Siede, a nice-looking young Mel-
bournian, has pleased the musical enthusiasts of Dresden
with his lyric symphony entitled “The Australian
Walpurgis Night.” August Siede (son of Julius of that
ilk) deserves credit for composing what Marshall Hall
calls “ the first musical work of downright merit done by
an Australian ” ; nevertheless the alleged approbation of
Dresden will not carry much weight with the public which
heard this same lyric symphony performed in Melbourne
by Marshall Hall’s orchestra 18 months ago. On that
occasion the composer called it a symphonic overture,
and never a word was said about its “ Walpurgis ”
significance. Not knowing what they were expected to
comprehend the audience couldn’t get a thrill or half a
thrill from the sombre composition. It kept the instru-
mentalists pretty busy, and was doubtless “ musicianly ”
and all that sort of thing—but O ! it was a severe trial
even to people who had come with the intention of dis-
covering genius in it. Perhaps the orchestra, under the
personal direction of anxious young Siede, failed to do
justice to the iob.
Mary Conly, the buxom soprano from Melbourne, has
been getting some promising engagements in England.
Mary Oonly has a fine, strong voice and she sings artisti-
cally, but in the matters of style, smile and corsetry she
was still very backward when she left Australia a couple
of years ago.
Arthur Elwood died in London the other week. Only
a few Australian play-goers have troubled to remember
that Elwood loomed'for a short time as leading man of
the Brough and Boucicault 00. He took the part of a
dreamy, romantic hero in “ The Masqueraders” and com-
peted with Titheradge for possession of the heroine—a
lovely barmaid, impersonated by Geraldine Olliffe. Tith.
was the vicious baronet who bid top price for the barmaid
and secured her hand in marriage, but subsequently the
two men played a game of picquet in which the Dreamer
staked his fortune and the baronet staked his “ missus,’’
and lost her. At the moment the lady was quite anxious to
pass over to the other and nicer man, but subsequently'
she suffered from awful British scruples re the sanctity
of marriage, and remained with the boozy baronet, and
thus spoilt the play for Australian tastes. It failed, and
Elwood also was a non-success on account of his persistent
monotone and depressing romanticism. A polished,
gentlemanly actor, utterly deficient in force, he dropped
out of theco., but his wife—Miss M. Talbot —stayed on
awhile and did some good work in the society dame line
of character. The many old admirers of Miss Talbot on
this side will be sorry to hear of her bereavement.
Wilson Barrett lately celebrated the 21st anniversary
of “ The Silver King” by reappearing in the name part.
The stodgy old gentleman honestly believes that it was
his original performance of Wilfrid Denver which made
the success of the play. His friends have told him so.

of a graphophone in the distance, if that wasn’t the band.
The Ma replied : “ No, dear, that’s not a band—that’s one
of those what-you-may-call-its.”

“ G.Y.” i Knew a Hawkes Bay (Maori-
land) J.P. (M.H.R. as well) who was summoned
for allowing cattle to wander. When case was
called defendant was occupying bench, and gave
judgment as follows : “I feel very sorry to see a
man of defendant’s circumstances in this position.
He has been known to me personally for many
years, and has always borne a good character.
As the offence is not likely to occur again, he will
be convicted and discharged.”
“War E.” : Mention of Andrew Lynch
(8., 17/12/’O3) recalls the fact that even to-day
there may be seen in many houses in the Careoar
district examples of Andy’s thoughtful gener-
osity in the shape of well-bound copies of
Hansard and Votes and Proceedings ! The
stranger is invariably shown these monuments of
wisdom, with the remark: “This illigant book
was presented to us by our second-cousin, Mr.
Andrew Lynch, M.P. God rest his soul!” The
sacred book is then carefully placed on the parlor
table in company with the “ Holy Bible ” and the
“Family Doctor.”
“ 0.K.” : Touching Gov. Loch’s ignorance
of the duties of his position, Thb Bulletin
repeats the story of his having proposed to take
over the Defence Deportment from the Victorian
Government. This astounding fact remained a
secret between Loch and the local politicians of
that period, who grovelled wormishly to Sir Henry
and “ dear ” Lady L. The public, however, got
an inkling of His Excellency’s sense of self-
importance when he allowed a working-man to
be given into custody, and sentenced to 14 days,
(without the option), for passing a disrespectful
remark upon the vice-regal “ orderly.” The irre-
verent toiler, struck by the pompous demeanor of
the person who rode in front of the Loch equipage,
said to his mate in a loud tone of voice—“ Bill,
who's that on the ’orse ? ” Whereupon the
proud flunkey hailed a passing policeman and
gave the caitiff citizen in charge with His Ex.’s
warm approval. Magistrate Panton decided that
the bald space in the above remark amounted to
disloyalty under the circumstances. Governors
have been steadily losing weight since those days.

“G-.” : Once watched a certain whiskered
politician make four intoxicated attempts to board
a tram. At the first time of asking, the hairy one
was indignantly cast off the footboard by a
matron who had been in to the grocer’s to order the
boiled ham, and over whose face the tangled
legislator had shed his whiskers like a bad simoom.
Rescuing his bell-topper from the gutter, he
awaited the next tram on his hands and knees;
and, as it swung past him, he leapt frantically
toward the parallel laps of half-a-dozen shop-girls.
He slipped from their sloping knees, however, like
a disrupted star falling regretfully out of the
zodiac, and was left, pondering the excessive
slipperiness of all things. When the next tram-
gong clanged, he rushed it, striking a bank
director by whom he was brushed away like a bit
•of fluff from the countenance of the Almighty.
Then a policeman took charge of him and put him
and his whiskers tenderly into the fourth tram-car
that came along, and ended the show-
“ Alba” speaks disrespectfully of a poet
and a statesman :
Senator J. 0. Neild would make an admirable character-
study for a novelist. He is the great fighting cheese-
monger. His oppressive fluency drowns one like a
cataract of lemonade or hairwash. ’ There is something in
the deep cellars of his being prompting him to become a
literary statesman and a poetic Kitchener. Yet as a
poet he fs far behind Purtell, the Melbourne kerbstone
balladist. There is more blood and stamina in a tin of
milk than in anything he has written or said. One day,
in a crowd, I looked through a microscope at Neild’s
moustache. It was like gazing at a rope works that had
become bogged in a soap-and-glue factory. Clots of
gelatinous matter hung in shreds about his mouth ; his
upper lip was a Niagara of hair. Ac Mafeking time, when
the cheesemonger spirit threw aside its apron and fell
upon the beer, Neild used to lead the departing con-
tingents to the Quay. He rode in front of the
brass band with a boiled ferocity in his eye that
only Homeric slaughter could quench. At the
troopships Neild usually made a speech to the men,
shook his nickel-plated scabbard, and then went
home. There used to be a huge bullock at Glebe Island
abattoirs known as Red Judas. It was R.J.’s business to
make friends with the mob in the yard, and lead them in
strings to a place where a blithesome fellow with a poleaxe
leaned from a gallows. Judas trotted up with a fresh
lot about every hour and passed under the gallows, but
was spared. One day the poleaxe man fell ill. He
was replaced next morning by a cross-eyed butcher
who didn’t know Red Judas, and slew him oif-hand. The
oversight disarranged biz at the abattoirs for a while,
but there were dozens of bullocks eager to take the
place of Judas. Some day, if this country is invaded,
a cross-eyed commander may insist on Neild going a
little more to the front, and then either he or the enemy
will be killed. Australia would certainly never replace
Neild’s moustache.
A letter from Henry Lawson .
Dear Bulletin.—l’m awfully surprised to find myself
sober. And, being sober, I take up mv pen to write a
few lines, hoping they will find you as well as I am at
present. I want to know a few things. In the first
place : Why does a man get drunk ? There seems to be
no excuse for it. I get drunk because I’m in trouble,
and I get drunk because I’ve got out of it. I get drunk
because I am sick, or have corns, or the toothache ; and
I get drunk because I’m feeling well and grand. I got
drunk because I was rejected ; and I got awfully drunk
the night I was accepted. And, mind you, I don’t like
to get drunk at all, because I don’t enjoy it much,
and suffer hell afterwards. I’m always far better and
happier when I’m sober, and tea tastes better than
beer. But I get drunk. I get drunk when I feel
that I want a drink, and I get drunk when I
don’t. I get drunk because I had a row last night and
made a fool of myself and it worries me, and when things
are fixed up I get drunk to celebrate it. And, mind you,
I’ve got no craving for drink. I get drunk because I’m
frightened about things, and because I don’t care a damn.
Because I’m hard up and because I’m flush. And, some-
how, I seem to have better luck when I’m drunk. I don’t
think the mystery of drunkenness will ever be explained—
until all things are explained, and that will be never. A
friend says that we don’t drink to feel happier, but to
feel less miserable. But I don’t feel miserable when I’m
straight. Perhaps I’m not perfectly sober just now,
after all. I’ll go and get a drink, and write again later.

“ L.W.” : The Imperial House of Austria
has. been making copy for the evening papers
again, and various journalistic snobs will arise
and deplore the way the Hapsburgs are dragging
the monarchical honor in the dirt. All the same,
from a human standpoint, most, or at least many,
of the Austrian scandals may be counted as
righteousness to that ancient line. ■ When a scion
of the house of Austria falls in love with a woman
lie’s always gentleman enough to marry her law-
fully, it he can. Thrones have been cast aside for
the love of actresses by the heirs of Austria —and
very few other royal families have been capable of
such self-sacrifice. No Guelph is built that way.
So I take off my hat to the House of Austria !

“ Spinifex ” : Consider the “bloke” and
the challenge thereof. Offend him, and straight-
way he dares you to engage in mortal combat —by
deputy. The average challenge runs thus: * ‘ Think
yer a bit of a crack, doyer? Well, let me tell
yer. Igot a cobber wot’ll knock th’stuffin’outer
yez in two ’its; an’ don’t you fergit it, neither.”
This method of deputing a friend to knock stars
out of the offending party should denote some-
thing. Can’t decide yet whether it means a low
standard of courage, or an extremely high standard
of intelligence. Anyway, it’s a fool’s game for
“me cobber.”

Distraint.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
he little birds that build their nests
the branches green,
° cru el landlord them molests
With beak and talons keen.
They are not harried round for rent,
Ue SP°' IS their mirth,
heir roof it is the firmament,
Their garden is the earth.
In Gum tree town they dwell secure,
No bailiff comes to seize
Their little sticks of furniture
Ainid the waving trees.
When on their little quests they roam,
With rage they do not burn
To find some frowsy bird at home
When they at eve return.
I would I were a little bird,
The little birds among,
That I might sing, although unheard,
Of men a careless song.
If I unto the winds oould fling
These cares that make me pine—
No bird that flies in air would sing
A song as gay as mine.
I have a house with rooms so fine
Whose walls are painted green ;
An elegant and rich design
Is on the dado seen.
And in the front, in beauty rare,
The golden sunflowers glow,
And in the garden, free and fair,
The tall weeds gaily grow.
I would I were a joyous weed—
No lily white or rose— •
That I might bloom or run to seed
Exactly as I chose.
Alas, I am not bird or weed ;
The wolf is in my den—
I am a singer run to seed,
Yet called a citizen.
My furniture is sold. My care
By other men was bohght;
And so I sing Vogue la galore :
1 own the realm of Thought.
A gypsy I was born to be,
And still I Sigh for them—
The nomads in the desert free,
The shining tents of Shem.
N.S.W.
GREEVE ROE.
The Bagman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Noon on a midsummer Sunday in Queensland.
The man in a one-horse trap was the sole moving
object on a five-mile stretch of red road leading
into a border township. The whole turn-out was
red. The man, the trap, horse, harness; the
water bag slung to the guard rail; the luggage-
all were red with dust. A cloud of red dust
enveloped the whole, and steadily accompanied
its progress.
The scene gradually changed. The red road
was still there, but fenced in. Small paddocks
with actual grass were to be seen. The man did
not notice these things at first, but presently the
iron roof of,a homestead embedded in orange and
peach trees, with a fringe of timber in the back-
ground, attracted his attention.
“Peaches! Great Scott! I’d give half my
samples for a dozen ! ”
He pulled up at a neat white gate with “River-
view ” painted on it. A short distance off, the
branches of the peach trees waving seductively in
the breeze gave him invitation.
“Rather a bit of cheek,” he soliloquised, “to
call at a private house on Sunday. But I don’t
know. They’ll give me a drink, anyway, and
perhaps some peaches.”
He got down, opened the gate, and led the
horse through. “ I won’t mention biz,” he said to
himself, as he shut the gate. He drove towards
the house, his thought taking a brighter hue.
“Bush people are hospitable—about dinner-time—
comfortable-looking place—good dinner—drink—
bath—order—and go on refreshed.”
He reached a gate in the fence from which a path
led up to the house, and tied his‘ horse to the
horse-rail. He took two steps towards the house.
Then the bagman instinct became tod strong for
him, and he returned and extricated one small
sample-case from the vehicle.
He reached the house. From a back room lie
heard the welcome sound of cutlery on crockery.
His knock was answered by a stout, good-
tempered-looking man,- dressed in cool white
garments. He bore in his hand a long tumbler of
beer. The mist on the outside of the glass denoted
that it was also cool. At the sight the bagman
was roused to violent energy.
“My name is Brady,” he said ; “I represent
Sawkins and Smith, tea merchants, of London,
Adelaide, Melbourne, Sydney, and Brisbane.
Allow me to offer you my card'. I have called to
see if X can do any business with. you. These .”
“Friend,” broke in the other, over whose
face passed a look more of sorrow than of anger,
“dost thou not know that this is the Sabbath
day ? Is it not written in the Book, 1 Six days
shalt thou labor and do all that thou hast to do,
but the seventh is the Sabbath of the Lord thy
God ; in it thou shalt do no manner of work, thou
and thy maidservant, thy manservant, and thy
daughter, nor thy cattle, nor the oxen that
treadeth out the stranger within thy gates;”’ here
he looked at the hot, dusty individual before him
and drank half the beer; “for in six days the
Lord made heaven and hell, the sea, and all the
birds therein, and camped on the seventh day,
wherefore the Lord blessed the seventh day and
hallowed it.’” Here he finished the beer.
“I beg your pardon,” said Brady, with con-
centrated bitterness; “I did not know I was
trying to sell tea to a dashed conventicle,” and
he snatched up his case and turned towards the
gate.
“Friend,” said the stout man as Brady retired
down the path, “I hope what I have said will do
thee good. I have an office in town, there will I
see thee and do business with thee.” But Brady
did not wait to hear him out, and the dusty
caravan was again rapidly travelling towards the
township.
* * * *
Jim Brady, once smart, but now a wreck,
reached town in a dazed state. He engaged a
room, saw to his horse, and made a bee-line tor
the bar for his long-deferred cool drink. While
discussing this, and waiting for the bath to be
filled, he asked the landlord : >t
“Are people very religious hereabouts?
“ Well, I don’t know,” was the reply. “We ve
tried twice to keep a resident clergyman here, for
the sake of the children, and the children used to
attend service pretty regularly, _ while their
parents went fishing and shooting, but the
stipend never could be collected, and the parson
wouldn't stay without it. A priest and two
E arsons visit us now and again, but I can t
onestly say I think we’re very religious.”
• * * *
Twelve o’clock next morning saw Jim Brady,
spick and span, like the smart Jim of city fame,
returning to his pub. He looked dispirited, and
he was ; business, though he was ready to do it
wholesale or retail, had not been too good. As he
neared the hotel he noticed ‘‘Black, Jones and
Co., Auctioneers, Salesmen, etc.” painted on a
board across the footpath.
“May as well try,” he said.
He entered the office. Then he glared with
indignation, for seated at the table was his friend
of the previous day. He was clothed in garments
of respectability, and his face wore a severe rather
than the well-remembered sorrowful expression,
as he glanced at the incomer over a pair of gold-
rimmed pince-nez.
“ What can I do for you ? ” he inquired.
Jim made some introductory remarks, and
presented his card.
“ Tea, eh ? Very well, let me see your samples.”
Jim arranged them, and began to descant on the
virtues of the different blends. The customer
listened, asked various shrewd questions, and at
length took a pinch of a sample, tasted it, and
said : “ What can you do that at? ”
“ Ah ! ” said Jim, “that is a particularly choice
blend. I sell more of that than of any. Luckily
the firm has a large stock, and shipments to
arrive. I can do it you at 2s. for small lots, or
Is. lOd. for a large order. ”
The stout gentleman’s expression changed from
severe to ferocious. He jumped to his feet.
“ Two shillings ! D—n and your soul, you
infernal impudent monkey ! D’ye see those
chests, d—n you ? And those, and those, d—n
you ? Look at the brand ; same as yours, isn’t
it ? I’m selling that here at Is. lOd. Do you take
me for a blithering idiot, or are you a gory ass ? ”
and the stout man’s curses died away in a sub-
dued growl.
Then the bagman turned on him with a smooth
voice, but with fire in his eye.
“ I beg your pardon,” he said, “but are you the
stout, white-clothed snuffle-buster with the beer
in his hand that I intruded on yesterday, or his
twin brother ? Your countenance suggests that
inhospitable person, but your language doesn’t.”
The stout man yelled with laughter.
“Of course I’m the same man. 'l’m taken that
way occasionally on Sundays when I’m short of
amusement. ’
“ Quite so—and you’re taken this way sometimes
on Mondays. Now those chests are empty, and
your tea is the same sort of thing as your piety.
Just come to the hotel and do a drink, and if you
give me a big order I’ll do it at Is. and then
I’ll do a swearing match against you for £l, or
I’ll do a snuffling match for £5, or I 11 fight you
in the front street for Gd. But I’m going to sell
you some tea, to make up for that beer you didn’t
give me yesterday.”
And the stout man admitted it was a fair offer.
NE'ER-DO-WEEL.
What Befell the Artist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Very lonely was the City of Dull Noises.
In the moving desert of its streets, human sands,
caught by the siroccos of Chance, whirled around
and onward—ever around and for ever onward.
The ways were barren of flowers and the tall
houses hid the skies. No song of birds reminded
men of freedom and the fields, but the canary
trilled nervously in the gilded cage, a theme of
memories, like unto the yearnings of a blind
troubadour behind prison bars.
The Artist sat in his workroom, four storeys
high.
The tools of his trade were about him. His
palettes were grimed with the coarse exhalations
of impure 'atmospheres ; his stumps of crayon
greasy from the perspiration of fingers; even the
prepared Indian ink had dried up disgustedly in
its little French bottles.
The sickly light of a city sunbeam goaded the
Artist’s tired eyes until they closed.
He had taken passage to the Land of Just-
Beyond, where the love-work never tires a strong
man’s hand ; where the eagles gather on their
rocks above the clouds to gloat upon the sun.
Dream-fancies stood out in bas-relief on the
white marble of his brain.
He splashed clean colors on to virgin canvas.
He blended and he builded. He borrowed their
sheen from dainty sea-shells on Pacific beaches ;
he pilfered ( ,his scarlet from the flame-tree, his car-
mine from the waratah. He robbed the dawn of
its glory, and tied it to the sunset with a loop of
camel’s hair. He stole the glitter from the waters
and the moonlight shadows from the forest. He
paved his pictures with the sunlight and bridged
them with the Milky Way.
At large he wandered in wide domains where
all was glorious and new. Milkmaids, red-cheeked
and full-hipped, sang divinely beneath tall palms.
Brown-bearded cavaliers rode over sunny land-
scapes to the crack of stockwhips.
He listened to the rustle of the wind-swept
maize ; the ring of the wood-cutter’s axe upon
the mountain cedar.
He creaking of the saw in the saw-
pit, the crunch of the cane in the mill, the groan-
ing of riven logs under the splitter’s wedges and
maul.
He heard the bang of the shotgun, a splashing
of water, a whirring of wings.
He heard, he saw, he awoke ; and seizing
wearily his palette and brushes, he proceeded to
the painting of a pot-boiler, which brought him
exactly two pounds and two shillings of money at
the Annual Artistic Australian Exposure of Very
Little Pictures.
E. J. BRADY.
“ Platelayer ” : Why not a 1 or 2 per cent,
tax on gross receipts of turf and other sporting
clubs as something towards a Commonwealth
sinking fund? The tired Australian would thus,
in spite of himself, be doing something to support
■us leanins'-noot.. for there arc few Australians too
weary io attend a race-meeting.
“ Yic. ”: Why do all our artists depict Austra-
lia as a placid-expressioned young female, draped
in a smirk and about seven yards of double-width
calico ? My idea of a true portrayal of Australia
calls for a Man, not a woman; with an anvil-
chin, far-seeing, steady eyes, rope-muscled limbs,
and draped merely in a pair of pants, shirt,
bluchers, and slouch hat—the shirt sleeves, by
the way, rolled up.
“The employing secretary of Frederick
Eckstein (Wernher, Beit and Co.), a director of
several Rand mines, has introduced Italian miners
to the field.” Kaffirs, Hindu coolies, Chinese
coolies, cheap Italians—anybody, in fact, but the
Britisher, who will demand a voice in the govern-
ment of the country. Yet it was in the name of
political freedom that this same Britisher spilt a
sea of blood and about £250,000,000 of money.


RHEUMATISM
Is a form of Blood Poisoning. You will never have Rheu-
matism if your Kidneys are well. For Healthy Kidneys
filter from the Blood all the Rheumatic and other
Poisons that cause Disease. Keep the Kidneys well,
and they will keep you well.
Rheumatism is not a
disease in itself; it is but
the symptom of a disease.
It is caused by an acid in
the blood, called uric acid,
of which there is enough
formed every twenty-four
hours to cause certain death
if not removed.
Nature has provided the
kidneys to take this acid
out of the system, and they
are the only means by which
it can be removed. As the
blood passes through the
kidneys it is filtered, and
the acids and poisons are
passed out of the system
along with the surplus
water.
But when the kidneys
get sick they do not do
this work properly, and
some of this acid stops in
the system, causing endless
pain and suffering. It will
crystallise somewhere, and
when it crystallises in the
muscles, the little, sharp,
jagged crystals cut and tear
the flesh, causing great pain.

That is muscular rheumatism.
They may crystallise in the joints, and that is the cause of gout j or in

The acid will penetrate every nerve and fibre of the body, causing
nervousness, headache, sleeplessness, and interfering with the valves of the
heart; it will affect the eyes, and will cause a general weakness and fagged
feeling, bloating of limbs, &c.
The whole health of your body therefore depends on keeping your kidneys
well. There can be no rheumatism with healthy kidneys, no nervousness, no
headaches, no bad blood. Keep the kidneys well, so they may filter al'

writes: “ Whilst in South Africa with the 6th Imperial Bushmen, through
constant exposure to all sorts of weather, sleeping in wet clothes, etc., I got
a very severe attack of lumbago. I was so bad that I could not get up off the
ground—let alone mount my horse. Two military doctors had ago at me.
One blistered my back until all the skin was off; and the other gave me
medicine —but neither of them did me any good. Fortunately a comrade
eame along with a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and before I had
finished the box I was as right as ever I was in my life. Since then I always
keep a box of the pills handy, in case of the lumbago returning ; but, so far, I

'Possum.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A correspondent suggests: “In case the Common-
wealth should be needing a motto, what about ‘’Possum’?
‘ I am able,’ also the name of a critter pretty
familiar to us all.)”
“ Referring to general trade, I would point out that it
is the prosperity of other nations which makes them able
to buy Australian wool, and when they leave off buying
our produce w,e Australians will have to live on ourselves
like the cannibals of old.”—G. H. Reid at Singleton.
Thus does Puncheon Reid conclude,
Dealing in prophetic mood
With the taboo Borealian
’Gainst commodities Australian ;
And does wail that we good pals
Soon must needs turn cannibals.
Yet does young Australia muster
Grit to grin at all the bluster.
Thumbs in belt and tongue in cheek,
Only one word will he speak—
Good word, “ ’Possum ! ”
’Possum ! ’Possum ! I am able !
Count me not with things unstable.
Cobden creed may rot or shrivel,
Cobden screeds degrade to drivel.
Foreign nations take their chance,
Retrogression or advance.
We on nationhood upstanding,
Ever deepening and expanding,
Answer all the rant and railing
With the one word, all availing,
That word “’Possum!”
Puncheon Reid declares that John,
Hans and Jacques and Jonathan,
All in kindliest mood conspire
To create what we require.
We contrariwise declare
Their success breeds our despair.
Free us from that exploitation,
And right soon a stalwart nation
All our life shall bud and blossom ;
Each man standing on his “ ’Possum !”
Always “’Possum!”
Wheat to grind and wool to spin,
Mineral wealth untold to win,
Ample virgin gold to buy
All our land will not supply,
Dowered with all things mankind prizes,
What care we who ostracises ?
Who goes down or who stands up ?
Lady Nature crowned our cup.
And we’ll drink to her who made us,
And reply, whoe’er upbraid us—
“ ’Possum ! ’Possum !”
But another terror looms,
Opes another.book of dooms.
Lo ! Bull-Cohen, square and stern,
Will prepare to do his turn.
Stout Bull-Cohen vents a roar,
Swears that never, never more
His good gold our way shall come.
His big loans make our life hum.
Thus he swears with tongue and pen.
Young Australia, say what then?
“ Say ? Why, ’Possum.”
“ ’Possum ! ’Possum ! ” always ’Possum !
Take your loans to Abram’s bosom !
Loans and landlords, duns and debts,
Eat up all our cash assets.
Raw material, foreign friends,
Use us for their private ends.
Landlords keep us under whip,
Good Bull-Cohen makes us skip.
If the whole lot went to blazes
Could we tighten up our traces ?
Thunder ! “ ’Possum 1’
Never time so fair as now,
Now to make and clinch the vow;
No men work for foreign bosses,
(Fat men bulging on our losses).
But, from shearing, we’ll to spinning,
Keep at home the gold we’re winning.
And we’ll hold our land in fee
For our own posterity.
Can we rule it thus, good brother,
Thyself, myself, not another ?
“ Each man : ’Possum! ”
N.S.W.
F.M.
“The Kid.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Come back ’ere, Tommy, an’ take ther baby.”
Tommy clattered his barrow over the rough
bricks to the yard gate, but the shrill voice rose
again. “ D’y’ear, yer young varmint, yer take ther
baby.”
Tommy slowly pushed the barrow back to the
verandah, where an untidy woman stood with
a screaming baby under her soapy arm. He
flung his cap savagely at a familiar fowl, and
dumped the child down on a bundle of mangling
he was taking home. The screams instantly
ceased, and the thin, dirty little face brightened
as the barrow bumped down the yard and through
the lane. Tommy pushed it along, and surveyed
the occupant with angry eyes.
Ever since that baby had entered the world he
had monopolised Tommy, who could never sneak
out without him. If a fight was on he always
arrived when it was all over because he had the
baby. Many a tempting shot at a Chinaman had
to pass unaccomplished because he could not run
away fast enough with the baby.
And the baby, though a cross peevish little
mortal, never shed a tear when with Tommy.
That was what made him wild. If his mother
picked him up, he screamed and struggled until
she cried, “Ere, Tommy, fer Gawd’s sake take
this blessed kid.” With Tommy it was instantly
pacified, and he had no excuse to give the slap he
ached to deliver.
Never a tear, no matter how uncomfortable he
might feel. Sometimes pushed under one arm
while Tommy played marbles, or jogging madly
up and down while Tommy tore along after afire-
engine. To-day he had tried to slip out alone,
for he had a piece of lollie he did not intend
sharing. Slyly he turned away his head while
sucking it, but the quick eyes discovered him and
a grimy little hand was stretched out.
“ Yer greedy little wretch, yer shan’t have it.”
But the hand was stretched out further, never
yet knowing denial.
Tommy snapped a piece off with his teeth and
pushed it into the ready mouth.
“Take it, yer little pig. It’s enough ter have
yer tied to a feller’s neck widout wantin’ every-
thin’ ’e’s got. Take yer sticky paws off them there
clothes or ”
A group of boys quarrelling over their marbles
instantly took his attention.
Dropping the barrow shafts and threatening
the baby with instant death if he moved. Tommy
went over to take part in the noisy discussion.
Suddenly there was a scatter, as some made a
grab at the marbles and cleared. Tommy flew to
his barrow and rattled off, but he was seen and
judged with the rest. As he was the only one of
his party in sight he was set upon by the losers
and roughly handled- His own marbles were
seized as spoils, and he was left with a bruised
body and the baby.
He shook his fist at the child, spluttering with
tears and anger.
“By cripes, I’m jest full o’ yer. A feller might
as well have a lump o’ iron slung round him.
Yer young devil, I’ll flatten yer yet.”
The offender simply clapped his hands and
laughed, believing it was all done to amuse him.
Yet there were times when Tommy found the
baby paid. Certain housekeepers where he car-
ried the mangling praised him for thinking so
much of his little brother. A slice of bread-and-
jam usually followed.
As the days went on the child grew more irrit-
able, and, to do Tommy justice, he often took
him out to rescue him from a beating.
One day he received a rude shock. An unwary
display of pop-corn elicited no demand. He
offered some with a sudden burst of generosity.
But the little head turned away from it.
After a long pause, Tommy asked, “ What’s up
wiv yer ? ”
A neighbor passing heard the remark, and
asked, “ What’s up with ther kid, Tommy ? ”
“ ’E won’t eat nothin’, an’ ’e won’t sit up.”
“ Take him ’ome ; the sun’s making him sick.”
Tommy took him home, and, finding the house
deserted, sat him among some clothes on the
verandah. To his great surprise, the baby made
no demur as Tommy slipped over the fence to
freedom.
He made up for lost time during the next few
days. His mother, heavy with drink, never
troubled, as the baby seldom cried after him now.
He could walk deliberately out and slain the
gate in the baby’s face, and the baby would only
sit listlessly watching. One morning after doing
this he peeped through the fence. He certainly
was not sorry to be free, but somehow things
didn’t seem natural. He took an apple from his
pocket and went back to the baby The little
hand, usually so eager, never moved.
He went through the inventory of his pockets.
A few marbles —not those, he might swallow them.
A piece of string and a penknife —both dangerous.
Some nails, buttons, a new peg top. He held it
up temptingly. The little arms stretched out
and passed it; crept about his neck, and the
drooping head fell limply upon his shoulder.
Down the lane rattled the barrow, past a group
of boys.
“ I say, Tommy,” cried one, pointing up the
lane to an advancing Chinaman, ‘ ‘ we’re goin’ ter
lick Wun Lung. Come on an’ ’elp; yer ain’t got
the kid ter stop yer now.”
Tommy did not see the Chinaman just then, for
he stared through a blinding mist at a dirty little
bonnet in the empty barrow.
SIMON.
Great is the rivalry between Milparinka
and Tibooburra, in the dismal north-west of
N.S.W. The population of the latter is about
treble the former, and Tibooburra has three times
as many pubs.—Tibooburra three, Milparinka
one. In the sunburnt centre of parched Australia
the number of pubs, is the crucial test of a town-
ship’s greatness. Milparinka could afford to crow
in the days of Tibooburra’s infancy. At that
time there was gold at Milparinka and Mt.
Brown, and it then boasted three pubs. Now, it
is merely a collection of ruined humpies, where
the coach stops bound for south-west Queens*
land. The best building in the place is
the court-house, which the M.P. had to
agitate for, not because it was wanted, but as a
matter of form. It was chiefly used for ping-pong
when too hot or wet for the P.M. to glay golf.
But Milparinka’s dignity got the nastiest kick of
all recently—the official kick of the Government.
Its great argument in favor of its paramountcy
was the fact that the P.M. lived in Milparinka,
and visited Tibooburra. Now the situation is
reversed. It still has one mainstay. The Stock
Board meets two or three times a year. If ever
those meetings should cease, the melodious name
of Milparinka must be rubbed off our map. For-
bear, ye Stock Board!
“ T’othersider ” : Kalgoorlie (W.A.) has a
greater pressure of dog, live and tinned, to the
square inch than any other town in Australia.
Not only are the pavements littered with sleeping
mongrels and nondescript curs, but even the
private bars and hotel smoking-rooms are infested
with them. Also, some people there, of the im-
ported-dude kind, cultivate the brutes by the
gross as pets. I remember, with pleasure, how
two of these Johnnies were suddenly hurled,
along with their pups, two-thirds of the way
across Hannan-street. Each Johnny owned about
five terriers, and they had the habit of strolling
about the town with a pup under one arm,
trousers turned up with the Piccadilly reef, a
glass over the right eye, and the four-legged
pestilences trooping behind. In that order they
ambled, one day, into the saloon-bar of the Aus-
tralia Hotel, and, putting down their armfuls
of pup, the two called loudly for two—haw—long
beahs. While Fluffy was icing the drinks, the
ten terriers climbed up on the lounge and lay
there, a palpitating mass of confused dog. As
the dudes aimed their “beaks” at the frescoed
ceiling, a well-known Boulder mine-boss strode in
and smote the heap of bull-terrier a tremendous
kick that scattered it all over the bar. Both
Johnnies dropped their drinks and yelled, “You
man ! how dah you kick my terriahs—how dah
you ’ ” waving their crook-sticks excitedly as they
spoke. Then the boss of the Great Gold-grab
Company exploded, and, with a furious combined
fist-and-foot movement, whirled both dudes along
the corridor and forth into the street, casting
great festoons of yelping pup after them. Then
he wiped the perspiration off himself, and had a
number of drinks. He deserved them.

Thus an American paper : “ Why, of course it had to
come : we have been watching' the papers for something
about it for weeks. ‘ Draga of Servia,’ that’s the name
of it, is going to be put on in Chicago first, and it will
probably be about the mellowest melodrama there ever
was. If we don’t get a historical novel about that Ser-
vian episode we’ll be mighty lucky.” “ Karageorgevitch’s
Curse, or the Squelched Swineherd ” is submitted as a
title for the novel.
“ Magda ” : Sudermann’s latest play is the sad story of
an old man’s impotent republicanism. Scene laid in
Prussia, a few years after the Franco-German war, when
the Vaterland was full of military spirit and Gorsave the
Emperor. The hero, known to his provincial townsmen
as the Revolutionary Socrates, is the chairman of a
Treason Club. The local chief magistrate reports the
poor little club to the Government, whereupon all its
members, save Socrates, abandon the cause, and poor old
Soc. , who hopes to be cast into prison, is humiliated by
receiving a royal decoration as a “ suitable punishment
for a harmless law-breaker.” This is the first time a
dramatist has introduced a C.M.G. decoration as an
instrument of torture.
" Hamlet” the mummer : When I was a raw amateur,
a friend and I (his head was much swollen through
winning a 2d. competition) called on a third-rate actor-
manager for a job. “ Good day t Seeking engagements?
H’m ! What’s your line?” My conceited pal spoke in
an assumed deep voice. “ Well, I consider I; can lick
anyone in this tow T n. Can’t I?” he asked, turning to me.
I gave a half affirmative nod, and a smile. V You are
wanting experience. Only wish to go on while we are
here. Right. Come up ■ to-morrow and I’ll give yon
both a few lines.” My friend with the assumed voice and
swelled head spoke again, but was interrupted with'
"For God’s sake, young fellow, talk in your ordinary
voice.” That took him down a peg. He talked pretty
big to the minor members of the co., and they also cooled
him down by giving him wrong cues and slipping one or
two good speeches he had. At the end of the week the
manager came to me and said, “ Here’s 30s. for you
Say nothing to your friend Sparks.” Sparks came up to
him. “Can I have my salary, Mr. ?” “Salary,” he
replied; “ Salary ! You! Surely you don’t expect’any-
thing ! I understood you were <?nly coming on for
experience. And 3’ou demand a salary ! ■ Here ! out you
get, or I’ll call the door-keeper. You—you want a salary !
Why, you’re not worth sixpence! Scrape some of that
conceit off you. youngster, and you may do some good.”
That fellow had talent, and has since become a great
actor but only after he lost his conceit. And I well I
was so beastly inflated through getting the 30s. that I
never did any good afterwards.
“ S.T.S.” : But has the wet finger really anything to do
With the small-girl spitting habit (8., 17/12/3) ? I dis-
tinctly remember Thorne, playing with Mrs. Lewis
in pantomime in Melbourne Exhibition year (Grand
Flaneur’s year). He was a Bad Boy, and his veracity
came into doubt. He simply put one finger in his mouth
and held it up, remarking, “ See that ? ” Then he dried
it on his clothing, and held it up again. “ See that ?” he
" very well , then /” And the evidence was accepted
as conclusive. Now, Thorne was an English actor anneal-
ing to an Australian audience.
